8 min
Riba: When One Condition (Excess or Delay) Is Present Alone
Inter-madhhab dispute on riba conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prohibits delay (nasi'ah) in a sale when only genus (or only measure) is present, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
When only one riba condition exists, the semblance of excess prohibits delay, even if no real excess exists.

Riba in sales is traditionally defined by two conditions: excess (fadl) and delay (nasi'ah). The Hanafi school analyzes cases where only one of these conditions is present, leading to a nuanced ruling. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains that when genus or measure alone is shared with delay, the transaction is prohibited due to a semblance (shubhah) of riba, while the Shafi'i school restricts prohibition to cases where both conditions exist. Understanding this difference is crucial for determining the permissibility of credit sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage outlines three scenarios: (1) both excess and delay present, (2) both absent, (3) one present without the other. Focus on the third, which is the subject of dispute.
2
Analyze the Hanafi ruling for scenario three
When one condition is present (e.g., same genus but delay, or different genus with measure and delay), the Hanafi school rules that the delay is prohibited (haram) because the single condition creates a semblance of the full cause for riba.
The semblance (shubhah) is treated as a hindrance (mani') similar to the reality.
3
Understand the role of shubhah
The Hanafi reasoning is that each component of the full cause (genus and measure) carries a semblance of causality. When present alone, this semblance suffices to prohibit delay, even if no real excess exists.
The hadith 'When types differ, sell as you wish hand to hand' is cited to support that delay is prohibited when types are the same.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that only the combination of genus and measure/weight triggers riba. Therefore, genus alone (as in selling a Herawi cloth for another on credit) does not prohibit delay.
The disagreement revolves around whether shubhah is a valid basis for prohibition.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
Under Hanafi law, any credit sale of same-genus items (even if equal in measure) is prohibited. Under Shafi'i law, such a sale is permissible as long as the items are not measured or weighed, or if they are equal in measure and genus is not a factor.
This difference has real implications for everyday transactions like exchanging currency on credit.

Source Text

النسيئة أحد البدلين ،معدوم، وبيع المعدوم غير جائز، فصار هذا المعنى مُرجحاً لتلك الشبهة فلا يحلّ، وفي غير النَّسيئةِ لم يعتبر الشُّبهة لما قلنا أن الشبهة أدون من الحقيقة ، على أن الخبر المشهور وهو قوله : «إذا اختلف النوعان فبيعوا كيف شئتم بعد أن يكون يدا بيد يُؤيِّدُ ما قلنا. أما إذا وجدا معاً فحرم الفضلُ : كما إذا باع قفير بر بقفيزين منه ، فحرم النساء أيضاً ولو مع التساوي، كما إذا باع قفيز بر بقفيز منه، وأحدهما أو كلاهما نسيئة، وتحقيقه من سابقاً. ٢ . وأما إذا فقدَا حَلَّ الفضلُ والنَّساءُ : كما إذا باع ثوباً هَرَويَّاً بثوبين مرويين نسيئة، فهذا البيع جائز لعدم العلة ؛ فإنَّ عدم العلة وإن كان لا يوجب الحكم، لكن إذا اتحدت العلة لزم من عدمها العدم، لا بمعنى أنها تؤثر العدم، بل لا يثبت الوجود ؛ لعدم عليه فيبقى عدم الحكم وهو عدم الحرمة فيما نحن فيه على عدمه الأصلي، وإذا عدم سبب الحرمة، والأصل في البيع مطلقاً الإباحة إلا ما أخرجه الدليل كان الثابت الحل، كذا في «الفتح». . وإن وجد أحدهما دون الآخر كما إذا باع الحنطة بالشعير، أو باع ثوباً هروياً بهروي حل الفضل وحرم النساءُ ؛ لأنَّ اجتماع القدر والجنس حقيقة العلة، فيكون لأحدهما شبهة العلة، فيحرم بحقيقة العلة حقيقة الفضل، وهو بالقدر ؛ لأنه تفاضل حقيقة. ويحرم بشبهة العلّة شبهة الفضل ، وهو النساء ؛ لأنه يشبه الفضل وليس بتفاضل حقيقة إعمالاً للدليل بقدره، لا يقال : أحدهما جزء العلة، وبه لا يثبت الحكم، ولا شيء منه، فكيف يثبت بأحدهما حرمة النساء ؛ لأنا نقول : أحدهما علة تامة لهذا الحكم، وهو حرمة النساء، وإن كان بعض العلة في حق ربا الفضل حقيقةً، فلا يلزمُ المحذور، كذا في حاشية الدر المختار» (۲) للعلامة الطَّحْطَاوي نقلاً عن الحلبي الله. (۳) ((الهداية» (٣) : ٦٢). (٤) (الكفاية ( ٦ : ١٥٤ - ١٥٥). وعند الشَّافِعِي (٢) له الجنس بانفرادِهِ لا يُحَرِّمُ النَّسْ ) . لاتحادِ الجنس فيه، فافهم. [١]قوله : الجنس بانفراده ؛ قال في «النهاية» : ليس للتّخصيص فائدة ؛ فإنه كذلك الكيلُ والوزن، وحاصله : أن وجود أحدِ وصفي الربا لا يحرِّمُ النَّسَاء عنده ، فهو ينكر حرمة النساء. [٢] قوله : لا يُحرم النساء ؛ لأنَّ بالنقديَّةِ وعدمها لا يثبت إلا شبهة الفضل ، وحقيقة الفضل غير مانع فيه ؛ أي في الجنسين يجوز بيع الثوب الهروي الواحد بالاثنين ، فالشبهة أولى أن لا يكون مانعاً. ولنا : أنه مال الربا من وجه نظراً إلى القدر وحده ، كما في الحنطة مع الشعير، أو الجنس وحده كالثوب الهروي مع الثوب الهروي، والنقدية أوجبت فضلاً في المالية فتحقق شبهة الربا في محل صالح لعلة صالحة لها. والشبهة مانعة عن الجواز كالحقيقة حتى فسد البيع مجازفة ؛ لاحتمال الربا ؛ وهذا لأن كل حكم تعلّق بوصفين مؤثرين لا يتم نصاب العلة إلا بهما ، فلكل واحدٍ منهما شبهة العلية، فيثبت بشبهة العليَّةِ شبهه الفضل، كما يثبت بحقيقتها حقيقته، هذا ما لخصته. من «الهداية» (۳) و«الكفاية»(4) ، وغيرهما.
The delay [نسيئة] is one of the two countervalues, [but] it is non-existent, and selling what is non-existent is not permissible, so this meaning becomes preponderant over that semblance [شبهة], thus it is not lawful. And in non-delay [transactions] the semblance is not considered because we have said that the semblance is inferior to the reality. Moreover, the well-known report supports what we said: 'When the two types differ, sell however you wish provided it is hand to hand.' As for when both [excess and delay] are present together, then the excess [فضل] is prohibited, as when one sells a measure of wheat for two measures of it, and the delay [نساء] is also prohibited even if it is equal, as when one sells a measure of wheat for a measure of it with one or both delayed, and its verification is from earlier. As for when both are absent, then the excess and the delay are lawful, as when one sells a Herawi cloth for two Marwi cloths on credit, so this sale is permissible due to the absence of the cause [علة]; for the absence of the cause, although it does not necessitate the ruling, but if the cause is unified, its absence necessitates non-existence, not in the sense that it affects non-existence, but rather existence is not established due to its absence, so the absence of the ruling, which is the prohibition in our case, remains on its original non-existence, and when the cause of prohibition is absent, and the original principle in sale absolutely is permissibility except what the evidence excludes, then the established ruling is permissibility, as in 'al-Fath'. And when one of them is present without the other, as when one sells wheat for barley, or sells a Herawi cloth for a Herawi cloth, then the excess is lawful but the delay is prohibited; because the combination of quantity [قدر] and genus [جنس] is the reality of the cause, so each one [alone] has a semblance of the cause, so by the reality of the cause the reality of excess is prohibited, which is due to quantity because it is a real excess. And by the semblance of the cause the semblance of excess is prohibited, which is delay, because it resembles excess but is not a real excess, by applying the evidence to its full extent. One should not say: each one is a part of the cause, and by it the ruling is not established, nor by anything from it, so how is the prohibition of delay established by one of them? For we say: one of them is a complete cause for this ruling, which is the prohibition of delay, even if it is part of the cause in respect of the riba of excess really, so the difficulty does not arise, as in 'Hashiyat al-Durr al-Mukhtar' by al-Allama al-Tahtawi, quoting from al-Halabi. And according to al-Shafi'i, the genus alone does not prohibit delay due to the unity of genus in it, so understand. [1] His saying: the genus alone; it is said in 'al-Nihaya': there is no benefit in specification, for likewise [the only condition would be] measure and weight. Its summary: that the existence of one of the two attributes of riba does not prohibit delay according to him, so he denies the prohibition of delay. [2] His saying: it does not prohibit delay; because by monetary value [نقدية] and its absence only the semblance of excess is established, and the reality of excess is not a hindrance in it; i.e., in two genera it is permissible to sell one Herawi cloth for two, so the semblance is more entitled not to be a hindrance. And for us: it is property of riba in a certain respect, considering quantity alone, as in wheat with barley, or genus alone like Herawi cloth with Herawi cloth, and monetary value necessitates an excess in monetary value, so the semblance of riba is realized in a place suitable for a cause suitable for it. And the semblance is a hindrance to permissibility like the reality, such that a sale is invalid due to speculation because of the possibility of riba; and this is because every ruling attached to two effective attributes is not complete in the degree of cause except by both, so each one of them has a semblance of causality, so by the semblance of causality the semblance of excess is established, just as by its reality its reality is established. This is what I summarized from 'al-Hidayah' and 'al-Kifayah' and others.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rules of riba (usury/interest) from the Hanafi school, specifically examining the scenario where only one of the two riba-conditions—excess (فضل) or delay (نسيئة)—is present. The author builds upon the foundational principle that riba al-fadl (excess) and riba al-nasi'ah (delay) are each prohibited when both conditions (genus and quantity/measure) are present. However, when only one condition exists, a nuanced distinction emerges. The text first clarifies the general principle: the delay (نسيئة) is a deferred countervalue that is non-existent at the time of contract, and selling non-existent items is impermissible. This makes the semblance (شبهة) of riba preponderate, leading to prohibition of delay when genus alone (as in the case of wheat and barley) or quantity alone (as in same genus with different quantity on credit) is present. Conversely, in transactions without any delay (hand-to-hand), the semblance is not considered because it is inferior to reality. The well-known hadith from Ubada ibn al-Samit (gold for gold, etc.) is cited to support the permissibility when types differ and the transaction is hand-to-hand. The text then systematically explains three scenarios: (1) When both excess and delay are present (e.g., selling one measure of wheat for two measures on credit, or even selling equal measures on credit), both excess and delay are prohibited. (2) When both are absent (e.g., selling a Herawi cloth for two Marwi cloths on credit, which are different genera), the sale is permissible because the cause (unity of genus and quantity) is absent; the default permissibility of sales applies. (3) When only one condition is present, such as selling wheat for barley (different genus, same quantity? Actually different genus, so no excess by genus but delay is present) or selling a Herawi cloth for a Herawi cloth (same genus, but excess in quantity? No, here it's same genus, but selling one for two? Actually the text says 'as when one sells wheat for barley' which is different genus but both are measurable, so delay is present; or 'sells a Herawi cloth for a Herawi cloth' which is same genus, but here delay is present and excess might not be? The text says 'حل الفضل وحرم النساء' meaning the excess is lawful but delay is prohibited. In the first example (wheat for barley), there is no excess because different genera, but delay makes it prohibited. In the second (Herawi cloth for Herawi cloth), the cloths are the same genus, so if one sells one for two with delay, the excess is lawful? Actually the commentator clarifies that when quantity alone differs (same genus but different amounts, delayed) the excess (real) is prohibited? Wait: The text says 'لأن اجتماع القدر والجنس حقيقة العلة' so when both quantity and genus meet, it's the real cause for riba al-fadl. So when one of them is present, it is a semblance. But in the examples given: selling wheat for barley has different genus, so quantity may be same? Actually the text does not specify quantity; it says 'باع الحنطة بالشعير' which could be equal or unequal. But the ruling is that excess is lawful (since no genus unity, so no riba al-fadl), but delay is prohibited because of the semblance of cause (since both are measurable). Similarly, selling Herawi cloth for Herawi cloth (same genus, but here it seems they are selling one for two with delay? The earlier example with cloths: 'باع ثوباً هروياً بثوبين مرويين نسيئة' was permissible because different types. But then the second example is 'باع ثوباً هروياً بهروي' which means same genus, so if delayed, then the delay is prohibited because of similarity of genus, even if the quantities are equal? The text says 'حل الفضل' meaning there is no real excess because they are equal? Or if they are unequal, the excess would be prohibited? The commentator is arguing that the semblance of cause (whether from quantity alone or genus alone) suffices to prohibit delay, even if the real excess is not present. Later, the Hanafi position is contrasted with the Shafi'i: Shafi'i holds that genus alone does not prohibit delay, because only the combination of genus and measure/weight matters. The Hanafi response is that monetary value (نقدية) itself creates a semblance of excess, and that semblance is a valid hindrance (مانع) just like the reality. The passage concludes by summarizing from 'al-Hidayah' and 'al-Kifayah', emphasizing that each effective attribute (quantity, genus, monetary value) has a semblance of causality, and thus the semblance of excess (delay) is established.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubhah): A semblance or doubt arising from the presence of only one of the two attributes that constitute the full cause for riba, thereby creating a partial resemblance to the prohibited transaction.
نسيئة / نساء (nasi'ah / nisa'): A sale with deferred payment, where one or both countervalues are delayed. In this context, it refers to the prohibition of delay when combined with a semblance of riba.
فضل (fadl): Excess or inequality in quantity or value. In riba, it refers to the extra amount exchanged, as in riba al-fadl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, what is the ruling when one sells a Herawi cloth for another Herawi cloth on credit (with delay) but the quantities are equal?
A) The sale is permissible because there is no excess.
B) The sale is valid but makruh.
C) The delay is prohibited because of the unity of genus.
D) The sale is prohibited completely because it involves riba al-fadl.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the presence of either genus or measure alone with delay prohibits the transaction due to the semblance of riba, even without real excess.

Ownership of Game Found on Land
Ruling on ownership of found game

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership of birds' eggs, chicks, and game found on another's land, and identify the exception when the landowner has prepared the land.

Core Idea
Game and eggs belong to the taker, not the landowner, unless the landowner deliberately prepared the land.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary addresses a common question: if a bird hatches chicks or lays eggs on someone's land, or a deer breaks its leg there, who owns it? The Hanafi school rules that these belong to the person who takes them (the finder), not the landowner. However, there is an important exception when the landowner has taken preparatory steps to attract game.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
The text begins: 'If a bird hatches chicks or lays eggs in a land, or a deer breaks its leg therein, then it belongs to the taker.' Note that the ruling is that the finder owns the item.
The ruling is general: any game (sayd) found on land goes to the finder.
2
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The commentary explains: 'because game belongs to the one who takes it.' The reason is that eggs and chicks are considered game (sayd), and ownership is established by taking possession (qabd).
Eggs are considered the origin of game and are treated as game themselves.
3
Distinguish cases of causation
If a deer breaks its leg on its own (takassara), it belongs to the finder. If someone else breaks it, that person owns it. This shows that ownership follows the act of taking or causing the injury.
The word 'takassara' indicates self-breakage.
4
Identify the exception for prepared land
The text ends with an exception: 'if the landowner prepared his land for hunting or nesting (e.g., set up a place for chicks), then the finder does not own it; the landowner becomes the taker.'
Preparation (i'idad) changes the ruling: the landowner is considered to have constructive possession.
5
Apply the rule to new scenarios
Consider: a bird lays eggs in a farmer's field not prepared for nesting – the finder owns the eggs. But if the farmer built a nesting box, the farmer owns the eggs, even if someone else finds them first.
The key is whether the landowner intended to attract game and prepared accordingly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لو فرخ ) أو باض طير في أرض، أو تكسر ظبي) فيها ، فهو للآخذ) : أي لا يكون لصاحب الأرض ؛ لأن الصيد لمن أخذه ، والمراد بتكسر الطبي إنكسار رجله، وإنما قال : تَكَسَّر ؛ لأنَّه لو كَسَرَها أحدٌ يكون له لا للآخذ، وفي بعض الرواياتِ تَكنَّس (۳) : أي دَخَلَ في الكناس (۳) : وهو مأواه
[١] قوله : يردّ عليه...الخ ؛ ويمكن الجواب عنه بالمنع ، وهو أنه ليس شيء من تكاليف الشرع من هذا القبيل، فإنَّ الضرر فيها دنيوي، والنفع أخروي، ولا يجوز للعبد ترك النفع الأخروي ؛ لأنه حق الله .
بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإنَّ الضرر والنفع فيه دنيويان، ويجوز للعـبـد تـرك النفع الدنيوي، حتى لو تساهل به جاز، بل هو مما يعد من الصفات الحسنة، والفضائل المستحسنة، فقياس ما نحن فيه على تكاليف الشرع قياس مع الفارق فافهم. (۲) قوله : ولو فرخ ؛ قال في «الصراح»: فرخ جوزه ، فرخة مؤنث أفرخ أفراخ : فراخ ج أفراخ جوزه بیرون آدرون مرغ ، يقال : أفرخ الطائر وفرخ. انتهى. (۳) قوله : لأن...الخ ؛ يعنى لأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ صيد ، وإن لم يؤخذ بحيلة، فإنَّ كونه مأخوذاً بغير حيلة لا يخرجه عن كونه صيداً ، كصيد انكسر رجله في أرض أحد، والصيد يكون من أخذه ، والبيض في حكم الصيد ؛ لأنه أصل الصيد. ألا ترى أنه يجب الجزاء على المحرم بكسره، كما يجب بالاصطياد، وهذا إذا لم تكن أرضُهُ مهيأة لذلك، وإن كانت مهيأة للاصطياد، فهو لصاحب الأرض ؛ لأنّ الحكم لا يضاف إلى السبب الصالح إلا بالقصد، فمن حفر في أرضه بئراً ليسقط فيها، أو أعد مكاناً للفراخ. فلما أعدها لذلك لا يملكها الآخذ ، بل صاحب الأرض يصير بذلك قابضاً
And if a bird hatches (chicks) or lays eggs in a land, or a deer breaks (its leg) therein, then it belongs to the taker. ... (Commentary explains that the taker is the finder, not the landowner, because game belongs to the one who takes it. An exception is if the landowner prepared the land for hunting.)
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage discusses the ownership of birds' nests, eggs, and game that come to be on someone's land. The ruling is that these belong to the one who takes them (the finder), not to the landowner, because they are considered game (sayd). The deer breaking its leg is interpreted as breaking it on its own, not by someone else. If someone else broke it, the deer would belong to that person. Some narrations say 'takannasa' meaning entered its lair. The commentary then addresses an objection that likens this ruling to Sharia obligations (takalif) which involve worldly harm for otherworldly benefit. The response distinguishes: here both harm and benefit are worldly, and the owner may forgo worldly benefit, whereas Sharia obligations concern otherworldly benefit which cannot be waived. The text further explains that eggs are also considered game because they are the origin of game. However, if the landowner prepared his land for hunting or nesting (e.g., set up a place for chicks), then the finder does not own it; rather the landowner by that preparation becomes the 'taker' (qabid).

Key Terms
فرخ (farkh): To hatch chicks; also used for the chicks themselves.
باض (baada): To lay eggs.
تكسّر الظبي (takassara al-zabi): The deer breaks its leg (by itself).
صيد (sayd): Game; something that can be hunted.
كناس (kanas): The lair or dwelling place of a deer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A bird builds a nest and lays eggs in a tree on a landowner's property. The landowner never tended the tree or encouraged birds. Who owns the eggs according to Hanafi?
A) The landowner owns them because the tree is on his land.
B) The first person to take the eggs owns them.
C) The eggs are ownerless (mubah) and anyone can take them without ownership.
D) The bird owns them.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, game and eggs found on land belong to the finder, not the landowner, unless the landowner has prepared the land for hunting or nesting.

Condition of Hand-to-Hand Exchange in Sarf
Defines thaman and explains the requirement of qabd before iftiraq in sarf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of hand-to-hand exchange in sarf, identify its proofs, and explain the Hanafi position on whether it is a condition for validity or remaining validity.

Core Idea
In sarf, both parties must physically exchange the countervalues before parting, or the contract becomes invalid.

This lesson examines the critical condition of hand-to-hand exchange (qabd) in the contract of sarf. The Hanafi school requires immediate physical possession before the parties separate. We will explore the definition of price (thaman), the evidence from hadith, and the nuanced discussion on whether qabd is a condition for the contract's validity or its remaining validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? What is the hukm (fard/wajib/haram etc.)?
Here, the act is a sarf contract, and the condition is qabd before iftiraq.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, or Qiyas?
The dalil is the hadith of Ubadah ibn al-Samit and the report from Umar, plus consensus.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How exactly does this dalil prove the ruling? What is the legal link?
The explicit phrase 'yadan bi-yad' (hand-to-hand) indicates the necessity of immediate exchange before parting.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Does any school disagree? What is their proof?
Intra-Hanafi: some say qabd is a condition for validity (shart sihha), others say it is for remaining validity (shart baqa'). The more correct view is the latter.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Which position is relied upon and why?
The text states 'wa huwa al-ashahh' (that it is a condition for remaining valid) as the relied-upon view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرط فيه التقابض قبل الافتراق.
والمكيل والموزون يسمَّى عيناً بالعقد تارة، وديناً أخرى، فإن كان معيّناً في العقدِ كان مبيعاً، وإن لم يكن معيّناً وصحبه الباء، وقابله مبيع فهو ثمن. ونوع آخر من الثمن هو سلعة في الأصل : كالفلوس، فإن كانت رائجةً كانت ثمناً ، وإن كانت كاسدة كانت سلعة.
والثمن إذا أطلق يراد به الدراهم والدنانير، فإن كان كلا البدلين في البيع دنانير أو دراهم، فهو بيع الجنس بالجنس ، وإن كان أحدهما دنانير والآخر دراهم، فبيع الجنس بغير الجنس. ومن حكم الثمن أن لا يشترط وجوده في ملكِ العاقد عند العقد، ولا يبطل العقد بفوات تسليمه، ويصح الاستبدال به، وحكم المبيع بخلافه، والمراد بالثمن هاهنا ما خلق للثمنية، ومنه المصوغ ، فإنَّ بيعه صرف، سواء كان لمصوغ مثله أو بالنقد، ولكنه بسبب ما اتصل به من الصيغة لم يبق ثمناً صريحاً ؛ ولهذا يتعيَّن في العقد كما صرحوا به.
[١) قوله : وشرط فيه ؛ أي في الصرف سواء كان بالجنس أو بغيره التقابض : أي قبض العوضين قبل الافتراق بالأبدان ؛ لانعقاد الإجماع عليه، ولما روى الإمام مالك ه في «الموطأ» : عن عمر له أنه قال : لا تبيعوا الذهب بالذهب، إلا مثلاً بمثل، ولا تبيعوا الورق بالذهب أحدهما غائب والآخر ناجز وإن استنظرك أن يلج بيته ، فلا تنظره إلا يدا بيد ، هات وهات، إني أخشى عليكم الربا). ولما روى عبادة بن الصامت له أنه قال : «الذهب بالذهب، والفضة بالفضة»، إلى أن قال: «مثلاً بمثل، سواء بسواء، يداً بيد، فإذا اختلفت هذه الأصناف فبيعوا كيف شئتم إذا كان يداً بيد (۳) رواه مسلم، وأحمد، وغيرهما.
ولأنه لا بُدَّ من قبض أحدهما قبل الافتراق ؛ لئلا يكون افتراقاً عن دين بدين، فلا بُدَّ من قبض الآخر؛ لعدم الأولوية تحقيقاً للمساواة بينهما ؛ لأنَّ النقد خيرٌ من النسيئة، ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن يكون مما يتعيَّن بالتعيين ؛ كالمصوغ والتبر، أو لا يتعينان كالمضروب، أو يتعيَّن أحدهما دون الآخر؛ لإطلاق ما روينا.
ولأنه إن كان مما يتعيَّنُ بالتَّعيين فيه شبهة عدم التَّعيين ؛ لكونه من جنس الأثمان خلقة، كما صرَّحَ به العلامةُ الزَّيْلَعِي ) ، وغيره.
ثم قيل : التقابض شرط لصحة العقد في الصرف، وكان ينبغي أن يكون مقروناً به، إلا أنَّ حالة المجلس أقيمت مقامَ حالة العقدِ تيسيراً ، وقيل : شرط لبقاء العقد على الصحة، وهو الأصح. وفي فوائد القدوري»: المراد بالقبض هاهنا القبض باليد لا بالتخلية، وفي «المجتبى»: الافتراق في الصرف والسَّلَم يفارق الافتراق في الإيجاب والقبول وخيارِ المخيرة ؛ فإنَّ الإعراض بالقيام أو بالاشتغال بعمل آخر مفارقة ولا كذلك هاهنا. فإنَّ الافتراق بالأبدان هو المراد هاهنا دون المكان، حتى لو قاما فذهبا معاً، أو ناما، أو أغمي عليهما، أو طال قعودهما لا يبطل الصرف، وعن محمد ه : إن قاما أو أحدهما فهو فرقة ، وإن ناما جالسين فلا ، وعنه القعود الطويلُ فرقة دون اليسير، ذكره العلامة الشمني في كمال الدراية.
[١]قوله : وصح ؛ إن تقابضا في المجلس ؛ لأنَّ المستحق هو القبض قبل الافتراق دون التسوية ؛ لما مر من الحديث، فلا يضرُّهُ الجزاف وإن افترقا قبل قبضهما، أو قبل قبض أحدهما بطل ؛ لفوات الشرط وهو القبض. ولهذا لا يصح فيه شرط الخيار والأجل ؛ لأنَّ الخيار يمنع استحقاق القبض ما دام الخيارُ باقياً ؛ لأنَّ استحقاقة مبني على الملك، والخيار يمنعه، وبالأجل يفوتُ القبض المستحقُ بالعقدِ شرعاً، إلا إذا أسقط الخيار أو الأجل فيعود صحيحاً لزوال المفسد قبل تقرره
And a condition in it (sarf) is hand-to-hand exchange before parting. And measured and weighed items are called 'ayn by contract sometimes, and dayn other times. If specified in the contract, it is the sold item; if not specified but the contract was made with it, and its counterpart is the sold item, then it is the price. Another type of price is a commodity originally: like fulus (copper coins); if they are current they are a price, and if they are obsolete they are a commodity. And when the word 'price' is used absolutely, it refers to dirhams and dinars. If both countervalues in a sale are dinars or dirhams, it is a sale of the same genus; if one is dinars and the other dirhams, it is a sale of different genera. Among the rulings of the price is that its existence in the ownership of the contracting party at the time of contract is not required, and the contract is not invalidated by failure to deliver it, and substitution for it is valid. The ruling of the sold item is contrary to that. The meaning of price here is that which is created for being a price, including manufactured items (such as jewelry), for its sale is sarf, whether for a similar manufactured item or for cash, but due to the form (manufacturing) attached to it, it does not remain a pure price; hence it becomes specified in the contract as they have stated. [1] His saying: 'And a condition in it' meaning in sarf, whether with the same genus or other, is hand-to-hand exchange i.e., taking possession of both countervalues before parting by bodies, due to consensus upon it, and due to what Imam Malik narrated in al-Muwatta' from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'Do not sell gold for gold except like for like, and do not sell silver for gold with one absent and the other present, even if he asks you to wait until he enters his house, do not wait except hand to hand, take and give, for I fear riba for you.' And due to what Ubadah ibn al-Samit narrated that he said: 'Gold for gold, silver for silver...' until he said: 'like for like, equal for equal, hand to hand. So when these categories differ, then sell as you wish if it is hand to hand.' (Narrated by Muslim, Ahmad, and others). And because it is necessary to take possession of one before parting, lest it be a parting over debt for debt; so it is necessary to take possession of the other due to lack of preference, to achieve equality between them, because cash is better than credit. And there is no difference in that between that which becomes specified by specification (like jewelry and bullion) and that which does not become specified (like coined money), or one becomes specified and the other not, due to the generality of what we narrated. And because if it is something that becomes specified by specification, there is an element of non-specification in it, because it is of the genus of prices by nature, as al-Zayla'i and others stated. Then it was said: Hand-to-hand exchange is a condition for the validity of the contract in sarf, and it should have been simultaneous with it, but the duration of the majlis was made to stand in place of the state of the contract for facilitation. And it was said: it is a condition for the contract remaining valid, and that is the more correct. In Fawa'id al-Quduri: what is meant by qabd here is qabd by hand, not by vacating (takhliya). In al-Mujtaba: parting in sarf and salam differs from parting in offer and acceptance and the option of the khayar al-mukhayyara; for indeed, turning away by standing or engaging in another act is parting, but not so here. Indeed, parting by bodies is what is meant here, not parting from the place; so even if they stand and go together, or sleep, or become unconscious, or their sitting is prolonged, the sarf is not invalidated. But from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): If they stand, or one of them, it is parting; if they sleep while sitting, no. And from him: prolonged sitting is parting, not brief. This was mentioned by al-Shumnni in Kamal al-Diraya. [1] His saying: 'And it is valid' means: if they take possession in the majlis; because what is required is taking possession before parting, not equality; due to the hadith mentioned, so juzaf (selling without exact measure) does not harm it. If they part before both take possession, or before one of them takes possession, it is invalid due to the loss of the condition, which is taking possession. Hence, stipulating an option or a term is not valid in it, because the option prevents the right to take possession as long as the option remains; because that right is based on ownership, and the option prevents it; and with a term, the required taking possession by contract is lost legally. Except if the option or term is dropped, then it becomes valid because the corrupting factor was removed before it was established.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding the condition of hand-to-hand exchange (qabd) in the contract of sarf (exchange of gold, silver, and currency). The author begins by defining key terms: thaman (price) and mabi' (sold item). He explains that a price can be a commodity like fulus if current, or stated as dirhams/dinars. Manufactured gold/silver items (masugh) are sold through sarf due to their intrinsic nature as prices. The core ruling is that in sarf, both parties must take physical possession of the countervalues (qabd) before they separate from the meeting place (iftiraq). This is proven by consensus, the narration of Umar (ra) and the hadith of Ubadah ibn al-Samit recorded in Muslim. The rationale is that cash is better than credit, and to avoid partings over debt-for-debt. There is a subtle intra-Hanafi discussion: some say qabd is a condition for the contract's validity (shart sihha) and should occur at the contract, but the majlis (session) is considered as its extension. Others, considered more correct, say it is a condition for the contract's remaining valid. Qabd here is by hand, not by vacating (takhliya). Parting is defined by physical movement (by bodies), not just leaving the place. Muhammad's riwaya differentiates between standing and sleeping. The passage ends with a discussion on the validity of selling gold for silver with excess (fadl) and without exact measure (juzaf): it is valid if hand-to-hand occurs in the majlis, but becomes invalid if they part before qabd. Stipulating an option or term invalidates the contract because it delays the required qabd, but removing such stipulations reinstates validity.

Key Terms
الصرف (al-sarf): A contract for the exchange of gold, silver, or currency, where hand-to-hand exchange is required.
التقابض (al-taqabud): Both parties physically taking possession of the countervalues before leaving the meeting place.
الافتراق (al-iftiraq): Parting by bodies, meaning physical movement away from the immediate meeting place, not just leaving the spot.
الجنس (al-jins): Genus of the countervalue; same genus (gold with gold) or different genus (gold with silver).
الجزاف (al-juzaf): Selling without exact measure, weight, or count.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sarf contract (exchange of gold for silver), what is the consequence if the two parties stipulate a one-day term for delivery?
A) The contract is valid because the term is permissible in deferred sales.
B) The contract becomes invalid because the term prevents the required immediate hand-to-hand exchange.
C) The contract is valid if they still exchange hands before parting.
D) The contract becomes invalid only if they part before the term expires.

Key Takeaway
In sarf, hand-to-hand exchange before parting is an essential condition; without it the contract is invalid, and stipulating a term or option also invalidates it.

Definition and Parties of Kafalah
Defining kafalah and its parties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of kafalah, identify its essential parties, and determine the capacity requirement for a valid guarantor.

Core Idea
Kafalah joins the guarantor's liability to the debtor's liability in a claim, requiring capacity to donate.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya defines kafalah (suretyship) and identifies its key parties. Understanding this foundation is essential because it sets the conditions under which a guarantee is valid in Hanafi law, particularly the capacity of the guarantor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition
The passage opens with the definition: 'ضم ذمة إلى ذمة' (joining a liability to a liability). This means the guarantor adds his own liability to that of the principal debtor.
The definition focuses on the claim, not just the debt.
2
Note the scope of kafalah
Kafalah is valid for hadd penalties, qisas, and debts in sound form. It is not valid for the ransom of a mukatab.
This shows kafalah covers criminal and civil liabilities.
3
Determine the capacity condition
The guarantor must be capable of donating (free, adult, sane). Kafalah from a slave, minor, or insane person is invalid, but a slave can be pursued after emancipation.
Capacity is a condition for validity, not a pillar.
4
Identify the parties
The claimant is the one guaranteed for (makful lahu), the defendant is the one guaranteed against (makful 'anhu), also called the principal (asil). The object (makful bihi) is either the person (in kafalah bi'l-nafs) or the property (in kafalah bi'l-mal).
In kafalah bi'l-nafs, the one guaranteed against and the object are the same.
5
Distinguish types of kafalah
Kafalah bi'l-nafs involves guaranteeing the person's presence; kafalah bi'l-mal involves guaranteeing payment of a debt or property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هي ضَمَّ ذِمَّةٍ إلى ذِمَّةٍ. تصح الكفالة بالحدودِ والقصاص، كما سيأتي ذكره إن شاء الله تعالى، وفي الدين كور صحيحاً، حتى لا تجوز الكفالة ببدل الكتابةِ كما سيجيء تحقيقه. وأهلها من هو أهل للتبرع، فلا تصح من العبد والصبي والمجنون، لكن العبد يطالب بعد العتق، كما صرحوا به، والمدعي: مكفول له؛ لأنَّ فائدة الكفالة ترجع إليه، والمدعى عليه مكفول عنه، ويسمى بالأصيل أيضاً، والنَّفس في الكفالة بالنفس، والمالُ في الكفالة بالمالِ مكفول به، ففي الكفالة بالنفس المكفول عنه وبه واحد، ومن لزمته المطالبة يسمى كفيلاً.
It is the joining of a liability to a liability. Kafalah is valid for hadd penalties and qisas, as will be mentioned if Allah wills, and for debt in sound form, such that kafalah for the ransom of a mukatab is not permissible as its verification will come. And its [validity] condition is that [the guarantor] be capable of donating, so it is not valid from a slave, a minor, or an insane person, but the slave is pursued after emancipation, as they explicitly stated. The claimant is the one guaranteed for (makful lahu) because the benefit of kafalah returns to him; the defendant is the one guaranteed against (makful 'anhu), also called the principal (asil). The person in kafalah bi'l-nafs and the property in kafalah bi'l-mal is the guaranteed object (makful bihi). In kafalah bi'l-nafs, the one guaranteed against and the object are the same. Whoever becomes subject to the claim is called the guarantor (kafil).
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage defines kafalah and identifies its essential parties. It begins by defining kafalah literally as 'the joining of a liability to a liability' (ضم ذمة إلى ذمة), indicating that the guarantor (kafil) adds his own liability to that of the principal debtor (asil). The definition is legal, not linguistic. The passage then states that kafalah is valid for hadd penalties and qisas, and for debts in a sound form, but not for the ransom of a mukatab, as will be explained later. The key condition for a valid guarantor is that he be capable of donating (أهل للتبرع), meaning he must be free, adult, and sane. Thus, kafalah is invalid from a slave, a minor, or an insane person. However, if a slave gives kafalah and then becomes free, he can be pursued after emancipation. The passage then identifies the parties: the claimant is the one guaranteed for (makful lahu), the defendant is the one guaranteed against (makful 'anhu), also called the principal (asil). In kafalah bi'l-nafs, the person is both the one guaranteed against and the object, while in kafalah bi'l-mal, the property is the object. The person who becomes subject to the claim is the guarantor (kafil).

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): The joining of one liability to another in a claim (ضَمُّ ذمَّةٍ إلى ذمَّةٍ في المطالبة).
ضم ذمة إلى ذمة (ḍamm dhimma ilā dhimma): Adding the guarantor's liability to the principal's liability so both are liable for the same claim.
أهل للتبرع (ahl li'l-tabarru'): One who is legally capable of donating, i.e., free, adult, sane, and not under interdiction.
مكفول له (makfūl lahu): The creditor or claimant who benefits from the kafalah.
مكفول عنه (makfūl 'anhu): The principal debtor whose liability is joined by the guarantor.
مكفول به (makfūl bihi): The object of kafalah, which is either the person (in kafalah bi'l-nafs) or the property (in kafalah bi'l-mal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for a person to be a valid guarantor in Hanafi law?
A) He must be the debtor himself.
B) He must be capable of donating (free, adult, sane).
C) He must be a male relative of the debtor.
D) He must be the creditor.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah joins the guarantor's liability to the debtor's liability, requiring the guarantor to be capable of donating.

Kafalah Definition: Dispute on the Scope of Liability
Resolving an intra-Hanafi dispute on the correct definition of kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi definition of kafalah and explain why it is phrased as 'in the claim' rather than 'in the debt'.

Core Idea
Kafalah joins liability in the claim, not the debt, to cover both property and personal guarantees.

Kafalah (suretyship) is a contract where a guarantor joins the principal in liability. But what exactly is joined? The claim (mutalaba) or the debt (dayn)? This intra-Hanafi dispute is a model of conceptual refinement: the correct definition must be broad enough to encompass both financial guarantees (kafalah bi'l-mal) and personal guarantees (kafalah bi'l-nafs). Understanding this distinction sharpens your grasp of Hanafi legal definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
One view defines kafalah as joining liability in the claim (mutalaba); another defines it in the debt (dayn). Note that both are Hanafi views—an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
2
Examine the proof for 'in the debt'
Proponents argue that the claim depends on the debt: if no debt exists, no claim can be made. Thus, the joining should be in the debt.
This reasoning appears logical but is tested by personal guarantees.
3
Analyze the counter-argument for 'in the claim'
The relied-upon view counters that the term 'claim' (mutalaba) is broader—it includes both demanding property (debt) and demanding the person's presence. In kafalah bi'l-nafs, there is no debt, yet a valid claim exists. Therefore, 'claim' is the correct locus.
Al-Marghinani and others prefer this view.
4
Recognize the Miskin variation
Miskin (in Sharh al-Kanz) argued that in personal guarantees, both the principal and guarantor are demanded for the same thing: the surrender of the person. This further shows that 'claim' unifies the demand, irrespective of the subject matter.
5
Conclude the tarjih
The correct definition (al-asahh) is 'joining liability in the claim' because it covers all types of kafalah explicitly and is not limited to debts. The difference is conceptual; practical rulings remain unchanged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المطالبة لا في الدين، هو الأصح). وعند البعض ضَمُّ الذِّمَّةِ إِلى الذِّمَّةِ في الدين؛ لأنه لو لم يثبتِ الدِّينُ لم يثبت المطالبة، والأصح هو الأوَّلُ. أصلُهُ أنَّ الكفيل والمكفولُ عنه صارا مطلوبين للمكفول له، سواء كان المطلوب من أحدهما هو المطلوب من الآخر، كما في الكفالة بالمال أو لا، كما في الكفالة بالنفس، فإنَّ المطلوب من الأصيل المال، ومن الكفيل إحضار النفس، ولفظ المطالبة بإطلاقه ينتظمهما، هذا على رأي بعضهم. وجزم مسكين في «شرح الكنز» بأنَّ المطلوب منهما واحد، وهو تسليم النفس، فإنَّ المطلوب عليه تسليم نفسه، والكفيل قد التزمه. وإنما اخترت تعريفاً صحيحاً متناولاً بجميع الأقسام صريحاً.
In the claim, not in the debt — that is the correct [view]. And according to some, it is the joining of a liability to a liability in the debt, because if the debt is not established, the claim is not established. The correct is the first. Its origin is that the guarantor and the principal become both demanded by the creditor, whether what is demanded from one is the same as what is demanded from the other, as in kafalah for property, or not, as in kafalah for the person, for what is demanded from the principal is property, and from the guarantor is the production of the person. The term 'claim' (mutalaba) generally encompasses both — this is according to some. And Miskin stated in Sharh al-Kanz that what is demanded from both is one thing, which is the surrender of the person, for the principal is demanded to surrender himself, and the guarantor has undertaken it. And I have chosen a correct definition that explicitly covers all types.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the precise definition of kafalah. The relied-upon position (al-asahh) is that kafalah is 'joining of a liability to a liability in the claim' (ضم الذمة إلى الذمة في المطالبة). Some scholars, however, define it as 'in the debt' (في الدين), arguing that the claim depends on the debt. The correct view is the first because kafalah can apply to claims for the person (kafalah bi'l-nafs) where there is no debt, only an obligation to produce the person. The text explains that the guarantor and the principal both become subject to the claim (mutalaba) — the principal for the debt (in kafalah bi'l-mal) or surrender of himself (in kafalah bi'l-nafs), and the guarantor for the debt or for producing the principal. The term 'claim' (mutalaba) encompasses both. Miskin in Sharh al-Kanz argues that what is demanded from both is actually one thing: in kafalah bi'l-nafs, both are demanded to surrender the person. Ultimately, the chosen definition is one that explicitly covers all types of kafalah, both for property and for the person. This dispute does not affect the substantive rulings but clarifies the conceptual basis.

Key Terms
المطالبة (al-muṭālabah): The claim or demand by the creditor against the debtor and the guarantor.
الأصيل (al-aṣīl): The principal debtor (المكفول عنه) in a kafalah contract.
الكفالة (al-kafālah): Suretyship or guarantee; a contract where the guarantor joins the principal in liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, kafalah is defined as joining of liability in the:
A) Debt (الدين).
B) Claim (المطالبة).
C) Person (النفس).
D) Property (المال).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines kafalah as joining liability in the claim (mutalaba) to encompass both financial and personal guarantees.

8 min
Suitable Conditions in Kafala
Explains validity of kafala with suitable conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a condition as 'suitable' (mulā'im) for kafala and distinguish it from unsuitable conditions.

Core Idea
A condition in kafala is valid only if it is a cause for the debt obligation or facilitates recovery.

In Hanafi law, kafala (suretyship) may be made conditional, but not all conditions are permissible. This lesson unpacks what makes a condition 'suitable' (mulā'im) – directly related to the cause of the debt or to ease of payment. Using examples from the text, we will learn to differentiate valid from invalid conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Find the conditional phrase in the kafala contract. For example, 'if you do not buy from so-and-so' or 'if the sold item is claimed'.
The condition is introduced with particles like 'ما' (if) or 'إذا' (when).
2
Determine the nature of the condition
Is the condition a direct cause for the debt (e.g., a sale that may be rescinded) or something unrelated (e.g., 'if the wind blows')?
Suitable conditions are those that are 'appropriate' (mulā'im) – linked to the obligation or to easy recovery.
3
Apply the ruling
If the condition is suitable, the kafala is valid and the guarantee is suspended until the condition occurs. If unsuitable, the condition is void but the kafala remains valid and the debt becomes due immediately.
This is based on the Hanafi position as explained in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو علق الكفالة بشرط ،ملائم، نحو: ما بايعت فلاناً ، أو ما ذاب لك عليه، أو ما غصبك فعلي ما ذابَ أي ما وَجَبَ، ففي هذه الصورة: ما ؛ شرطيّة معناه إن بايعت فلاناً، فيكون في معنى التَّعليق، وعنى بالملائم: المناسب، فإنَّ هذه الأشياء أسباب لوجوب المال، فيناسب ضمَّ الدَّمَةِ إِلى الدَّمة. فقولُه : ما بايعت فلاناً : أي ما بايعت منه أو ضمان المبيع إن لحقه آفة، فالمكفولُ به مجهول ؛ لاحتمال استحقاق الكل أو البعض، فيضمن الكفيل الكل والبعض. وفي «السراج الوهاج»: إذا استحق المبيع كان للمشتري أن يخاصم البائع أولاً ، فإذا ثبت عليه استحقاق المبيع كان له أن يأخذ الثمن من أيهما شاء، وليس له أن يخاصم الكفيل أولاً في ظاهر الرواية ، وعن أبي يوسف له أنَّ له ذلك، وأجمعوا على أنَّ المبيع لو ظهر حُراً كان له أن يخاصم أيهما شاء. [١]قوله : بشرط ملائم ؛ وقد أطلق القُدُوري في «المختصر»(۲) بأنه يجوز تعليق الكفالة بالشروط، ولكن صرَّحَ في «شرحه» بأن المراد شرط يكون لوجوب الحق، كقوله : إذا استحق المبيعُ ، أو لإمكان الاستيفاء، وسهولة الأداء، كقوله : إذا قدم زيد وكان زيد مكفولاً عنه أو مضاربه، ولعل المراد بالملائم هذا. وقال بعضهم: إن تعليق الكفالة يجوز بسبب وجوب الحق وبغيره لا يجوز، كذا «القنية)، وغيرها. [٢] قوله : فلاناً ؛ قيَّد بقوله : فلاناً ؛ ليصير المكفول عنه معلوماً، فإنّ جهالة المكفول عنه تمنع صحة الكفالة بجهالة المكفول له في الإضافة، وقد مرَّ ذكره سابقاً، فلو قال : ما بايعت من النَّاسِ فعليَّ ضمانُهُ فهو باطل ؛ لتفاحش جهالة المكفول عنه وبه ، بخلاف إنفراد جهالة المكفول به ، فإنها حينئذ قليلة يتحمل. (۱) قيد : بفلان ؛ إشارة إلى أن المكفول عنه يجب أن يكون معلوماً ؛ لأن جهالته تمنع صحة الكفالة. ينظر: «فتح باب العناية(٢ : ٤٩٩). (۲) (مختصر القدوري (ص٥٦). (۳) «قنية المنية»(ق۲۳۹/أ).
Or if he makes the kafala conditional upon a suitable condition, such as: 'if you do not buy from so-and-so', or 'what is owed to you', or 'what he wrongfully took from you is my responsibility'. 'ما ذابَ' means 'what is owed'. In this form, 'ما' is a conditional particle meaning 'if you buy from so-and-so', so it is in the meaning of a condition. By 'suitable' he means 'appropriate', for these things are causes for the obligation of wealth, so it is appropriate to join one guarantee to another. His statement: 'ما بايعت فلاناً' means 'what you bought from him' or the guarantee of the sold item if a defect befalls, so the object of kafala is unknown due to the possibility of entitlement to all or part, so the guarantor guarantees all and part. In 'al-Siraj al-Wahaj': If the sold item is claimed, the buyer may sue the seller first; if the claim is proven against him, he may take the price from whichever of them he wishes. He may not sue the guarantor first in the apparent narration, and from Abu Yusuf that he has that right, and they agreed that if the sold item turns out to be a free person, he may sue whichever he wishes. [1] His statement: 'with a suitable condition'. Al-Quduri in 'al-Mukhtasar' stated that it is permissible to suspend kafala upon conditions, but in his commentary he specified that the intended condition is one for the obligation of the right, like saying 'if the sold item is claimed', or for the possibility of recovery and ease of payment, like saying 'if Zayd arrives' and Zayd is the guaranteed person or his agent. Perhaps 'suitable' means this. Some said: suspending kafala due to a cause of obligation is permissible, otherwise not, as in 'al-Qunyah' and others. [2] His statement: 'so-and-so'; he restricted by saying 'so-and-so' to make the guaranteed person known, for ignorance of the guaranteed person prevents the validity of kafala along with ignorance of the beneficiary in the connection, and it has been mentioned earlier. So if he said 'I guarantee for whoever I buy from among the people', it is invalid due to extreme ignorance of the guaranteed person and the object, unlike the case of ignorance of the object alone, for that is then minor and can be tolerated. (1) He restricted with 'by so-and-so' indicating that the guaranteed person must be known because ignorance of him prevents the validity of kafala. See: 'Fath Bab al-Inayah' (2:499). (2) 'Mukhtasar al-Quduri' (p.56). (3) 'Quniyat al-Munyah' (q 239/a).
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the text of al-Wiqayah, explaining the ruling on conditional kafala. The main text states that kafala may be made conditional upon a 'suitable' condition (شرط ملائم). The commentator clarifies that suitable conditions are those directly related to the obligation of the debt, such as a condition that the sold item is reclaimed, or that a person arrives who is the guarantor. The examples given by the author include 'if you do not buy from so-and-so' (ما بايعت فلاناً) which is a conditional sentence where 'ما' is a particle meaning 'if'. The suitability lies in the fact that these events are causes for the debt to become due, so it is logically consistent to link the guarantee to them. The passage also addresses the issue of ignorance (جهالة) of the guaranteed person or the object. If the guaranteed person is unknown, the kafala is invalid. However, if only the object of kafala is unknown but the person is known, it is permissible because the ignorance is minor and can be tolerated. Finally, the passage quotes 'al-Siraj al-Wahaj' which discusses a dispute: in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), the buyer may only sue the seller first if the sold item is reclaimed; he cannot sue the guarantor first. But Abu Yusuf allows suing the guarantor first. They agree that if the item turns out to be a free person, the buyer may sue either. This is an intra-Hanafi disagreement on procedural priorities.

Key Terms
ملائم (mulā'im): In this context, it means a condition that is appropriate or suitable, i.e., directly related to the cause of the debt obligation.
كفالة (kafāla): A contract of suretyship or guarantee, binding the guarantor to fulfill an obligation if the principal fails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a suitable condition in kafala according to the Hanafi school?
A) 'If you visit me' (unrelated to debt)
B) 'If the sold item is reclaimed' (cause of obligation)
C) 'If the sun rises' (unrelated)
D) 'If you pay me a dirham' (already a term of the contract)

Key Takeaway
A conditional kafala is valid only if the condition is mulā'im (suitable) – directly related to the cause of the debt or to its recovery.

6 min
Procedural Priority in Kafala: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on procedural priority in kafala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi positions on whether the buyer can sue the guarantor first when the sold item is claimed.

Core Idea
In a claimed sale, the apparent narration forbids suing the guarantor first; Abu Yusuf allows it.

When a sold item is claimed by a third party, the buyer may seek recourse from the seller or the guarantor. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on the procedural order: must the buyer sue the seller first, or can he directly sue the guarantor? This lesson examines the evidence and practical consequence of this ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A buyer purchases an item, and a third party claims ownership. There is also a guarantor who guaranteed the seller's obligation (e.g., to return the price if the item is claimed).
The guarantor's liability is conditional on the claim being proven.
2
State the apparent narration position
According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), the buyer must first sue the seller. Only after the seller's liability is established can he sue the guarantor.
This is the majority position in the school.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that the buyer may choose to sue either the seller or the guarantor directly, without first suing the seller.
This is a minority position within the Hanafi school.
4
Note the agreed exception
Both sides agree that if the sold item turns out to be a free person (i.e., the sale is void because a free person cannot be sold), the buyer may sue either party immediately.
This is because the seller's liability is clear and the guarantor's liability is equally clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), can the buyer sue the guarantor first when the sold item is claimed?
A) Yes, always.
B) No, never.
C) Only if the seller is absent.
D) Only if the item is a free person.

Key Takeaway
The apparent narration prohibits suing the guarantor first; Abu Yusuf allows it, but the mufta bihi follows the apparent narration.

5 min
The Effect of Ignorance (Jahalah) on the Validity of Kafala
Distinguishes the effect of ignorance of the person vs. object in kafala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ignorance of the guaranteed person (invalidating) and ignorance of the object (tolerable) in a kafala contract.

Core Idea
Ignorance of who is being guaranteed invalidates kafala; ignorance of the amount is minor and tolerable.

A valid kafala requires clarity about the guaranteed person. If the guarantor does not know who he is guaranteeing, the contract is void. However, if the exact amount of the debt is unknown but the person is known, the kafala is still valid. This lesson explains the rationale and gives examples from the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of ignorance
Is the ignorance about the guaranteed person (e.g., 'whoever I buy from') or about the object of the guarantee (e.g., 'whatever is owed')?
The passage distinguishes between these two types.
2
Apply the rule for ignorance of the person
If the guaranteed person is unknown (e.g., 'I guarantee whoever I buy from among the people'), the kafala is completely invalid (batil) because of extreme ignorance.
The reason: the identity of the guaranteed person is essential; without it, the contract is too uncertain.
3
Apply the rule for ignorance of the object
If the guaranteed person is known but the exact amount is unknown (e.g., 'I guarantee whatever is owed to you'), the kafala is valid because the ignorance is minor and can be tolerated.
The passage says: 'بخلاف انفراد جهالة المكفول به فإنها حينئذ قليلة يتحمل' (unlike the case of ignorance of the object alone, for that is then minor and can be tolerated).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو علق الكفالة بشرط ،ملائم، نحو: ما بايعت فلاناً ، أو ما ذاب لك عليه، أو ما غصبك فعلي ما ذابَ أي ما وَجَبَ، ففي هذه الصورة: ما ؛ شرطيّة معناه إن بايعت فلاناً، فيكون في معنى التَّعليق، وعنى بالملائم: المناسب، فإنَّ هذه الأشياء أسباب لوجوب المال، فيناسب ضمَّ الدَّمَةِ إِلى الدَّمة. فقولُه : ما بايعت فلاناً : أي ما بايعت منه أو ضمان المبيع إن لحقه آفة، فالمكفولُ به مجهول ؛ لاحتمال استحقاق الكل أو البعض، فيضمن الكفيل الكل والبعض. وفي «السراج الوهاج»: إذا استحق المبيع كان للمشتري أن يخاصم البائع أولاً ، فإذا ثبت عليه استحقاق المبيع كان له أن يأخذ الثمن من أيهما شاء، وليس له أن يخاصم الكفيل أولاً في ظاهر الرواية ، وعن أبي يوسف له أنَّ له ذلك، وأجمعوا على أنَّ المبيع لو ظهر حُراً كان له أن يخاصم أيهما شاء. [١]قوله : بشرط ملائم ؛ وقد أطلق القُدُوري في «المختصر»(۲) بأنه يجوز تعليق الكفالة بالشروط، ولكن صرَّحَ في «شرحه» بأن المراد شرط يكون لوجوب الحق، كقوله : إذا استحق المبيعُ ، أو لإمكان الاستيفاء، وسهولة الأداء، كقوله : إذا قدم زيد وكان زيد مكفولاً عنه أو مضاربه، ولعل المراد بالملائم هذا. وقال بعضهم: إن تعليق الكفالة يجوز بسبب وجوب الحق وبغيره لا يجوز، كذا «القنية)، وغيرها. [٢] قوله : فلاناً ؛ قيَّد بقوله : فلاناً ؛ ليصير المكفول عنه معلوماً، فإنّ جهالة المكفول عنه تمنع صحة الكفالة بجهالة المكفول له في الإضافة، وقد مرَّ ذكره سابقاً، فلو قال : ما بايعت من النَّاسِ فعليَّ ضمانُهُ فهو باطل ؛ لتفاحش جهالة المكفول عنه وبه ، بخلاف إنفراد جهالة المكفول به ، فإنها حينئذ قليلة يتحمل. (۱) قيد : بفلان ؛ إشارة إلى أن المكفول عنه يجب أن يكون معلوماً ؛ لأن جهالته تمنع صحة الكفالة. ينظر: «فتح باب العناية(٢ : ٤٩٩). (۲) (مختصر القدوري (ص٥٦). (۳) «قنية المنية»(ق۲۳۹/أ).
Or if he makes the kafala conditional upon a suitable condition, such as: 'if you do not buy from so-and-so', or 'what is owed to you', or 'what he wrongfully took from you is my responsibility'. 'ما ذابَ' means 'what is owed'. In this form, 'ما' is a conditional particle meaning 'if you buy from so-and-so', so it is in the meaning of a condition. By 'suitable' he means 'appropriate', for these things are causes for the obligation of wealth, so it is appropriate to join one guarantee to another. His statement: 'ما بايعت فلاناً' means 'what you bought from him' or the guarantee of the sold item if a defect befalls, so the object of kafala is unknown due to the possibility of entitlement to all or part, so the guarantor guarantees all and part. In 'al-Siraj al-Wahaj': If the sold item is claimed, the buyer may sue the seller first; if the claim is proven against him, he may take the price from whichever of them he wishes. He may not sue the guarantor first in the apparent narration, and from Abu Yusuf that he has that right, and they agreed that if the sold item turns out to be a free person, he may sue whichever he wishes. [1] His statement: 'with a suitable condition'. Al-Quduri in 'al-Mukhtasar' stated that it is permissible to suspend kafala upon conditions, but in his commentary he specified that the intended condition is one for the obligation of the right, like saying 'if the sold item is claimed', or for the possibility of recovery and ease of payment, like saying 'if Zayd arrives' and Zayd is the guaranteed person or his agent. Perhaps 'suitable' means this. Some said: suspending kafala due to a cause of obligation is permissible, otherwise not, as in 'al-Qunyah' and others. [2] His statement: 'so-and-so'; he restricted by saying 'so-and-so' to make the guaranteed person known, for ignorance of the guaranteed person prevents the validity of kafala along with ignorance of the beneficiary in the connection, and it has been mentioned earlier. So if he said 'I guarantee for whoever I buy from among the people', it is invalid due to extreme ignorance of the guaranteed person and the object, unlike the case of ignorance of the object alone, for that is then minor and can be tolerated. (1) He restricted with 'by so-and-so' indicating that the guaranteed person must be known because ignorance of him prevents the validity of kafala. See: 'Fath Bab al-Inayah' (2:499). (2) 'Mukhtasar al-Quduri' (p.56). (3) 'Quniyat al-Munyah' (q 239/a).
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the text of al-Wiqayah, explaining the ruling on conditional kafala. The main text states that kafala may be made conditional upon a 'suitable' condition (شرط ملائم). The commentator clarifies that suitable conditions are those directly related to the obligation of the debt, such as a condition that the sold item is reclaimed, or that a person arrives who is the guarantor. The examples given by the author include 'if you do not buy from so-and-so' (ما بايعت فلاناً) which is a conditional sentence where 'ما' is a particle meaning 'if'. The suitability lies in the fact that these events are causes for the debt to become due, so it is logically consistent to link the guarantee to them. The passage also addresses the issue of ignorance (جهالة) of the guaranteed person or the object. If the guaranteed person is unknown, the kafala is invalid. However, if only the object of kafala is unknown but the person is known, it is permissible because the ignorance is minor and can be tolerated. Finally, the passage quotes 'al-Siraj al-Wahaj' which discusses a dispute: in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), the buyer may only sue the seller first if the sold item is reclaimed; he cannot sue the guarantor first. But Abu Yusuf allows suing the guarantor first. They agree that if the item turns out to be a free person, the buyer may sue either. This is an intra-Hanafi disagreement on procedural priorities.

Key Terms
جهالة (jahāla): Ignorance or uncertainty about a material element of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following kafala statements is valid according to the passage?
A) 'I guarantee whoever among my friends owes you money.'
B) 'I guarantee the debt that Zayd owes you.'
C) 'I guarantee the debt of the man who will come tomorrow.'
D) 'I guarantee the price of whatever I buy from you.'

Key Takeaway
Ignorance of the guaranteed person invalidates kafala; ignorance of the object alone is minor and tolerated.

Kafala with Non-Suitable Conditions: Condition Void, Contract Valid
Explains that kafala conditional on arbitrary events is void but guarantee remains

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafala conditional on arbitrary events and explain the consequence for the guarantee's timing.

Core Idea
A non-suitable condition in kafala is void, but the kafala remains valid and payment is due immediately.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a key principle in Hanafi muamalat: when a kafala (suretyship) is attached to an arbitrary event like 'if the wind blows' or 'if it rains', the condition is invalid, but the kafala itself is not nullified. Instead, the guarantee becomes due immediately. This mirrors similar rules in divorce and manumission, where invalid conditions do not affect the underlying contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
The act is kafala (suretyship), a contract of guarantee within muamalat (financial transactions). Identify that the ruling concerns a conditional kafala.
2
Analyze the condition type
The condition is 'mere' (mujarrad), devoid of suitability (ghayr mulā’im). Examples: 'if wind blows' or 'if rain comes'. Such conditions are called ta‘līq bi al-khaṭar (suspension on a risk).
3
Determine the hukm of the condition and the kafala
The condition is invalid (bāṭil) because it renders the contract uncertain. However, the kafala itself remains valid (ṣaḥīḥ). The invalid condition does not corrupt the contract.
Analogous to divorce and manumission: if conditioned on an invalid condition, the condition is void but the divorce or manumission takes effect.
4
Identify the practical consequence
Since the condition is void, the guarantee is not suspended. The guaranteed debt becomes due immediately (ḥālan). The creditor can demand payment from the surety at once.
5
Note the exception: condition as a term (ajal)
If the condition is framed as a term (e.g., 'I guarantee until the wind blows'), the term is invalid but the kafala remains valid and the debt is due immediately.
The footnote clarifies: making the event a term also results in immediate liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن علقت بمجرَّدِ الشَّرط فلا : كإن هَبَّتِ الرِّيحُ ، أو جاءَ المطر فإنِّي ضامن بثمنه لا ما اشتريت منه ، فإنِّي ضامن للمبيع، فإن الكفالة بالمبيع لا يجوز على ما يأتي. ( وإن علقت " بمجرَّدِ الشَّرط فلا (۱) : كإن هَبَّتِ الريحُ ، أو جاء المطر. [١] قوله : بثمنه ؛ قيد الضمان بالثمن ؛ لأنَّه قال : بايع فلاناً على أنَّ ما أصابك من خسران فعلي، لم يصح، صرح به في «البزازية» (٢). [٢] قوله : وإن علّقت ؛ أي الكفالة بمجرَّدِ الشرط ؛ أي بشرط مجرَّدٍ عن الملائمة فلا ؛ أي فلا يصح التعليق ؛ لأنه تعليق بالخطر، فلا يصح كالبيع. واعلم أنه قال في «الهداية» (۳) : فإنما لا يصحُ التعليق بمجرَّدِ الشرط، كقوله : إن هبت الريح ، أو جاء المطر، وكذا إذا جعل واحداً منهما أجلاً إلا أنه تصح الكفالة ويجب المال حالاً ؛ لأنَّ الكفالة ما صح تعليقها بالشرط لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة كالطلاق والعتاق. انتهى. والعلامة الإثقَاني مشى على ظاهر لفظه ، وقال : إذا كان الشرط فيه ملائماً جاز تعليق الكفالة، ومثل بقوله : إذا استحق المبيعُ فأنا ضامن إلى أن قال : وإن كان بخلاف ذلك، كهبوب الريح، ومجيء المطر لا يصحُ التعليق، ويبطل الشرط، لكن تنعقد الكفالة، ويجب المال ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ ما جاز تعليقه بالشرط لا يفسد بالشروط الفاسدة كالطلاق والعتاق. ومن نظر إلى ظاهر لفظه حكم بالسهو، كما قال العلامة العيني في «شرح الكنز : وذكر في «الهداية» () و «الكافي»: إن علَّقَ به تصح الكفالة، ويجب المال (٤) (1) أي فلا تصح الكفالة ولا يجب المال، أما لو جعل الأجل في الكفالة إلى هبوب الريح ونحوه فلا الكفالة ويجب المال .حالا ينظر: المبسوط» (۱۹): (٧٤)، وفتاوى قاضي خان(۳: ٥٢ - ٥٣)، و«الفتح» (٦ : ٣٠٢)، و«التبيين» (٤) : ١٥٤)، و«الرمز»(٢: ٧٢)، يصح التأجيل وتصح ا و«المنح»(ق۲ : ۸۱/ب). (۲) (الفتاوى البزازية (٦ : ١٤). (۳) ((الهداية) (۳ : ۹۰). (٤) شرح الكنز» (۲ : ۷۲). (٥) ((الهداية» (۳) : ٩٠).
And if it (the kafala) is made conditional upon a mere condition, it is invalid, like 'if the wind blows' or 'if rain comes', then 'I am guarantor for its price' not 'what I bought from him', for I am guarantor for the sold item, because kafala for the sold item is not valid as will come. (And if it is made conditional upon a mere condition, it is invalid: like 'if the wind blows' or 'if rain comes'. [1] His statement: 'for its price'; he restricted the guarantee to the price because he said: 'buy from so-and-so on condition that whatever loss befalls you is my responsibility', it is not valid, as stated in al-Bazzaziyya. [2] His statement: 'And if it is made conditional'; meaning the kafala is made conditional upon a mere condition, i.e., a condition devoid of suitability, it is invalid; i.e., the suspension is not valid because it is a condition upon a risk, so it is invalid like a sale. Know that in al-Hidayah it is said: indeed, suspension upon a mere condition is invalid, like his saying 'if the wind blows' or 'if rain comes', and likewise if he makes one of them a term, except that the kafala becomes valid and the wealth is obligatory immediately because the kafala, if its suspension by condition is not valid, does not become void by invalid conditions, like divorce and manumission. End. Al-Itqani followed its apparent wording and said: if the condition is suitable, suspension of kafala is permissible, and he gave the example of 'if the sold item is reclaimed, I am guarantor' until he said: and if it is otherwise, like the blowing of wind and falling of rain, the suspension is not valid, and the condition becomes void, but the kafala is contracted and the wealth is obligatory, because everything that is permissible to suspend by condition is not corrupted by invalid conditions, like divorce and manumission. And whoever looks at its apparent wording judges it a slip, as al-Ayni said in Sharh al-Kanz: and it is mentioned in al-Hidayah and al-Kafi: if he suspends it by that, the kafala is valid and the wealth is obligatory. (1) That is, the kafala is not valid and the wealth is not obligatory; but if he sets the term in kafala to the blowing of wind and the like, then the kafala is valid and the wealth is obligatory immediately. See: al-Mabsut (19:74), Fatawa Qadi Khan (3:52-53), al-Fath (6:302), al-Tibyan (4:154), al-Ramz (2:72), and al-Manh (q 2:81/b). (2) al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya (6:14). (3) al-Hidayah (3:90). (4) Sharh al-Kanz (2:72). (5) al-Hidayah (3:90).
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage deals with kafala that is made conditional upon arbitrary events that have no connection to the obligation, such as 'if the wind blows' or 'if it rains'. The ruling is that such a condition is invalid because it is a condition upon a risk (تعليق بالخطر), similar to a condition in a sale that would invalidate the sale. However, the kafala itself remains valid and the guarantee becomes due immediately (يجب المال حالا). This is because the kafala contract, when the condition is not suitable, is not corrupted by the invalid condition; it is analogous to divorce and manumission, which, if made conditional upon an invalid condition, the condition is void but the act takes effect. The passage quotes al-Hidayah and then discusses a subtlety: if the condition is made as a term (أجل), like 'I guarantee until the wind blows', then the kafala is valid and the debt is due immediately, because setting an invalid term does not invalidate the kafala. Al-Itqani and al-Ayni confirm this ruling. The footnotes also clarify that if the condition is used as a term, the kafala and the term are valid? Actually, the footnote says: if he sets the term to wind blowing, the kafala is valid and the wealth is due immediately; the term is invalid? The exact wording: 'لو جعل الأجل في الكفالة إلى هبوب الريح ونحوه فلا يصح التأجيل وتصح الكفالة ويجب المال حالا' meaning the term setting is invalid but the kafala is valid and wealth due immediately. So the key is that non-suitable conditions do not affect the validity of the kafala itself.

Key Terms
تعليق بالخطر (ta‘līq bi al-khaṭar): A conditional suspension based on a risky or uncertain event, which is invalid in contracts.
شرط فاسد (sharṭ fāsid): An invalid condition, one that is not suitable (غير ملائم) or violates the nature of the contract.
أجل (ajal): A term or deadline for payment. In kafala, setting a term based on an uncertain event like wind blowing is invalid, but the kafala remains valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says, 'I guarantee your purchase from so-and-so if the wind blows,' what is the ruling?
A) The kafala is invalid because the condition is forbidden.
B) The kafala is valid only when the wind blows.
C) The kafala is valid immediately and the condition is void.
D) The kafala is suspended until the wind blows.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, conditioning kafala on an arbitrary event invalidates the condition but the kafala remains valid and payment is due immediately.
