Kafalah Conditioned on an Inappropriate Condition: Valid or Void?
Ruling on kafalah conditioned on a mere risk event

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafalah conditioned on an unsuitable condition, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the reconciliation method (ta'jil) to resolve conflicting texts.

Core Idea
An invalid condition does not void the kafalah; the money becomes due immediately.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya deals with a tricky case: if someone says 'I guarantee the price if the wind blows' – that condition is unsuitable. Early Hanafi sources (Zahir al-Riwayah) declared the entire kafalah invalid. But later Hanafi jurists, led by al-Hidayah, ruled that the kafalah remains valid and the money due immediately, drawing an analogy with divorce and emancipation. We will trace this dispute and learn the preferred fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
Determine what the kafalah is conditioned upon (e.g., a random event like wind blowing) and note that this is an unsuitable condition (ghayr mula'im).
The condition is purely external and unrelated to the guarantee.
2
State the apparent ruling from early texts
According to al-Mabsut and Fatawa Qadikhan, such conditioning renders the entire kafalah invalid because it is based on a risk (khatar).
This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
3
Present the ruling from al-Hidayah
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah holds that the condition is void but the kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately, drawing a parallel with talaq and itq where invalid conditions do not invalidate the original act.
This becomes the mufta bihi position.
4
Analyze the reconciliation
Later scholars reconcile the conflicting texts by interpreting 'conditioning' (ta'liq) as 'term-setting' (ta'jil), so that the statement is read as 'I guarantee you, with the term set to the wind blowing,' which makes the guarantee valid and the condition (term) void.
This harmonizes al-Mabsut and al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن علقت بمجرَّدِ الشَّرط فلا : كإن هَبَّتِ الرِّيحُ ، أو جاءَ المطر فإنِّي ضامن بثمنه لا ما اشتريت منه ، فإنِّي ضامن للمبيع، فإن الكفالة بالمبيع لا يجوز على ما يأتي. ( وإن علقت " بمجرَّدِ الشَّرط فلا (۱) : كإن هَبَّتِ الريحُ ، أو جاء المطر. [١] قوله : بثمنه ؛ قيد الضمان بالثمن ؛ لأنَّه قال : بايع فلاناً على أنَّ ما أصابك من خسران فعلي، لم يصح، صرح به في «البزازية» (٢). [٢] قوله : وإن علّقت ؛ أي الكفالة بمجرَّدِ الشرط ؛ أي بشرط مجرَّدٍ عن الملائمة فلا ؛ أي فلا يصح التعليق ؛ لأنه تعليق بالخطر، فلا يصح كالبيع. واعلم أنه قال في «الهداية» (۳) : فإنما لا يصحُ التعليق بمجرَّدِ الشرط، كقوله : إن هبت الريح ، أو جاء المطر، وكذا إذا جعل واحداً منهما أجلاً إلا أنه تصح الكفالة ويجب المال حالاً ؛ لأنَّ الكفالة ما صح تعليقها بالشرط لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة كالطلاق والعتاق. انتهى. والعلامة الإثقَاني مشى على ظاهر لفظه ، وقال : إذا كان الشرط فيه ملائماً جاز تعليق الكفالة، ومثل بقوله : إذا استحق المبيعُ فأنا ضامن إلى أن قال : وإن كان بخلاف ذلك، كهبوب الريح، ومجيء المطر لا يصحُ التعليق، ويبطل الشرط، لكن تنعقد الكفالة، ويجب المال ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ ما جاز تعليقه بالشرط لا يفسد بالشروط الفاسدة كالطلاق والعتاق. ومن نظر إلى ظاهر لفظه حكم بالسهو، كما قال العلامة العيني في «شرح الكنز : وذكر في «الهداية» () و «الكافي»: إن علَّقَ به تصح الكفالة، ويجب المال حالا ، وهو سهو ؛ لأنَّ الحكم فيه أنَّ التعليق لا يصح ، ولا يلزمه المال ؛ لأنَّ الشرط غيرُ ملائم ، فصار كما لو علقه بدخول الدارِ ونحوه ، مما ليس بملائم، نعم ؛ لو جعل الأجل في الكفالة إلى هبوب الريح ونحوه ، لا يصح التأجيل ، ويجب المال حالاً. انتهى. وهكذا في «التبيين»، و«المنح» لكن أجاب عنه صاحب «البحر» (۳) : بأن قوله : إلا أنه تصح الكفالة ؛ إنما يعود إلى الأصل بنحو : إن هبت الريح ، لا إلى التعليق بالشرط، وقوله : لما صح تعليقها ؛ معناه : لما صح تأجيلها بأجل متعارف مجازاً جاز عدم الثبوتِ في الحال في كل منهما. وقال في «الفتح») : فالحاصل أنَّ الشرط الغير ملائم لا تصح معه الكفالة أصلاً ، ومع الأجل الغير الملائم نصح حالة، ويبطل الأجل، لكن تعليل المصنّف له هذا ، بقوله : لأنَّ الكفالة لما صح تعليقها بالشرط لا تبطلُ بالشروط الفاسدة كالطلاق والعتاق يقتضي أن في التعليق بغير الملائم يصح الكفالة حالة، وإنما يبطل الشرط. والمصرح به في «المبسوط»)، وفي فتاوى قاضي خان (٦) : أنَّ الكفالة باطلة ، فتصحيحه أن يحمل لفظ تعليقها على معنى تأجيلها ، بجامع أنَّ في كل منهما عدم ثبوتِ الحكم في الحال، وقلد المصنف الله في هذا الاستعمال لفظ «المبسوط»، فإنّه ذكر التعليق وأراد التأجيل. انتهى. ولعلمي أن هذا أحسنُ وجوه التصحيح، فتأمل فيه ولا تعجل.
And if it is conditioned upon a mere condition, then no: such as 'If the wind blows, or the rain comes, then I am the guarantor for its price' not 'for what I bought from him', for I am the guarantor for the sold item, because kafalah for the sold item is not permissible as will come. (And if conditioned upon a mere condition, then no: such as if the wind blows or the rain comes. [1] His statement: 'for its price' restricts the guarantee to the price because he said: 'Sell to so-and-so on condition that if any loss hits you, it is on me' – that is not valid, as explicitly stated in al-Bazzaziyyah. [2] His statement: 'And if conditioned' i.e., kafalah conditioned upon a mere condition, i.e., a condition devoid of suitability, then no, i.e., the conditioning is not valid because it is conditioning upon a risk, so it is not valid like a sale. Know that he said in al-Hidayah: The conditioning upon a mere condition is not valid, like his saying 'If the wind blows, or the rain comes', and likewise if he makes one of them an (end-term then the kafalah is valid and the money becomes due immediately, because kafalah – just as its conditioning upon a condition is not valid – is not corrupted by invalid conditions, like divorce and emancipation. End. Al-Ithqani followed its apparent wording and said: If the condition is suitable, conditioning of kafalah is permissible, and he gave as example his saying 'If the sold item is reclaimed, I am guarantor' up to his saying: 'And if it is otherwise, like the blowing of the wind and the coming of rain, the conditioning is not valid, and the condition is invalidated, but the kafalah is contracted, and the money is obligatory, because everything whose conditioning by a condition is permissible is not corrupted by invalid conditions, like divorce and emancipation.' And whoever looks at its apparent wording judges it as an oversight, as said by al-Ayni in Sharh al-Kanz: And it is mentioned in al-Hidayah and al-Kafi: If he conditions with it, the kafalah is valid and the money becomes due immediately. This is an oversight because the ruling in it is that the conditioning is not valid, and the money is not obligatory because the condition is unsuitable, so it becomes like conditioning upon entering a house and the like, which are not suitable. Yes, if he makes the term in kafalah up to the blowing of the wind and the like, the term-setting is not valid, and the money becomes due immediately. End. And thus in al-Tibyan and al-Manh. But the author of al-Bahr responded to him: that his statement 'except that the kafalah is valid' refers to the original like 'if the wind blows', not to conditioning upon the condition; and his statement 'since its conditioning is valid' means: since its term-setting by a conventional term metaphorically, it is permissible not to establish the ruling immediately in each of them. And he said in al-Fath: The upshot is that the unsuitable condition: kafalah is not valid with it at all; and with the unsuitable term, it is valid immediately, and the term is invalidated. But the author's reasoning for this, by his saying 'because kafalah – just as its conditioning by condition is valid – is not corrupted by invalid conditions, like divorce and emancipation' requires that in conditioning with an unsuitable condition, the kafalah is valid immediately, and only the condition is invalidated. But what is explicitly stated in al-Mabsut and in Fatawa Qadikhan is that the kafalah is invalid. So its correction is that the word 'conditioning' should be interpreted to mean 'term-setting', because in each there is non-establishment of the ruling immediately. And the author followed al-Mabsut in this usage, for he mentioned conditioning and intended term-setting. End. And I know that this is the best face of correction, so ponder it and do not rush.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling of kafalah conditioned upon an inappropriate condition (shart ghayr mula'im). The base matn states that if kafalah is conditioned upon a mere condition like 'if the wind blows' or 'if the rain comes', then the kafalah is invalid. However, the commentator clarifies that this refers to conditioning with a condition that is not suitable (ghayr mula'im). In al-Hidayah, it is stated that if one makes such a condition, the condition is invalid but the kafalah itself is valid and the money becomes due immediately, drawing an analogy to divorce and emancipation where invalid conditions do not invalidate the original act. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Al-Ithqani followed the apparent wording of al-Hidayah, while al-Ayni considered it an oversight. Al-Ayni argued that the ruling should be that the conditioning is invalid and the money is not obligatory because the condition is unsuitable. The author of al-Bahr reconciles by stating that the phrase 'the kafalah is valid' refers to the original kafalah (the base act) not the conditioning. Al-Fath summarizes that with an unsuitable condition, kafalah is invalid; with an unsuitable term, it is valid immediately. The correct interpretation is to treat 'conditioning' as 'term-setting' (ta'jil), as found in al-Mabsut, because in both cases the ruling is not established immediately. The commentator advises to ponder this as the best resolution.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract of guarantee where the kafil (surety) becomes liable for a debt or obligation of the asil (principal).
التعليق بالشرط (al-ta'liq bi al-shart): Making the validity or obligation of a contract contingent upon a future uncertain event (shart).
الشرط غير الملائم (al-shart ghayr al-mula'im): A condition that is not appropriate or customary in the context of the contract, such as wind blowing or rain falling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person says, 'I guarantee the price of the sold item if the wind blows,' what is the Hanafi ruling according to the relied-upon position?
A) The kafalah is invalid because the condition is unsuitable.
B) The kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately; the condition is void.
C) The kafalah is suspended until the wind blows.
D) The kafalah is valid only if the condition is written.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an unsuitable condition attached to kafalah does not void the contract; the guarantee stands and the debt is payable now.

Kafalah for 'Whatever You Have Owed' – Amount Determination
Ruling on kafalah when amount is disputed and evidence exists

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on kafalah when the debt amount is disputed and determine the surety’s liability based on evidence or admission.

Core Idea
Evidence establishes liability like direct sight; without evidence, the surety is believed on his admission with oath.

This passage addresses a common scenario in guarantee (kafalah) where the surety says 'I guarantee for whatever you have owed' without specifying an amount. If the creditor and principal disagree on the debt, how much must the surety pay? The Hanafi rule clarifies: the surety is only liable for the amount proven by evidence (bayyinah). If no evidence exists, the surety's own admission under oath determines his liability, while the principal's admission of a larger amount only affects himself. We will explore the reasoning, including the qiyas (analogy) that equates evidence to direct sight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and legal question
The passage deals with a kafalah contract where the surety guarantees 'what you have owed' – an unspecified debt. The creditor and principal (asil) dispute the amount. The question: what is the surety (kafil) liable for?
The key is that the amount is not fixed at the time of guarantee.
2
Apply the ruling with evidence
If there is evidence (bayyinah) proving the debt amount, the surety is liable for that exact amount. The proof is qiyas: what is established by evidence is like what is established by direct sight. Just as seeing the debt directly would make the surety liable, so does proof by evidence.
Evidence here means two male witnesses or one male and two females (Hanafi requirement).
3
Apply the ruling without evidence
If no evidence exists, the surety is believed concerning the amount he admits to, and he must take an oath. The principal’s admission of a larger amount only binds himself, not the surety.
This protects the surety from being bound by the principal's potentially exaggerated claim.
4
Examine the logical structure (qiyas)
The commentator cites the qiyas: 'what is established by evidence is like what is established by direct sight.' The 'illa (effective cause) is the establishment of fact. Direct sight establishes certainty; evidence also establishes certainty (in legal terms). Thus, the same liability follows.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) because the link is obvious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كفل بما لك عليه ضَمِنَ قَدْرَ ما قامت به بينةٌ، ويلا بينةٍ صُدِّقَ الكفيل فيما يُقِرُّ به مع حَلْفِهِ، والأصيل فيما يُقر بأكثر منه على نفسه فقط فإن كفلَ بما لك عليه ضَمِنَ قَدْرَ ما قامت به بينة، وبلا بينةٍ صُدِّقَ الكفيل فيما يقر به مع خَلْفِهِ، والأصيل فيما يُقِرُّ بأكثر منه على نفسه فقط) [١] قوله : ضمن قدر ما قامت به بيّنة ؛ لأنَّ الثابت بالبينة كالثابت عياناً ، ولو عاين ما عليه وكفل عنه لزمه ما عليه، فكذا إذا ثبت بالبينة.
For if he guarantees for what you have owed, he is liable for the amount established by evidence; and without evidence, the surety is believed in what he admits with his oath, and the principal is believed in what he admits to more than that, for himself only. For if he guarantees for what you have owed, he is liable for the amount established by evidence; and without evidence, the surety is believed in what he admits with his oath, and the principal is believed in what he admits to more than that, for himself only.) [1] His statement: 'He is liable for the amount established by evidence' because what is established by evidence is like what is established by direct sight; and if he witnessed what he owed and guaranteed for him, he would be liable for what he owed, so likewise if it is established by evidence.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling for a situation where the surety says 'I guarantee you for whatever you have owed to so-and-so' (kafalah bima lak alayh). The amount of the debt may be disputed. The ruling: the surety is liable only for the amount that is proven by evidence (bayyinah). If there is no evidence, the surety is believed (his word is accepted) regarding the amount he admits to, but he must take an oath. The principal (asil) is believed only regarding his own admission of a larger amount, but that admission only binds himself (not the surety). The commentator explains that what is established by evidence has the same force as what is seen directly (cognizance). So if the surety had direct knowledge of the debt and guaranteed, he would be liable; similarly, if the debt is proven by evidence, he is liable. This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).

Key Terms
الكفيل (al-kafil): The surety who guarantees the debt of the principal.
الأصيل (al-asil): The principal debtor on whose behalf the guarantee is made.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically the testimony of two witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the absence of evidence, the surety's liability is determined by:
A) The principal's admission
B) The surety's admission, with his oath
C) The creditor's claim
D) The judge's estimation

Key Takeaway
A surety is liable only for the debt amount proven by evidence or, if none, by his own admission under oath; the principal's larger admission does not bind the surety.

Suretyship for the Deceased: Valid or Invalid in Hanafi Fiqh?
Intra-Hanafi dispute with talil on kafala after death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on kafala for a deceased person's debt and explain the legal reasoning (talil) behind each view, including the concept of dayn saqit.

Core Idea
A debt is not extinguished by death, but can become 'fallen' in worldly rulings, dividing the school.

Kafala (suretyship) is a contract where a person joins his liability to that of the principal for a debt. But what if the principal dies? Can a living person still guarantee the deceased's debt? This passage reveals a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds such a guarantee invalid because the debt has 'fallen' in worldly rulings, while his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, consider it valid. We will trace their arguments and see how this dispute affects practical fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What exactly is being disputed? Is it about the validity of a new kafala contract created after the principal's death, or about an existing kafala after the principal dies? The text clarifies it is about initiating a new kafala after death.
Note the exception: if kafala was made during life and the principal dies insolvent, it remains valid.
2
State the position of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that kafala after death is valid. Their main proof: a hadith where the Prophet (ﷺ) prayed over a deceased only after a companion guaranteed his debt. Also, they argue that debt is not dropped by death; it remains until payment, discharge, or dissolution.
They emphasize that the debt continues in the Hereafter and the guarantor does not become free.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa says kafala for a deceased is invalid because it is kafala for a 'fallen debt' (dayn saqit). He defines debt as an act (payment) which a deceased cannot perform, so it falls in worldly rulings. Demand (mutalaba) from the original debtor is gone, so it cannot be transferred to the guarantor.
He distinguishes worldly rulings from Hereafter: the debt remains for the Hereafter but not for worldly enforcement.
4
Analyze the responses to objections
The text addresses an objection: if the debt fell, the guarantor would be free, but he is not; so the debt must exist. Two responses: the guarantor is a substitute (khalaf) so he doesn't become free; or the falling is by necessity limited to the original debtor, not extending to the guarantor or the claimant.
This shows the subtlety in defining what 'fallen' means.
5
Apply to the practical fatwa hierarchy
Although Abu Hanifa's view is logically coherent, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the school is the validity, because it is from Zahir al-Riwayah and supported by the hadith. The text also includes Abu Yusuf's specific exception for kafala during illness.
The tarjih is implied: the Sahibayn's view is more practical and dominant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي رواية : قال ذلك علي الله ، فصلى عليه، ذكرَهُ العلامةُ الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز» ، فلو لم تصح الكفالة لما صلى بعدها ؛ ولأنَّ الدين واجب عليه في حياته، وهو لا يسقط إلا بالإيفاء أو الإبراء أو انفساخ سبب الوجوب. وبالموت لم يتحقق شيء من ذلك ، فلا يسقط ؛ ولهذا يؤاخذ به في الآخرة، ولا يبرؤ كفيله في حياته بموته ، ولو تبرع أحدٌ بقضاءه صح، ولو لم يكن عليه دين لما جاز للطالب أخذه من المتبرع ، وكذا يبقى إذا كان به كفيل، أو ترك مالاً. ولأبي حنيفة له : أنَّ الكفالة عن الميت كفالة بدين ساقط ، والكفالة بدين ساقط باطلة، وإنّما قلنا: إنها بدين ساقط ؛ لأنَّ الدين في الحقيقة فعل؛ ولهذا يوصف بالوجوب، يقال: وجب عليه الدين، أي أداؤه ، كما يقال : وجبت عليه الصَّلاةُ : أي أداؤها، والأداء لا يتصوَّرُ من الميِّتِ فسقط سواء كان له مال أو لم يكن له مال في حق أحكام الدنيا ؛ ليصح تحقيق معنى الكفالة، وهو ضمُّ الذمَّةِ إلى الذمَّةِ في حق وجوب المطالبة. والمطالبة ساقطة عن الأصيل فلا يمكن إيجابها على الكفيل تبعاً، إذ لا يُضَمُّ الموجود إلى المعدوم، إلا أنه مالٌ في بعض الأحكام كوجوب الزكاة ؛ لأنه يؤول إليه بواسطة الإيفاء، والميت عاجز عن الإيفاء بنفسه وبكفيله في حياته، إذ الفرضُ أن لا كفيل له، فسقط عنه الدِّينُ في حقٌّ أحكام الدنيا ضرورة، وصحة الكفالة تقتضي قيام الدين في حق أحكام الدنيا ؛ ليتحقق معنى الكفالة : أعني ضمُّ الذمَّةِ إلى الذمةِ في المطالبة. والمؤاخذة من أحكام الآخرة لا من أحكام الدنيا، وإنما لم يبرأ بموتِهِ كفيله في حياته ؛ لأنه كان خلفه في الاستيفاء منه، فجعلَ الدِّينُ باقياً في حقه كما لو كان للميت مال، وصح التبرع لقضائه ؛ لأنَّ التبرُّعَ لا يعتمدُ قيامَ الدَّين ؛ لأنه تبرئة في حق الآخرة ، فإنَّ الدين باق في حق الطالب ؛ لأنه أمر بينهما. إلا إذا كَفِلَ عن مورثه في مرضه مع غيبة غرمائه وعند أبي يوسف إذا بلغه الخبر وأجاز جاز وهذا الخلاف في الكفالة بالنفس والمال معاً إلا إذا كَفِلَ عن مورثه في مرضه مع غيبة غرمائه وأما الكفالة فأمر بين الكفيل والأصيل ؛ لأنه التزم ما على الأصيل، والحديث يحتمل أن يكون إقراراً بكفالة سابقة، فإنَّ لفظ الإقرار والإنشاء في الكفالة سواء، ولا عموم الحكاية الفعل، ويحتمل أن يكون وعداً لا كفالة، وكان امتناعه من الصَّلاة عليه ليظهرُ طريق قضاء ما عليه ، فلما ظهر بالوعدِ صلَّى عليه. لا يقال : لو سقط الدين لبرئ الكفيل ؛ لأنَّ براءته توجب براءة الكفيل، فلمّا لم يبرأ عُلِمَ أنّ عليه ديناً، فيجوز ابتداء الكفالة به؛ لأنا نقول : الكفيل خلف عنه فلا يبرأ ، أو نقولُ في حق الطالب: لا يسقط ؛ لأنَّ سقوطه ضروري فلا يتعدى المطلوب، كذا قالوا هذا المقام. وقيدت المسألة بالكفالة بعد موته ؛ لأنه لو كَفِلَ في حياتِهِ ثُمَّ مَاتَ مُفلِساً لم تبطل الكفالة، وكذا لو كان به رهن ثم مات مفلساً لا يبطلُ الرَّهن ؛ لأنَّ سقوط الدِّينِ عنه في أحكام الدنيا ضرورة، فيتقدَّرُ بقدرها، فأبقيناه في حق الكفيل والرهن ؛ لعدم الضرورة، ذكره في «البحر» نقلا عن «المعراج». قوله : وعند أبي يوسف الخ؛ واختلف المشايخ على قوله : فقيل : عنده تصح بوصف التوقف، حتى إن رضي به الطالب بعد القيام من المجلس نفذ، وإن لم يرض به بطل. وقيل : بوصف النفاذ ورضاء الطالب ليس بشرط عنده، وهو الأصح ؛ لأنَّ الكفالة التزام من الكفيل لا إلزام فيه على غيره ، فيتم بالملتزم وحده كالإقرار ؛ فلهذا تصح مع الجهالة. ومن قال بوصف التوقفِ جعل الكفيل كالفُضُولي في النكاح، إذا زوج امرأةٌ وليس عنها قائل، حيث يتوقَّفُ النّكاحُ عنده على إجازتها فيما وراء المجلس، فكأنه جعل قوله : كفلت لفلان عن فلان بكذا عقداً تاماً، لكنه تصرف لغيره فيتوقف على رضائه.
And in a narration: 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said that, so he prayed upon him, mentioned by Allamah Zayla'i in Sharh al-Kanz. So if the suretyship were not valid, he would not have prayed after it. And because the debt was obligatory upon him in his life, and it is not dropped except by payment, or discharge, or dissolution of the cause of obligation. By death, none of these occurred, so it does not drop. Hence he is taken to account for it in the Hereafter, and his guarantor is not freed by his death during his lifetime, and if someone voluntarily pays it, it is valid; and if he had no debt, it would not be permissible for the creditor to take from the volunteer. Similarly it remains if there is a guarantor or he leaves property. And for Abu Hanifa there is that suretyship for the deceased is suretyship for a fallen debt, and suretyship for a fallen debt is invalid. We only say it is for a fallen debt because debt in reality is an act; therefore it is described as obligatory: it is said 'the debt became obligatory upon him', i.e., its payment, just as it is said 'prayer became obligatory upon him', i.e., its performance. And performance is inconceivable from the deceased, so it falls, whether he has property or not, with respect to worldly rulings, to realize the meaning of suretyship, which is joining one liability to another in respect of the right of demand. And demand is dropped from the principal, so it cannot be imposed on the guarantor as a follow-up, since the existing cannot be joined to the non-existing, except that it is property in some rulings like the obligation of zakat because it eventually reaches it through payment. And the deceased is incapable of payment by himself or by his guarantor in his lifetime, because the assumption is that he has no guarantor. So the debt fell from him in worldly rulings by necessity. And validity of suretyship requires the debt to exist in worldly rulings to realize the meaning of suretyship, i.e., joining liability to liability in demand. And accountability is from the rulings of the Hereafter, not from worldly rulings. And his guarantor is not freed by his death in his lifetime because he was his agent in recovery, so the debt is considered remaining in his right as if the deceased had property. And voluntary payment is valid because voluntary payment does not depend on the existence of the debt, since it is a discharge in the right of the Hereafter, for the debt remains in the right of the claimant because it is a matter between them. Except if he became surety for his testator during his illness with absence of his creditors. And according to Abu Yusuf: if he received the news and consented, it is valid. And this disagreement is in suretyship for the person and for property together, except if he became surety for his testator during his illness with absence of his creditors. And as for suretyship, it is a matter between the guarantor and the principal because he undertakes what is upon the principal. And the hadith may be an acknowledgment of a prior suretyship, for the wording of acknowledgment and origination in suretyship is the same, and there is no generality from a singular action. And it may be a promise not a suretyship, and his refraining from praying upon him was to make apparent the way to pay what was upon him. When it became apparent by the promise, he prayed upon him. It cannot be said: if the debt fell, the guarantor would be free, because his freedom necessitates the freedom of the guarantor. Since he is not free, it is known that he has a debt, so suretyship for it can be initiated. We say: the guarantor is his agent, so he is not free. Or we say regarding the claimant: it does not drop because its dropping is by necessity so it does not extend to the requested. Thus they said this. And the issue is restricted to suretyship after his death, because if he became surety in his lifetime then died insolvent, the suretyship does not become void. Similarly, if there was a mortgage then died insolvent, the mortgage does not become void because the dropping of the debt from him in worldly rulings is by necessity, so it is limited to its extent. So we left it in the right of the guarantor and the mortgage due to absence of necessity. He mentioned it in al-Bahr quoting from al-Mi'raj. His statement: 'And according to Abu Yusuf, etc.'; and the scholars differed on his statement. It is said: according to him it is valid with the description of suspension, so that if the claimant consents after leaving the gathering, it is effective; if he does not consent, it is void. And it is said: with the description of immediate effect, and the consent of the claimant is not a condition according to him, and this is the most correct because suretyship is an undertaking by the guarantor, not an imposition on others, so it is perfected by the obligor alone like acknowledgment; hence it is valid even with ignorance. And whoever said with the description of suspension made the guarantor like a proxy in marriage when a woman gives herself in marriage without a guardian, where the marriage is suspended on her permission after the gathering. It is as if he made his statement 'I became surety for so-and-so for so-and-so for such' a complete contract, but it is a disposition for another so it is suspended on his consent.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a deep intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity of suretyship for the debt of a deceased person. The text first presents the position that kafala is valid after death, supported by a narration that the Prophet (ﷺ) prayed over a deceased only after a companion guaranteed his debt. The reasoning is that the debt does not fall due to death; it remains obligatory and can only be dropped by payment, discharge, or dissolution of the cause. Hence the guarantor remains bound. Then Abu Hanifa's opposing view is given: kafala for a deceased is kafala for a fallen debt, which is invalid. He argues that debt is essentially an act (payment), and since the deceased cannot perform the act, the debt falls in worldly rulings. Demand cannot be established against a guarantor if it does not exist against the original debtor. The text then discusses exceptions: if the suretyship was made by a testator during illness with creditors absent, Abu Yusuf allows it with consent. The author then addresses an objection: if the debt fell, the guarantor would be free; but since the guarantor is not free, the debt must exist. The response is that the guarantor is a substitute, so he doesn't become free; alternatively, the debt drops only by necessity for the original debtor, not for the claimant. Then a qualification: the ruling is restricted to kafala after death; if it was made during life and the debtor dies insolvent, the suretyship remains valid. Finally, a commentary on Abu Yusuf's statement: scholars differ whether it is valid with suspension (awaiting creditor's consent) or immediately effective; the latter is more correct because kafala is a unilateral undertaking. The analogy with a proxy marriage illustrates the suspension view. Throughout, the author maintains a Hanafi perspective, carefully distinguishing between worldly and Hereafter rulings.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafala): Suretyship or guarantee, where a person (kafil) joins his liability to that of the principal (asil) for a debt or for the person.
دين ساقط (dayn saqit): A debt that is considered dropped or fallen in terms of worldly legal enforcement, though it may still exist in the Hereafter.
المطالبة (al-mutalaba): The right of the creditor to demand payment from the debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is kafala for a deceased person invalid?
A) Because the debt is completely extinguished by death, including in the Hereafter.
B) Because the debt is considered fallen in worldly rulings, and demand cannot be transferred to the guarantor.
C) Because the guarantor cannot be held liable for a debt that the deceased did not acknowledge.
D) Because the Prophet (ﷺ) never accepted kafala after death.

Key Takeaway
Kafala for a deceased person's debt is a disputed matter in the Hanafi school; the mufta bihi position (Sahibayn) holds it valid because the debt is not extinguished by death.

Definition and Ruling of Kafala bi al-Qisma
Commentary defining 'qisma' and ruling on kafala for it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the different interpretations of 'qisma' in Hanafi fiqh and explain why kafala bi al-qisma is valid.

Core Idea
Kafala bi al-qisma is valid regardless of the exact meaning of 'qisma' — it can be a share or a tax.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents a detailed commentary on the term 'qisma' as it appears in the chapter of kafala (suretyship). The Hanafi mashayikh differed over its precise meaning, yet they agreed on the ruling: kafala bi al-qisma is valid. Let us explore the definitions and the key debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'qisma' and its various definitions
Read the passage and list the different meanings given to 'qisma' by the Hanafi scholars: e.g., the nawa'ib themselves, a portion thereof, regular taxes, or a division among partners.
Note that the word is used in the context of kafala, so it must denote something that can be guaranteed.
2
Understand the objection of Abu Bakr ibn Sa'id
He argued that 'qisma' is a verbal noun (masdar) meaning an action, and actions cannot be guaranteed. Find this objection in the text.
This objection would invalidate kafala bi al-qisma if accepted.
3
Analyze the rebuttal using the Quran
The Hanafi response cites Quran 54:28 where 'qisma' means 'portion' (nasib), not an action. Thus, 'qisma' can denote a share, which is a guaranteeable debt.
This linguistic evidence overrides the objection.
4
Extract the ruling (hukm)
The commentator states: 'wa ayyā mā kān, fa al-kafālahu bihā ṣaḥīḥah' — however it may be, kafala for it is valid.
The ruling is that the guarantee is sahih (valid), not that the act of division is guaranteed.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Consider a case where a man guarantees to pay his neighbor's share of a common irrigation tax. Under this ruling, the guarantee is valid because it pertains to a monetary obligation.

قوله فقد قيل ... الخ ; حاصله إن المشايخ اختلفوا في معناها :
١. فقال بعضُهم : هي النوائب بعينها، فالعطف للبيان والتفسير، وتكون الرواية بالواو ، أو حصة منها بأن قسَّم ما ينوب العامة ، نحو: مؤنة كري الأنهار المشتركة، فأصاب واحداً شيء من ذلك، فيجب أداؤه فكفل به رجلٌ. ٢. وقال بعضُهم :القسمة : أي النائبةُ الموظفة المقرّرة من عند الإمام، وهي المقاطعات الديوانية في كلّ شهر أو ثلاثة أشهر، والمراد بالنوائب ما هو منها غير راتب فتغايرا ، فالرواية في هاتين : بأو.
. وقال بعضُهم معناها : إذا اقتسما ثم منع أحد الشريكين قسم صاحبه. ٤. وقال أبو جعفر الهندواني الله معناها : إن أحد الشريكين إذا طلب القسمة من صاحبه وامتنع الآخر عن ذلك فضمن إنسان ليقوم مقامه في القسمه جاز؛ لأن القسمة واجبة عليه.

As for al-qisma, it is said: they are the nawa'ib themselves, or a portion thereof, and it is said: they are the appointed regular nawa'ib, and al-tawa'ib are the irregular ones. However it may be, kafala for it is valid. (The commentator says:) His statement: 'it is said...' etc.; its summary is that the shaykhs differed regarding its meaning: 1. Some said: they are the nawa'ib themselves, so the conjunction is for clarification and explanation, and the narration is with 'waw', or a portion thereof... 2. Some said: al-qisma: the appointed regular nawa'ib determined by the imam, which are the provincial taxes every month or three months, and by al-nawa'ib is meant what is not regular among them, so they differ. 3. Some said its meaning: when two partners divide and then one prevents the other's share. 4. Abu Ja'far al-Hinduwani said its meaning: when one partner requests division from his partner and the other refuses, then a person guarantees to take his place in the division, it is permissible because division is obligatory upon him. 5. Abu Bakr ibn Sa'id said: this word is a mistake because al-qisma is a masdar, and the masdar is an action, and this action is not guaranteed. It was rebutted that al-qisma comes in the meaning of portion, as Allah said: 'And inform them that the water is a division between them', and what is meant is the portion.

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'qisma' in the context of kafala (suretyship). The commentator first reports different interpretations: some say it refers to the actual calamities or taxes (nawa'ib), others say it refers to the regular taxes imposed by the ruler. Despite the differences, the ruling is that kafala for it is valid. The commentator then elaborates on four opinions: first, that qisma is the nawa'ib themselves; second, that it is the regular taxes; third, that it refers to a share in a partnership division; fourth, that it is the guarantee to perform an obligatory division. Then a fifth opinion from Abu Bakr ibn Sa'id is mentioned, who argues that the word 'qisma' is a verbal noun meaning an action, which cannot be guaranteed. This is rebutted by citing the Quranic usage of 'qisma' to mean 'portion' (nasib), thus supporting the validity of kafala for it.

Key Terms
القسمة (al-qisma): In this context, it refers to either the calamities/taxes (nawa'ib), a share thereof, or an obligatory division among partners.
النوائب (al-nawa'ib): Calamities or taxes imposed on the public; also used for regular taxes (muwazzafa) and irregular ones (ghayr muwazzafa).
الكفالة (al-kafala): Suretyship; a contract where one person guarantees the fulfillment of an obligation by another.

Surety's Word on Term in Kafala: Hanafi Position
Ruling and dispute on term in kafala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the surety claims a term in kafala, explain the distinction from iqrar, and evaluate the opposing arguments of al-Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
In kafala, the surety's word is taken because he only undertook a specific type of claim.

This lesson examines a specific dispute in kafala (suretyship): when the surety claims he guaranteed the debt with a deferred term, but the creditor insists it was immediate payment. The Hanafi ruling is that the surety is believed with his oath, contrary to the rule in iqrar (acknowledgment of debt) where the creditor's word prevails. We will explore the reasoning behind this distinction, the objections from al-Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf, and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Dispute Context
Read the passage to identify the specific scenario: the surety claims he guaranteed the debt with a term (e.g., one month), but the creditor claims immediate payment. State the Hanafi ruling: the surety's word is taken with his oath.
2
Compare with Iqrar (Acknowledgment of Debt)
Contrast the ruling in kafala with the analogous scenario in iqrar: if someone acknowledges a debt as deferred but the creditor says it is immediate, the creditor's word is taken. Understand why the rule differs.
In iqrar, the default presumption for debts is immediate payment.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Distinction
The commentator explains that in iqrar, the debt is presumed immediate because it arises from transactions typically requiring immediate exchange. In kafala, the surety does not acknowledge a debt per se, but a right to claim after a specified time. The undertaking of claim is of a specific type (immediate or deferred).
Kafala is a commitment to the claim, not the debt itself.
4
Examine the Opposing Views
Identify the positions of al-Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf. Al-Shafi'i treats deferment as a description, so the acknowledger's word should be taken in both cases. Abu Yusuf analogizes to option (khiyar): if someone acknowledges kafala with an option, the option is void, so the surety should not be believed about the term.
These objections challenge the distinction between iqrar and kafala.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Response
The commentator refutes Abu Yusuf by showing that deferment does not void the kafala, unlike option which invalidates the contract. Therefore, the surety can be believed about the term without contradicting his acknowledgment.
The distinction between iqrar and kafala is upheld on the basis of the nature of the obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال : ضمنته إلى شهر صُدَّقَ هو ، وإن ادعى الطالبُ أَنَّه حال
أي قال الكفيل : كفلتُ بهذا المال لكن المطالبة بعد شهر، وقال الطالب: لا بل على صفة الحلول، فالقولُ قولُ الكفيل مع الحلف، وهذا بخلاف ما إذا أقر بدين مؤجل ، وقال المقر له : لا بل هو حال، فالقول للمقر له.
(1) قوله : والفرق ...الخ ; توضيحه : إن المقرَّ بالدين أقر بما هو سبب المطالبة في الحال، إذ الظاهر أن الدين كذلك ; لأنه إنما يثبت بدلاً عن قرض أو إتلاف أو بيع ونحوه ، والظاهر أن العاقل لا يرضى بخروج مستحقه في الحال إلا ببدل في الحال، فكان الحلول الأصل، والأجل عارض، فكان الدين المؤجَّلُ معروضاً بعارض لا نوعاً، ثم ادعى لنفسه حقاً، وهو تأخير المطالبة والآخر ينكره. وفي الكفالة ما أقرّ بالدين على ما هو الأصح ، بل بحق المطالبة بعد شهر ، والمكفول له يدعيها في الحال، والكفيل ينكر ذلك ، فالقولُ له ; وهذا لأنّ التزام المطالبة يتنوع إلى التزامها في الحال، أو في المستقبل كالكفالة بما ذاب أو بالدَّرَك ، فإنّما أقر بنوع منها ، فلا يلزم بالنوع الآخر، كذا في «الفتح».
وقال الشافعي : القول للمقر فيهما ; لأن الأجل وصف فيهما ، يقال : دين مؤجل وحال، وفي الأوصاف القولُ للمقر ، وقال أبو يوسف : القولُ للمقر له في الفصلين رواه عنه إبراهيم بن يوسف الله ; لأن المقر قد أقر له بحق ، ثم ادعى تأخيره فلا يُصَدَّقُ إلا بحجة ; لأنه ادَّعى أن له على صاحبه حقا وهو التأخير، ألا ترى أنه لو أقرّ بالكفالة على أنه بالخيار جازَ إقراره بالكفالة وبطل الخيار لما قلنا.
ونحن بينا الفرق بين الفصلين وليس هذا كالخيار ; لأن الخيار معنى يُبْطِلُ الكفالة فلا يُصَدَّقُ بإبطالها بعد الإقرار بخلاف الأجل ; لأنه ليس بإبطال، وإنما هو نوع في
And if he says: I guaranteed it until a month, he is believed, and if the claimant claims that it is immediate. Meaning: the surety says: I guaranteed this money but the claim is after a month, and the claimant says: no, it is immediate, then the word is the surety's with his oath. This is unlike if he acknowledged a deferred debt, and the acknowledgee says: no, it is immediate, then the word is the acknowledgee's. (1) His statement: 'the difference...' etc.; its clarification: Indeed, the one who acknowledges a debt admits to what is the cause of immediate claim, because the apparent is that the debt is so; since it is established as compensation for a loan, destruction, sale, etc., and the apparent is that a rational person would not consent to the loss of his due immediately except for an immediate substitute. Thus immediacy is the original, and deferment is an accident. So a deferred debt is a debt affected by an accident, not a different type. Then he claims a right for himself, which is the delay of claim, but the other denies it. In kafala, he did not acknowledge the debt according to the soundest view, but rather the right of claim after a month, and the guaranteed creditor claims it immediately, and the surety denies that, so his word is taken. This is because the undertaking of claim varies into undertaking it immediately or in the future, like kafala for what is lost or for the defect. So he admitted to one type and is not bound by the other. As in al-Fath. Al-Shafi'i said: the word is the acknowledger's in both cases because deferment is a description in both; it is said: deferred and immediate debt, and in descriptions the word is the acknowledger's. Abu Yusuf said: the word is the acknowledgee's in both cases, narrated from him by Ibrahim ibn Yusuf, because the acknowledger has acknowledged a right, then claims its delay, so he is not believed without proof because he claims a right over the other (i.e., the delay). Do you not see that if he acknowledged kafala with an option, his acknowledgment of kafala is valid and the option is void by what we said. And we have explained the difference between the two cases, and this is not like the option because the option is a meaning that invalidates kafala, so he is not believed in voiding it after acknowledgment, unlike deferment because it is not voiding, but rather a type of commission.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage discusses a ruling in kafala: if the surety claims that he guaranteed the debt with a one-month term, but the creditor says it was immediate, the surety's word is taken with oath. This differs from the case of iqrar (acknowledgment of debt): if someone acknowledges a debt as deferred, but the creditor says it is immediate, the creditor's word is taken. The commentator explains the difference: in iqrar, the debt is presumed to be immediate because it arises from transactions that are typically immediate. The acknowledgment of a deferred debt is an exception to the norm, so the claimant's word prevails. In kafala, the surety did not acknowledge a debt per se, but rather a right of claim after a month. The right of claim can be immediate or deferred, so the surety only undertook a specific type of claim. Therefore, his word about the term is accepted. The passage then notes that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees and says the acknowledger's word should be taken in both cases because deferment is a description. Within the Hanafi school, Abu Yusuf also disagrees, holding that the acknowledgee's word is taken in both cases, analogizing kafala with option (khiyar). The commentator refutes this by showing that deferment does not invalidate the kafala, unlike option, so the analogy is not valid.

Key Terms
حلول (hulul): Immediate payment; opposite of deferment (ajal).
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a right or debt; a unilateral admission that creates a binding obligation.
كفالة (kafala): Suretyship; a contract where a person guarantees the appearance of the debtor or payment of a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The surety is believed with his oath.
B) The creditor is believed.
C) The one with witnesses.
D) The qadi decides discretionarily.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the surety's word is taken regarding the term of payment in kafala because he undertook a specific type of claim, unlike in iqrar where the debt is presumed immediate.

Recourse Between Mutual Sureties: The Half-Excess Rule
Ruling and commentary on mutual kafala between two co-debtors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on recourse between mutual sureties and apply it to cases where debts differ in description or cause.

Core Idea
A co-surety can only recover from the other the amount paid in excess of half the joint debt.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a key ruling on mutual kafala. When two people are co-debtors and also sureties for each other, the payment allocation is critical. The rule hinges on the dual capacity of each person: each is primarily liable for his own half and only secondarily liable as surety for the other's half. Understanding this distinction prevents incorrect recourse claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
The matn states: 'Each surety does not have recourse against his partner except for what he paid in excess of half.' This is the fundamental principle.
This rule applies when debts are equal in description and cause.
2
Understand the dual capacity
Each person is both original debtor (asil) for his own half and surety (kafil) for the other's half. Payment first satisfies his own liability.
Thus, if A pays 800 on a 1000 joint debt, 500 is A's share, 300 is excess as surety.
3
Examine the condition of equal debts
The commentary notes that the default rule assumes debts equal in description (e.g., both immediate) and cause (e.g., both from sale). If they differ, allocation changes.
Difference in description: one deferred, one immediate. Difference in cause: loan vs. sale price.
4
Apply the rule to differing debts
If debts differ in cause, intention (niyya) matters. The payer can allocate payment to the partner's debt. If debts differ in description, early payment cannot be recovered before maturity.
Intention is valid across different types (jins) but futile within the same type.
5
Consider non-mutual suretyship
If only one is surety for the other (not reciprocal), and he pays, he is believed if he claims the payment was for the principal's share.
This is based on trust and the surety's own admission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في كفالة الرجلين (دين على اثنين كفل كل عن الآخر، لم يرجع على شريكه إلا بما أدى زائداً على النصف) اشتريا عبداً بألف وكفل كل منهما عن صاحبه بأمره للبائع، فكل ما أداه أحدهما لا يرجع به على صاحبه إلا أن يكون زائداً على النصف. وحاصل المسألة: أنه إذا كان الدين على اثنين وكفل كل واحد منهما عن الآخر جاز العقد، وما أداه أحدهما من الدين لا يرجع على الآخر إلا إذا زاد على النصف، فينصرف إلى ما عليه كفالة، فيرجع على شريكه إن كفل بأمره. وفي قوله: دين على اثنين؛ إشارة في استواء الدينين صفة وسبباً، فلو اختلفا صفة كان ما على أحدهما مؤجلاً وما على صاحبه حالاً، فإن أدى صح تعينه على شريكه، ورجع به عليه، وعلى عكسه لا يرجع؛ لأن الكفيل إذا عجل ديناً مؤجلاً ليس له الرجوع على الأصيل قبل الحلول. ولو اختلفا سبباً كإن كان على أحدهما فرضاً وما على الآخر ثمن مبيع، فإنه يصح تعيين المؤدى؛ لأن النية في الجنسين المختلفين معتبرة، وفي الجنس الواحد لغو. ولو كفل أحدهما عن صاحبه دون الآخر، وأدى الكفيل فجعله عن صاحبه، فإنه يصدق.
Chapter on the suretyship of two men: (A debt owed by two [persons], each having stood surety for the other, he does not have recourse against his partner except for what he paid in excess of half.) They bought a slave for one thousand and each stood surety for the other by the seller's order, so whatever one pays, he does not have recourse against his partner except if it exceeds half. The summary: When the debt is on two and each stands surety for the other, the contract is valid. Whatever one pays of the debt, he does not have recourse against the other unless it exceeds half; then it is attributed to the suretyship he owes, so he has recourse against his partner if he stood surety by his order. In his statement 'debt on two', there is an indication of equality in description and cause. If they differ in description—one's debt being deferred and the other's immediate—if he pays, his allocation to his partner is valid and he has recourse; but in the opposite case he does not have recourse because the surety if he pays a deferred debt early has no recourse against the principal before maturity. If they differ in cause—e.g., one owes a loan (qard) and the other the price of a sale—then allocation of payment [by intention] is valid because intention in different categories is considered, but in the same category it is futile. If one stands surety for the other without the other reciprocating, and the surety pays and attributes it to the principal, he is believed.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on kafala, specifically the case of two persons who are co-debtors and who have also stood surety for each other. The base text (matn) states the core rule: each surety can only have recourse against the other co-surety for the amount paid in excess of half the total debt. The commentator (sharh) expands by explaining the example of buying a slave jointly for 1000 dirhams, with each person being surety for the other. The key principle is that each person is primarily liable for his own half (as original debtor) and liable as surety for the other's half. When one pays, the payment is first applied to his own liability (half), and only if he pays more than half does the excess become a payment on his suretyship obligation, allowing recourse. The commentator then discusses conditions: if the two debts differ in maturity or cause, the allocation of payment can be valid or invalid based on intention. For differing causes (e.g., a loan vs. a sale price), intention matters; for the same cause, intention is futile. Also, if only one is surety for the other, and he pays, he is believed if he claims he paid for the principal's share. This is all Hanafi fiqh as transmitted in the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafala): A contract of suretyship where a person (kafil) guarantees the debt or obligation of another (asil).
أصيل (asil): The principal debtor whose debt is guaranteed by the surety.
كفيل (kafil): The surety who guarantees the debt of the asil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two persons buy a slave jointly for 1000 dirhams, and each stands surety for the other. One pays 800 dirhams. How much can he recover from his co-surety?
A) 800 dirhams
B) 400 dirhams
C) 300 dirhams
D) 0 dirhams

Key Takeaway
In mutual kafala, each surety's payment first goes to his own half; only the excess can be recovered from the co-surety.

Recourse Between Co-Sureties for a Third Party's Debt
Ruling on recourse between co-sureties with mutual kafala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recourse between mutual co-sureties for a third party's debt and explain the qiyas basis for the equal split.

Core Idea
When two sureties stand surety for each other for the same debt, each is liable for half — equality of guarantee overrides any disparity in payment.

This lesson examines a specific case of mutual suretyship where two individuals guarantee a third party's debt and also guarantee each other. The Hanafi ruling establishes that the paying surety recovers half from his co-surety, regardless of the payment amount. Understanding the reasoning behind this equality is crucial for distinguishing it from other surety scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that there are two sureties for the principal debtor, and then each surety also stands surety for the other (mutual kafala). This creates a unique relationship distinct from cases where one is both debtor and surety.
The mutual suretyship is valid by Hanafi fiqh.
2
State the ruling
When one surety pays any amount of the debt, he can recover half of that amount from the co-surety. This applies even if the payment is small.
The rule is based on equality of cause.
3
Identify the proof (dalil)
The proof is qiyas: both sureties are equal in the cause (illa) of guarantee (daman al-kafala). Since their liability arises from the same type of contract, the payment is shared equally.
The illa is the guarantee inherent in the suretyship contract.
4
Contrast with the first case
In the first case (not fully detailed here), one person is both a principal debtor and a surety. There, principality outweighs suretyship, so the recourse is different. Here, both are purely sureties, so equality applies.
Understand the difference to avoid confusion.
5
Apply the rule
Given a specific payment amount, calculate the recourse as half. Then the paying surety can also seek full reimbursement from the principal debtor, jointly with the co-surety or alone.
The co-surety is not liable for the full amount, only half.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كفلا بشيء عن رجلٍ، وكفل كل به عن صاحبه رجع عليه بنصف ما أدى وإن قل. على رجل ألف فكفل كل واحد من شخصين آخرين عن الأصيل بهذا الألف، ثم كفل كل واحد من الكفيلين عن صاحبه بأمره بهذا الألف، فكل ما أداه أحدهما وإن قل رجع على الآخر بنصفه بخلاف الصورة الأولى، فإن الأصالة ترجح على الكفالة. توضيحه: أنه إذا كان على رجل دين ألف درهم مثلاً، فكفل عنه رجلان كل واحد منهما بجميعه على الانفراد، ثم كفل كل واحد من الرجلين عن صاحبه بما لزمه إذ الكفالة عن الكفيل جائزة، كما تجوز عن الأصيل. فكل ما أدى أحدهما رجع بنصفه على صاحبه، ثم يرجعان على الأصيل إن شاء، وإن شاء رجع هو بالكل على الأصيل؛ لأن ما عليهما مستويان، فلا ترجيح للبعض على البعض إذ الكل كفالة، فيكون المؤدى شائعاً عنهما، ويرجع بنصفه على شريكه إذ لا يؤدي إلى الدور؛ لأن قضية عقد الكفالة الاستواء لاستوائهما في العلة، وهي ضمان الكفالة. فلما كان كذلك كان لمن أدى ولاية الرجوع على صاحبه بنصف ما أدى.
And if they stood surety for something on behalf of a man, and each stood surety thereby for his partner, he has recourse against him for half of what he paid, even if it is little. (Explanation:) A man owes one thousand, so two other persons each stand surety for the principal for this thousand, then each of the two sureties stands surety for his partner by his order for this thousand. So whatever one pays, even if little, he has recourse against the other for half of it, contrary to the first case, because principality outweighs suretyship. Clarification: When a man owes a debt of one thousand dirhams, for example, and two men stand surety for him, each for the entire amount individually, then each of the two men stands surety for his partner for what is incumbent on him, because suretyship by a surety is permissible just as it is permissible for the principal. So whatever one pays, he has recourse for half against his partner; then they both may have recourse against the principal if they wish, or the one who paid may have recourse for the whole against the principal because what they owe is equal, so no part is preferred over another since it is all suretyship; thus the payment is shared between them, and he has recourse for half against his partner because it does not lead to circularity; for the implication of the suretyship contract is equality because they are equal in the cause, which is the guarantee of suretyship. Therefore, the one who pays has the authority to have recourse against his partner for half of what he paid.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This second ruling presents a distinct scenario from the first. Here, two individuals act as sureties for a debt owed by a third person (the principal debtor). Then, each of these sureties additionally stands surety for the other (i.e., mutual kafala between the sureties for the same debt). The ruling states that when one surety pays any amount towards the debt, he can recover half of that amount from the other co-surety, regardless of how small the payment is. This differs from the first case because in the first case each person was both a principal debtor and a surety; here both are purely sureties. The commentator explains the reasoning: since both are equally sureties, the payment is considered shared between them; each is liable for half. Therefore, the paying surety has recourse for half. They can then both seek reimbursement from the original debtor. The cause ('illa) for equality is that both are equally liable as sureties; unlike the first case where the original debtorship outweighed the suretyship, here there is no original debtor role to create priority. This is a clear Hanafi position based on the principle that suretyship implies equality among co-sureties.

Key Terms
كفالة عن الكفيل (kafala 'an al-kafil): Suretyship by one surety for another surety; the text confirms it is permissible.
ضمان الكفالة (daman al-kafala): The guarantee inherent in the suretyship contract; the cause for equal liability among co-sureties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Zayd owes 1000 dirhams to a seller. 'Amr and Bakr each stand surety for Zayd for the full amount. Then 'Amr and Bakr also stand surety for each other. 'Amr pays 600 dirhams to the seller. How much can 'Amr recover from Bakr?
A) 600 dirhams
B) 300 dirhams
C) 100 dirhams
D) 0 dirhams

Key Takeaway
When two co-sureties mutually guarantee each other for a third party's debt, the paying surety recovers half from the other, based on equality of the cause of guarantee.

Introduction to Hawalah: Definition, Evidence, and Conditions
Definition, legal basis, and initial conditions of hawalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hawalah, identify its evidentiary basis from the hadith, and list the conditions (sanity and consent) required for the transferor.

Core Idea
Hawalah transfers debt; it is valid only if the transferor is sane and consents freely.

This lesson opens the Book of Hawalah with its definition and legal foundation. You will learn how hawalah relates to kafalah, its precise technical meaning, and the Prophetic hadith that establishes its permissibility. We then extract the initial conditions that the transferor must meet for the contract to be valid. Understanding these basics is critical before moving to detailed rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its definition
Notice that hawalah linguistically means transfer; technically it is 'transfer of debt from one liability to another'. Name the parties: muhil (transferor), muhtal (creditor), muhtal alayh (transferee), and the debt itself (muhal bih).
The definition is specific to debt; it does not include transfer of physical property.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith 'Whoever is referred to a solvent person should accept the referral' is cited, along with consensus. The imperative 'falyattabi' (should accept) indicates permissibility according to Hanafi usul.
The hadith is recorded by al-Tabarani; other versions exist in Muslim.
3
Explain the wajh al-dalalah
In Hanafi usul, a command after a prohibition indicates permissibility, not obligation. Here the command is to accept the referral, so hawalah is ja'iz (permissible).
Compare with Shafi'i usul where a command generally indicates obligation; difference in usul leads to same conclusion? Actually both schools accept it as permissible.
4
Extract the conditions for the transferor (muhil)
The passage states sanity (aql) is required, and consent (rida) is required. Insanity or coercion invalidates the hawalah. Puberty (bulugh) is a condition for enforceability (nufadh), not basic validity.
A sane minor's hawalah is suspended until guardian approval (the passage cuts off here; continued elsewhere).
5
Recognize the parties and terms
Memorize the four terms: muhil, muhtal (or muhal lahu), muhtal alayh (muhal alayh), and muhal bih. These will recur throughout the book.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : كتاب الحوالة ؛ لما فرغ المصنِّفُ الله عن مسائل الكفالة شرع في كتاب الحوالة بمناسبة أن كلاً من الحوالة والكفالة عقد التزام ما على الأصيل المتوثق إلا أن الحوالة تتضمَّنُ إبراء الأصيل إبراء مقيداً كما ستعرف، فكانت كالمركب مع الفرد، والمفرد يقتضي التقدم ، فلزم تأخير الحوالة ؛ ليوافق الوضع الطبع.
وأيضاً الكفالة كما تتعلق بالدين تتعلق بالعين، فكانت أعم أحكاماً بخلاف الحوالة، فإنها تختص بالدين ؛ فلذا قدمت ا الكفالة . على الحوالة. وقال في «المصباح المنير») : تحول عن المكان : انتقل عنه وحولته تحويلاً ، نقله من موضع إلى موضع، وحوّل هو تحويلاً يستعمل لازماً ومتعدياً، وحولت الرداء نقلة كل طرف إلى موضع الآخر، والحوالة مأخوذة من هذا فأحلته بدينه نقلته إلى ذمّة غير ذمتك، وأحلتُ الشيء إحالة نقلته أيضاً. انتهى. ولذا قال في «المغرب» (۲) : تركيب الحوالة يدلُّ على الزوال والنقل، ومنه التحويل، وهو نقل الشيء من محل إلى محل.
وهو في اصطلاح الفقهاء : نقل الدين من ذمّةٍ إلى ذمّة. كما ذكره الشارح . وفي عرفهم يقال : المديون: محيلاً، والدائن : محتالاً، ومحتالاً له، ومحالاً له، ومن يقبلُ الحوالة : محتالاً عليه ومحالاً عليه، والمال محالاً به.
والحوالة مشروعة : بالإجماع ، وقال النبي : من أحيل على مليء فليتبع»، رواه الطبراني (۳) عن الأعرج عن أبي هريرة له ، والأمر بالاتباع دليل الجواز؛ ولأنه التزام ما يقدر على تسليمه، فوجب القولُ بصحته دفعاً للحاجة.
وشرط صحتها :
في المحيل : العقل ؛ فلا تصح حواله مجهول وصبي لا يعقل، والرضا فلا تصح حوالة المكْرَه ، وأما البلوغ فشرطُ النفاذ، فصحة حوالة الصبي العاقل موقوفة على
He said: 'Book of Hawalah.' When the author finished with issues of kafalah, he began the book of hawalah due to the connection that both hawalah and kafalah are contracts of obligation on the original guarantor, except that hawalah includes a restricted discharge of the original debtor, as you will know. So it is like a compound with a simple, and the simple requires precedence, thus hawalah is delayed to suit the natural order. Also, kafalah relates to both debt and specific property, so it is more comprehensive in rulings, unlike hawalah which is specific to debt, therefore kafalah was placed before hawalah. He said in al-Misbah al-Munir: 'tahawwala' from place: he moved from it; 'hawwaltuhu' I moved it from one place to another; 'tahawwala' himself moves, used intransitively and transitively; 'hawwaltu al-rida'a' I moved each end to the other's place; 'hawalah' is derived from this: 'ahaltuhu bi daynihi' I transferred him with his debt to another's liability; 'ahaltu al-shay'a' I transferred it also. End. Thus he said in al-Mughrib: the root of 'hawalah' indicates removal and transfer, from it is 'tahwil' which is transferring something from one place to another. In the terminology of jurists, it is the transfer of debt from one liability to another, as the commentator mentioned. In their custom, the debtor is called 'muhil', the creditor 'muhtal' or 'muhtal lahu' or 'muhal lahu', the one who accepts the transfer is 'muhtal alayh' or 'muhal alayh', and the property is 'muhal bihi'. Hawalah is legislated by consensus. The Prophet said: 'Whoever is referred to a solvent person should accept the referral.' Recorded by al-Tabarani from al-A'raj from Abu Hurayra. The command to follow indicates permissibility. And because it is an undertaking of something one can deliver, so it is necessarily considered valid to meet a need. Conditions of its validity: in the transferor (muhil): sanity; so the hawalah of an insane person or a child who lacks reason is not valid. Also consent; so the hawalah of a coerced person is not valid. As for puberty, it is a condition for enforceability, so the validity of a sane minor's hawalah is suspended on...
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage introduces the Book of Hawalah (Transfer of Debt). The commentator explains why hawalah follows kafalah: both are contracts obligating the original debtor, but hawalah includes a restricted discharge of the original debtor, making it more complex, so the simpler kafalah comes first. Also, kafalah covers debts and specific property, while hawalah only debts, so kafalah is more comprehensive. The definition is given linguistically and technically: linguistically, it means transfer from one place to another; technically, it is the transfer of a debt from one person's liability to another. The parties are named: transferor (muhil), creditor (muhtal), and transferee (muhtal alayh). The legality of hawalah is established by consensus and by a hadith: 'Whoever is referred to a solvent person should accept the referral' — the imperative indicates permissibility. Additionally, it meets a need (transfer of debt for convenience). The conditions for validity regarding the transferor (muhil) are: sanity (not insane or minor lacking reason) and consent (not coerced). Puberty is a condition for enforceability (nufadh), not for the contract's basic validity, so a sane minor's hawalah is suspended pending guardian approval. The passage cuts off at this point, continuing on the next page.

Key Terms
حوالة (ḥawālah): Transfer of a debt from one person's liability to another's.
محيل (muḥīl): The transferor, i.e., the original debtor who transfers the debt to another.
محتال / محال له (muḥtāl / muḥāl lahu): The creditor who accepts the transfer.
محتال عليه / محال عليه (muḥtāl ʿalayh / muḥāl ʿalayh): The transferee, i.e., the one who assumes the debt obligation.
محال به (muḥāl bihī): The debt itself that is transferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minimum condition for validity of hawalah concerning the transferor's mental state?
A) He must be an adult.
B) He must be sane (ʿāqil).
C) He must be a free person.
D) He must be the debtor of the transferee.

Key Takeaway
Hawalah is a permissible contract transferring a debt, valid only when the transferor is sane and consents freely.

The Extent of Bara'ah in Hawalah: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad al-Shaybani
Presents the ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the extent of bara'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the extent of bara'ah in hawalah and apply its thamarah to practical cases involving release of debt and pawn transactions.

Core Idea
In hawalah, the muhil is freed from the claim only, not the debt — the debt remains contingently.

The hawalah contract transfers a debt to a new debtor, but the exact nature of the original debtor's freedom (bara'ah) is contested within the Hanafi school. This lesson explores the difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on whether the muhil is freed from both the debt and the claim or only from the claim. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for determining the validity of subsequent releases and the status of collateral.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify the exact views of Abu Yusuf (freed from debt and claim) and Muhammad (freed from claim only). Note that they agree on the validity of hawalah.
The dispute is only about the extent of bara'ah.
2
Identify the thamarah (practical consequences)
Analyze the two cases given: (1) the muhtal releases the muhil, and (2) the pawner transfers the mortgagee's debt. Determine how each position affects these scenarios.
These cases illustrate the real-world difference.
3
Follow the implications from the author's statement
The text derives further implications: if the muhil is freed (per the author's view), then in analogous situations (buyer-seller, mortgagee, marriage dower) the right of detention is lost. Understand the logic behind these implications.
These implications assume Muhammad's view or a similar partial bara'ah.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and the tarjih reason
Later Hanafi jurists preferred Muhammad's view due to caution (ihtiyat) — it preserves the possibility of recourse if the new debtor defaults. This is the operative position for fatwa.
The tarjih authority includes al-Marghinani and others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ اخْتَلَفُوا فِي الْبَرَاءَةِ؛ فَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ: يَبْرَأُ عَنِ الدَّيْنِ وَالْمُطَالَبَةِ، وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ: يَبْرَأُ عَنِ الْمُطَالَبَةِ فَقَطْ، وَلَا يَبْرَأُ عَنِ الدَّيْنِ، وَثَمَرَةُ الْخِلَافِ تَظْهَرُ فِي مَوْضِعَيْنِ:
الْأَوَّلُ: إِذَا أَبْرَأَ الْمُحْتَالُ الْمُحِيلَ مِنَ الدَّيْنِ، فَعِنْدَ الْأَوَّلِ لَا يَصِحُّ، وَعِنْدَ الثَّانِي يَصِحُّ.
وَالْثَّانِي: إِنَّ الرَّاهِنَ إِذَا أَحَالَ الْمُرْتَهِنَ بِالدَّيْنِ عَلَى إِنْسَانٍ كَانَ لِلرَّاهِنِ أَنْ يَسْتَرِدَّ الرَّهْنَ عِنْدَ الْأَوَّلِ، كَمَا لَوْ أَبْرَأَهُ مِنَ الدَّيْنِ، وَلَيْسَ لَهُ ذَلِكَ عِنْدَ الثَّانِي، كَمَا لَوْ أَجَّلَ الدَّيْنَ.
وَمُقْتَضَى مَا ذَكَرَهُ الْمُصَنِّفُ مِنْ بَرَاءَةِ الْمُحِيلِ أَنَّ الْمُشْتَرِيَ لَوْ أَحَالَ الْبَائِعَ بِالثَّمَنِ عَلَى أَجَلٍ لَمْ يَمْلِكْ حَبْسَ الْمَبِيعِ، وَكَذَا لَوْ أَحَالَ الْمُرْتَهِنَ الرَّهْنَ لَا يَحْبِسُ، وَلَوْ أَحَالَ الزَّوْجُ الْمَرْأَةَ بِصَدَاقِهَا لَمْ تَحْبِسْ نَفْسَهَا، بِخِلَافِ الْعَكْسِ فِي الثَّلَاثَةِ، صَرَّحَ بِهِ فِي «الْبَحْرِ» وَغَيْرِهِ.
Then they differed regarding the bara'ah: Abu Yusuf said: He is freed from the debt and the claim; Muhammad said: He is freed from the claim only, and not freed from the debt. The fruit of the difference appears in two places: First: If the muhtal releases the muhil from the debt, then according to the first it is not valid, and according to the second it is valid. Second: If the pawner transfers the debt of the mortgagee to a person, then according to the first the pawner may take back the pawn, just as if he were released from the debt; according to the second he does not have that, just as if he deferred the debt. And the implication of what the author mentioned regarding the bara'ah of the muhil is that if the buyer transfers the price to the seller on credit, he does not have the right to detain the sold item; similarly if the mortgagee transfers the pawn, he does not detain; and if the husband transfers the dower of the wife, she does not detain herself, contrary to the reverse in these three cases, as stated in 'al-Bahr' and others.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the precise nature of bara'ah (freedom) in hawalah. After establishing that the muhil is freed, the text presents a difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Yusuf holds that the muhil is freed from both the debt itself (al-dayn) and the claim (al-mutalaba). Muhammad holds that the muhil is freed only from the claim, meaning the creditor cannot demand payment from him, but the debt remains in his liability, so if the new debtor fails to pay, the muhil may still be liable? Actually the text says 'freed from claim only', implying that the debt remains in his liability, but in practice the creditor cannot sue him except in case of tawa. The practical difference (thamara) appears in two cases: (1) If the muhtal (creditor) voluntarily releases the muhil from the debt, according to Abu Yusuf this release is invalid because the muhil has already been freed from the debt; according to Muhammad it is valid because the debt still exists in the muhil's liability. (2) If a pawner (rahn) transfers the mortgagee's debt to a third party, according to Abu Yusuf the pawner can take back the pawn because the debt to the mortgagee is extinguished; according to Muhammad, the debt remains, so the pawner cannot take back the pawn. The text then derives implications from the author's (al-Marghinani's) statement that the muhil is freed: if the buyer transfers the price to the seller on a deferred basis, the seller cannot detain the sold item (because he has been paid by the new debtor, and the original debt is gone); similarly in other cases. The reverse situations (where the creditor transfers the right to another) are different.

Key Terms
المطالبة (al-mutalaba): The right of the creditor to demand payment from the debtor.
البراءة (al-bara'ah): Freedom from debt or claim; discussed here in the context of its extent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the practical consequence (thamara) of the ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the pawner (rahin)?
A) According to Abu Yusuf, the pawner may take back the pawn; according to Muhammad, he may not.
B) According to Abu Yusuf, the pawner may not take back the pawn; according to Muhammad, he may.
C) Both agree the pawner may take back the pawn.
D) Both agree the pawner may not take back the pawn.

Key Takeaway
In hawalah, the muhil is freed only from the claim, not the debt, per the mufta bihi view of Muhammad, allowing the creditor to still release him and the pawner cannot retrieve his pawn.

Conditions for Reverting to the Original Debtor in Hawalah
Ruling statement with expansions on reversion rights in hawalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule: the muhtal does not revert to the muhil except in cases of tawa, khiyar, or faskh, and apply it to distinguish between valid and invalid reversion.

Core Idea
In hawalah, the creditor cannot go back to the original debtor unless the new debtor becomes insolvent or an option was reserved.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary clarifies an important exception to the binding nature of hawalah. While the default rule is that the original debtor is freed from the debt, the text specifies conditions under which the creditor may revert. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for properly structuring hawalah contracts and knowing the rights of the parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Read the matn: 'the muhtal does not revert to him'. This is the default: the original debtor is free from claim after a valid hawalah.
2
Examine the Exception: Tawa
The matn says 'except when his right perishes (tawa)'. Tawa means the new debtor cannot pay. The Hanafi school allows reversion in this case.
Contrast with Shafi'i who do not allow.
3
Explore Additional Exceptions from Commentary
The commentary adds two more exceptions: khiyar (if the creditor stipulated the right to choose whom to pursue) and faskh (if the parties rescind the hawalah). Also, a subsequent hawalah can cancel the first (naqd).
These are expansions of the matn.
4
Note the Cross-Madhhab Difference
Al-Shafi'i holds that even with tawa, the creditor cannot revert. This highlights the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) between the schools.
5
Apply the Rule to a Scenario
Given a case: A owes B, A transfers B to C via hawalah. C becomes insolvent. Under Hanafi, B can go back to A. Under Shafi'i, B cannot. Identify which exceptions apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَمْ يَرْجِعْ عَلَيْهِ الْمُحْتَالُ، إِلَّا إِذَا تَوَى حَقُّهُ.
[١] قَوْلُهُ: وَلَمْ يَرْجِعْ...الْخَ؛ هَذَا إِذْ لَمْ يُشْتَرَطِ الْخِيَارُ لِلْمُحَالِ، أَوْ لَمْ يَفْسَخْهَا الْمُحِيلُ وَالْمُحْتَالُ، أَمَّا إِذَا جُعِلَ لِلْمُحَالِ الْخِيَارُ، أَوْ أُحِيلَ عَلَى أَنَّ لَهُ أَنْ يَرْجِعَ عَلَى أَيِّهِمَا شَاءَ صَحَّ، كَذَا فِي «الْبَزَّازِيَّةِ».
وَكَذَا إِذَا فُسِخَتْ رَجَعَ الْمُحْتَالُ عَلَى الْمُحِيلِ بِدَيْنِهِ؛ وَلِذَا قَالَ فِي «الْبَدَائِعِ»: إِنَّ حُكْمَهُمَا يَنْتَهِي بِفَسْخِهَا وَبِالتَّوَى. وَفِي «الْبَزَّازِيَّةِ»: الْمُحِيلُ وَالْمُحْتَالُ يَمْلِكُ النَّقْضَ، فَيَبْرَأُ الْمُحْتَالُ عَلَيْهِ.
وَفِي «الذَّخِيرَةِ»: إِذَا أَحَالَ الْمَدْيُونُ الطَّالِبَ عَلَى رَجُلٍ بِأَلْفٍ أَوْ بِجَمِيعِ حَقِّهِ وَقَبِلَ ثُمَّ أَحَالَهُ بِجَمِيعِ حَقِّهِ عَلَى آخَرَ، وَقَبِلَ مِنْهُ صَارَ الثَّانِي نَقْضًا لِلْأَوَّلِ وَبَرِئَ الْأَوَّلُ. كَذَا فِي «الْبَحْرِ».
[٢] قَوْلُهُ: إِلَّا إِذَا تَوَى حَقُّهُ؛ وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ: لَا يَرْجِعُ عَلَيْهِ إِنْ تَوَى حَقُّهُ.
And the muhtal does not revert to him (the muhil) except when his right perishes (tawa). [1] His saying: 'and does not revert... etc.'; this is when no option (khiyar) is stipulated for the muhal, or the muhil and muhtal have not rescinded. But if an option is made for the muhal, or the hawalah is made on the condition that he may revert to whichever of them he wishes, it is valid, as in al-Bazzaziyya. Similarly, if it is rescinded, the muhtal reverts to the muhil for his debt; therefore the author of al-Bada'i' said: Their ruling ends with rescission and with tawa. In al-Bazzaziyya: The muhil and muhtal have the right to cancel, so the muhtal 'alayh becomes free. In al-Dhakhira: If the debtor transfers the creditor to a person for a thousand or for all his right, and he accepts, then transfers him for all his right to another, and he accepts from him, then the second becomes a cancellation of the first and the first becomes free. So in al-Bahr. [2] His saying: 'except when his right perishes'; al-Shafi'i said: He does not revert to him even if his right perishes.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about the conditions under which the creditor (muhtal) may revert to the original debtor (muhil). The matn states that the muhtal does not revert to the muhil except when his right perishes (tawa, i.e., the new debtor becomes insolvent). The commentary explains that this ruling applies when no option (khiyar) has been stipulated for the creditor (muhal) and when neither the muhil nor the muhtal has rescinded the contract. If the hawalah was made with an option for the creditor to choose whom to pursue, or if it is stipulated that he may revert to either, then it is valid and he may revert. Similarly, if the hawalah is rescinded (by agreement or by a court?), the creditor can revert. The commentary cites al-Bada'i' stating that the effect of the hawalah ends by rescission or by tawa. Al-Bazzaziyya adds that both the muhil and the muhtal have the right to cancel, which frees the new debtor. Al-Dhakhira mentions a case where a subsequent hawalah cancels the first. Finally, the commentary notes a difference with al-Shafi'i, who holds that the creditor cannot revert even if the new debtor becomes insolvent. This shows the Hanafi view that tawa provides a right of recourse.

Key Terms
التوى (al-tawa): Perishing of the right; here meaning the new debtor's insolvency or inability to pay.
الخيار (al-khiyar): An option stipulated in the hawalah contract allowing the creditor to choose whom to pursue.
النقض (al-naqd): Cancellation of the hawalah, either by mutual agreement or by a subsequent transfer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as stated in the text, when can the creditor (muhtal) revert to the original debtor (muhil) after a valid hawalah?
A) Anytime the creditor wishes.
B) Only if the new debtor becomes insolvent (tawa) or if an option was stipulated or the contract is rescinded.
C) Only if the original debtor agrees.
D) Never; the transfer is final.

Key Takeaway
Lām yarjiʿ al-muḥtāl ʿalayhi illā idhā tawā ḥaqquhu – The creditor does not revert except when his right perishes, or an option is stipulated, or the contract is rescinded.
