Death of Assignee Insolvent: Debt Returns to Assignor
Ruling and proof for return of debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the assignee dies insolvent and explain the proof from hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
The assignee's liability is a substitute; when it fails, the original debt revives.

This passage from the Book of Assignment addresses a critical scenario: what happens if the person you assigned a debt to (al-muhtal 'alayh) dies insolvent? The Hanafi school rules that the debt returns to the original debtor (al-muhil). The text provides a hadith from 'Uthman and a logical analogy to sale, while refuting the analogy to a usurper.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the death of the assignee (al-muhtal 'alayh) while insolvent. The ruling is that the debt returns to the dhimma of the assignor (al-muhil).
This is wajib – a mandatory legal consequence.
2
Locate the dalil
Two proofs are cited: (1) A narration from 'Uthman (radiyallahu 'anhu), both mawquf and marfu', stating that the debt returns upon the assignee's death insolvent, and the general principle 'la tawa' (no loss) on a Muslim's property. (2) Rational proof: the assignee consented only on the implicit condition of receiving payment; when that condition fails, the right reverts.
The hadith is ahad, zanni al-thubut, but sufficient for a wajib ruling.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly states the return. The principle 'la tawa' implies the assignee's right is protected. The rational proof: the assignee's dhimma is a substitute (khalaf) for the assignor's; when the substitute is lost (by death insolvent), the original obligation revives, like in sale when the sold item is lost.
The text also distinguishes from the usurper case where no substitution exists.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The text presents an opposing view: some analogize to a usurper and the usurper's usurper, where loss with one does not revert to the other. The Hanafi school rejects this analogy.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate; the Hanafi position is the relied-upon (mufta bihi).
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi proof is based on a hadith and the logical substitution relationship. The opposing analogy fails because in assignment there is a chain of substitution, unlike in usurper where the owner has an independent choice.
The tarjih authority is the text itself; it is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحوالة ما تصح به الحوالة
بموت المحتال عليه
لأنَّ الدِّينَ انتقل من ذمّتِهِ، فبرئت، فلا يعود إليه إلا بسبب جديد، فصار كالغاصب، وغاصب الغاصب إذا اختار المغصوب منه تضمين أحدٍ بما برئت ذمَّة الآخر، ثمَّ بالتَّوَى عنده لا يعود الحق على الآخر. ولنا : ما رُوي عن عثمان رضي الله عنه موقوفاً ومرفوعاً في المحتال عليه إذا مات مُفْلِساً يعود الدين إلى ذمة المحيل، وقال: «لا تَوَى على مال امرئ مسلم»؛ ولأنَّ المحتال إنما رضي بهذا النقل بشرط وصول الدين من جهة المحتال عليه بدلالة الحال، وقد فات هذا الشرط بالتَّوَى، فيعودُ حقه إلى ذمة المحيل؛ لأنّ ذمّة المحال عليه خلف عن ذمة المحيل بإحالته. فإذا فات الخلف رجع بالأصيل، كما أنَّ البيع في العرف يراد به سلامة المبيع للمشتري، وسلامته من العيب فعند فواته بالاستحقاق أو بالهلاك أو عند فواتِ وصف السلامة يرجعُ المشتري بالعوض. بخلاف الغاصب وغاصب الغاصب؛ فإنّ أحدهما ليس بخلف عن الآخر، وإنما يثبت للمالك الخيار ابتداءً بملك المغصوب من أيهما شاء، فيأخذ منه عوضهُ من غير أن يحيله عليه أحد، فلا يرجع على أحد فافترقا.
Book of Assignment (Hawalah): What makes assignment valid. [Ruling] Upon the death of the assignee (al-muhtal 'alayh) [insolvent]: Because the debt transferred from his dhimma (liability), so it became free, and it does not return to him except through a new cause, so he becomes like the usurper (ghāṣib). And the usurper of the usurper, if the owner of the usurped property chooses to hold one of them liable, the liability of the other is discharged, and then upon loss (tawa) with him, the right does not return to the other. Our proof: What is narrated from 'Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him), as a mawquf and marfu' narration, regarding the assignee if he dies insolvent: the debt returns to the dhimma of the assignor (al-muhil), and he said: 'There is no loss (tawa) upon the property of a Muslim.' And because the assignee (al-muhtal) only consented to this transfer on the condition of receiving the debt from the assignee's side, as indicated by the circumstances (dalalat al-hal), and this condition has been defeated by the loss (tawa), so his right returns to the dhimma of the assignor, because the dhimma of the assignee is a substitute (khalaf) for the dhimma of the assignor by virtue of his assignment. So when the substitute is lost, he reverts to the principal, just as in sale, by custom, it is intended that the sold item is safe for the buyer and free from defect, so upon its loss by revocation (istihqaq) or destruction (halak) or upon the loss of the attribute of safety, the buyer returns the consideration. Unlike the usurper and the usurper's usurper, for neither is a substitute for the other; rather, the owner has the option from the beginning to take ownership of the usurped property from whichever he wishes, and he takes its compensation from him without anyone having assigned him to him, so he does not revert to anyone, thus they are distinct.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Book of Assignment (Hawalah). It states the Hanafi position: if the assignee (al-muhtal 'alayh) dies insolvent, the debt returns to the assignor (al-muhil). The text first presents the opposing view (that the debt does not return, analogizing to the case of a usurper and the usurper's usurper). Then it provides the Hanafi proof: a hadith from 'Uthman (radiyallahu 'anhu) — both in mawquf and marfu' forms — that the debt returns. Additionally, the hadith 'There is no loss (tawa) upon the property of a Muslim' indicates that the assignee's right is protected. The reasoning: the assignee consented to the transfer only on the implicit condition (dalalat al-hal) that he would receive payment from the assignee; when that condition fails due to loss, he reverts to the assignor, as the assignee's liability is a substitute for the assignor's. The analogy with sale is given: if a purchased item is lost or defective, the buyer returns it for the price. The distinction from the usurper case is that there is no substitution relationship; the owner has an independent choice to hold either liable, so loss with one does not revert to the other.

Key Terms
الحوالة (al-ḥawālah): Assignment or transfer of a debt from the assignor's (muhil) liability to the assignee's (muhtal 'alayh) liability.
المحتال عليه (al-muḥtāl ʿalayh): The person on whom the debt is assigned; the one who becomes liable to pay the assignee.
التَّوَى (al-tawā): Loss, destruction, or failure to recover the debt, e.g., death of the assignee insolvent or denial under oath without proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi proof, what happens when the assignee (al-muhtal 'alayh) dies insolvent?
A) The debt is lost forever and neither the assignor nor the assignee can claim it.
B) The debt returns to the dhimma of the assignor (al-muhil).
C) The debt shifts to the heirs of the assignee.
D) The assignee's liability is transferred to a surety.

Key Takeaway
When the assignee dies insolvent, the debt returns to the assignor because the assignee's liability is a substitute, and the condition of receipt fails.

Definition of Tawa and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Its Conditions
Defines tawa and lists conditions per Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of tawa, identify the two causes according to Abu Hanifa and the third cause according to the Sahibayn, and explain the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Tawa in hawalah is determined by specific events: insolvent death or denied oath; the Sahibayn add judicial bankruptcy.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary defines the concept of tawa (loss) in the context of hawalah (debt assignment). Tawa triggers the return of the assigned debt to the assignor. Imam Abu Hanifa recognized two causes, while his two companions added a third. Understanding this intra-Hanafi difference is crucial for correctly applying the rulings of hawalah in practice.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the lexical and legal meaning of tawa
The term tawa literally means destruction. In Islamic law, it refers to the loss of the right to recover the assigned debt, realized when the assignee's financial situation makes recovery impossible.
Tawa is defined by specific events, not by mere insolvency alone.
2
List the two causes according to Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa held that tawa is established by two matters: (1) the death of the assignee insolvent, meaning he leaves no property (ayn), no debt (dayn), and no surety (kafil) sufficient to cover the assigned amount; (2) the assignee's categorical denial of the assignment under oath, with no evidence from either party.
Insolvent death requires complete lack of recoverable assets; denial must be under oath.
3
Identify the third cause added by the Sahibayn
In addition to the two causes, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani consider the judge's declaration of bankruptcy (taflis) during the assignee's lifetime as a third cause of tawa.
Taflis is a judicial announcement of insolvency.
4
Note the mufta bihi position and sub-issues
Later fatwa relied on the Sahibayn's view, accepting the third cause. The passage also discusses sub-issues: a surety may prevent classifying death as insolvent (according to al-Ziyadat); if the assignee and assignor dispute insolvency, the assignee's statement is accepted with an oath.
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view, as in Fatawa Alamgiriyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١] قوله : بموت المحتال عليه ...الخ ؛ التَّوَى على وزن الحصى، وقد يمد : وهو الهلاك، كذا في «المصباح»، وفي الشرع : أحد الأمرين عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله، وأحد الأمور الثلاثة عند صاحبيه، أما الأمران: أحدهما : موت المحتال عليه مفلساً بأن لم يترك مالاً عيناً ولا ديناً ولا كفيلاً، والمراد بالعين : ما يفي بالمحال به، وكذا يقال في الدين، ولا بد في الكفيل أن يكون كفيلاً بجميعه، فلو كفل البعض فقد توى الباقي، كما صرحوا به، والمراد بالدين ما يمكن أن يثبت في الذمة، فيشمل : النقود والمكيلات، والموزونات. والثاني : أن يجحد المحال عليه الحوالة، ويحلفُ ولا بيَّنةً للمحيل ولا المحتال على المحتال عليه ، فإنَّ هلاك دين المحتال يتحقَّقُ بكلِّ واحدٍ من الموت والحلف المذكورين. وأما الأمور الثلاثة التي يثبتُ التَّوَى بأحدِها عند صاحبيه، فالإثنان منها ما مرّ، وأما الثالث : فهو أن يفلسه القاضي : أي يحكم بإفلاسه في حياته. وقد ذكر في «الفتاوى العالمكيرية» نقلاً عن «المحيط»: ولو كان القاضي يعلمُ أنَّ للميت ديناً على مُفلس، فعلى قول الإمام لا يقضى ببطلان الحوالة. انتهى. ووجود الكفيل يمنع موته مُفْلِساً على ما في «الزيادات»، وفي «الخلاصة» لا يمنع. كذا في «المنح»، وقد جزمَ صاحب «الفتح» بما في «الزيادات» بلا ذكر خلاف. ولو اختلفا، فقال المحتال : مات مفلسا، وقال المحيل بخلافه، فالقول للمحتال مع اليمين على العلم ؛ لأنه متمسك بالأصل وهو العسرة، كما لو كان حياً وأنكر اليسر، ذكره العلامة الشمني نقلا عن «المبسوط». [ ١ ] قوله : مفلساً ؛ بالتخفيف مصدره : إفلاس، قال في «المصباح المنير» : أفلس الرجلُ كأنه صار إلى حال ليس له فلوس، كما يقال : أَقْهَرَ ؛ إذا صارَ إلى حالٍ يُقْهَرُ عليه، وبعضهم يقول : صار ذا فلوس بعد أن كان ذا دراهم ، فهو مفلس، والجمع مفاليس، وحقيقة الانتقال من حالة اليسر إلى حالة العسرة. انتهى. [۲] قوله : بان فَلَسَه القاضي ; قال في «المصباح» : فلسه القاضي تفليساً : نادى عليه وأشهره بين الناس بأنه صار مُفلِساً. انتهى. وهذا مبني على أنَّ تفليس القاضي
[1] His saying: 'Upon the death of the assignee... etc.': Tawa, on the pattern of hasā, and may be elongated, meaning destruction. So in 'al-Misbah', and in Shar' (Islamic law): it is one of two matters according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him), and one of three matters according to his two companions. As for the two: first, the death of the assignee insolvent, meaning he left no property ('ayn), no debt, and no surety. 'Ayn means what suffices for the assigned debt, and similarly for debt; and the surety must be for the entire amount; if he guarantees part, then the rest is tawa, as they clearly stated. 'Debt' means what can be established in the dhimma, including currencies, measured items, and weighed items. Second: that the assignee denies the assignment and takes an oath, and there is no proof for the assignor or the assignee against the assignee. For the destruction of the assignee's debt is realized by each of the mentioned death and oath. As for the three matters by which tawa is established according to his two companions, two are as mentioned, and the third: that the judge declares him bankrupt (yufallisuhu), i.e., pronounces him bankrupt during his lifetime. And it is mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Alamgiriyya quoting from al-Muhit: 'And if the judge knows that the deceased has a debt due from an insolvent person, then according to Imam's view, the assignment is not judged invalid.' End. And the existence of a surety prevents his death being considered insolvent according to al-Ziyadat, and in al-Khulasa it does not prevent. So in al-Minah, and the author of al-Fath affirmed what is in al-Ziyadat without mentioning a difference. And if they differ: the assignee says 'He died insolvent' and the assignor says otherwise, then the statement is of the assignee with an oath based on knowledge, because he is clinging to the original (lack of assets) as if he were alive and denying wealth, mentioned by al-Shimni quoting from al-Mabsut. [1] His saying: 'insolvent (muflisan)': with lightening, its verbal noun is iflas. He said in al-Misbah al-Munir: 'A man becomes insolvent (aflasa) as if he reached a state where he has no fulus (small coins), just as it is said 'aqhara' when one reaches a state of being overcome. Some say: he became possessed of fulus after having dirhams, so he is muflis, plural mafalis, and it means the transition from a state of ease to a state of hardship.' End. [2] His saying: 'the judge declares his bankruptcy (bāna falaṣahu al-qāḍī)': He said in al-Misbah: 'The judge declared him bankrupt (fallasahu) by proclamation and public announcement among the people that he has become insolvent.' End. This is based on the fact that the judge's declaration of bankruptcy...
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the definition and conditions of tawa (loss) in hawalah. The commentator explains that tawa is destruction or loss. In Islamic law, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, tawa is established by two matters: (1) the death of the assignee in a state of insolvency (muflisan), meaning he leaves no property, debt, or surety sufficient to cover the assigned amount, and (2) the assignee's denial of the assignment under oath without evidence from the assignor or assignee. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), there is a third matter: the judge's declaration of the assignee's bankruptcy (taflis) during his lifetime. The text also discusses sub-issues: the meaning of 'muflis' (insolvent) linguistically and legally, the effect of a surety (whether it prevents the death from being considered insolvent – a difference between al-Ziyadat and al-Khulasa), and the rule if the assignee and assignor dispute the insolvency (the word is given to the assignee with an oath). The passage also clarifies that 'muflisan' means leaving no property, debt, or surety; 'din' includes fungibles; and the surety must cover the entire debt.

Key Terms
التَّوَى (al-tawā): Loss of the right to recover the assigned debt, realized by the assignee's death insolvent, denial under oath, or (per Sahibayn) judicial bankruptcy.
مُفْلِس (muflis): A person who has become insolvent, i.e., transitioned from ease (yusr) to hardship ('usr), leaving no assets or insufficient assets to cover debts.
تفليس (taflīs): The judge's declaration of bankruptcy, making public announcement that a person is muflis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what is the third matter that establishes tawa in hawalah?
A) The assignee becomes a minor.
B) The judge declares the assignee bankrupt (taflis).
C) The assignor becomes insolvent.
D) The assignee absconds.

Key Takeaway
Tawa in hawalah is established by specific events: insolvent death or denial with oath (Abu Hanifa), and also by judicial bankruptcy (Sahibayn and mufta bihi).

8 min
Status of Assignee After Assignor's Death: Hanafi Majority vs. Zufar
Explains why the assignee is an ordinary creditor after assignor's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on the assignee's status after the assignor's death and distinguish it from Zufar's view by identifying the key difference in the legal nature of hawalah.

Core Idea
The assignee is an ordinary creditor after the assignor's death because hawalah transfers the debt, not ownership of the debt.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute about the fate of an assigned debt when the assignor dies. The source from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a subtle question: does the assignee have priority over other creditors? The answer reveals a foundational principle about the nature of hawalah as a transfer (naql) rather than a sale (tamlik). We will unpack the reasoning of both the majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) and the minority view of Zufar, and see why the majority position is mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Begin with the factual context: the assignor (muheel) has made a hawalah transferring his debt against a third party to the assignee (muhtal). Then the assignor dies, leaving other creditors. The question is whether the assignee gets priority or shares equally.
The scenario involves both the assignor's death and the presence of other creditors.
2
State the majority Hanafi position
The majority (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) hold that the assignee is equal to other creditors (uswah) and shares proportionally. The assigned debt is part of the assignor's estate.
This is the position of al-Hidayah, al-Durar, and al-Fath.
3
Present Zufar's opposing view
Zufar argues by analogy (qiyas) to mortgage (rahn): since the assignee's right was attached to the debt during the assignor's life, he should have exclusive right after death, just as a mortgagee has priority over the mortgaged property.
Zufar is a prominent Hanafi scholar; his view shows the school's internal diversity.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to Zufar
The majority rebuts the analogy: hawalah does not transfer ownership of the debt (tamlik) but only transfers the right to collect. The assignee never gains possession (yad) over the debt, unlike a mortgagee who has possession of the collateral. Also, ownership of a debt from a non-debtor is invalid. Hence the assigned asset remains in the assignor's estate.
The key distinction: hawalah is a transfer of the right to demand, not ownership of the debt itself.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The majority view is relied upon (mufta bihi) because it is consistent with the nature of hawalah. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that under the majority, all creditors share the assigned debt proportionally; under Zufar, the assignee takes it exclusively.
This difference can significantly affect the distribution of an insolvent estate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع أن المحتال أسوة لغرماء المحيل بعد موته) ، إنما قال هذا ؛ لدفع تَوَهُم أن المحتال لَمَّا كان أسوة لغرماء المحيل بعد موته يكون حق المحيل متعلقاً بذلك الدين، فينبغي أن يكون للمحيل حق الطلب من المحتال عليه. فالحاصل أن الحوالة بالدين وإن كانت موجبة لتعلق حق المحتال بذلك الدين، لكنَّها أدنى مرتبة من الرهن ، حتَّى لا يكون المحتال أحق به بعد موت المحيل. تعلق به حق المحتال، كما إذا استهلك الرهن أحدٌ يضمنه للمرتهن ؛ لأنه يستحقه. صرح به في «الفتح»، وغيره. [١] قوله : مع أنَّ المحتال...الخ ؛ مع أنّ هذه الأموال إذا تعلق بها حق المحتال كان ينبغي أن لا يكون المحتال أسوةً لغرماء المحيل بعد موته، كما في الرهن مع [أنه] أسوة لهم ؛ لأنَّ العين التي بيد المحتال عليه للمحيل. والدين الذي له عليه لم يصر مملوكاً للمحال بعقدِ الحوالة لا يداً وهو ظاهر، ولا رقبة ؛ لأنّ الحوالة ما وضعت للتمليك ، بل للنقل، فيكون بين الغرماء، وأمّا المُرْتَهِنُ فملك المرهون يداً وحبساً ، فيثبت له نوع اختصاص بالمرهون شرعاً، ولم يثبت لغيره ، فلا يكون لغيره أن يشاركه فيه. كذا في «الدرر»). فالحاصل أن المحيل لو مات كان الدين والعين المحتالُ بهما بين غرمائه بالحصص. وقال زفره : يختص به المحتال، وهو القياس؛ لأنَّ حقه متعلق به حال حياتِه ، والمحيل كالأجنبي - عنه، حتى لا يكون له أخذه ، فصار كالخارج عن ملكه، فلا تُقْضَى به ديونه ولئن كان ملكه ثابتاً فتعلق حق المحتال سابق كالمرهون يختص بها المُرْتَهنُ؛ لتعلقِ حقه به سابقاً على حقه، وكدين الصحة يقدَّمُ على دين المرض لما قلنا. ولنا : أن هذا مال المحيل لم يثبت لغيرِهِ عليه يد الاستيفاء، فيكون بين غرمائه ؛ وهذا لأنه لم يملكه المحتال ؛ لأنَّ تمليك الدين من غير مـن عـلـيـه الدِّينِ باطل، لكن بالحوالة وجب للمحتال في ذمَّةِ المحال عليه دين مع بقاء دين المحيل ؛ ولهذا لو تَوَى [ما على المحال عليه ينوى على المحيل، ولم يثبت عليه أيضاً يد الاستيفاء ؛ لأن ثبوت اليد على ما في ذمَّةِ الغير لا يتصور.
Despite the fact that the assignee is equal to the other creditors of the assignor after his death, the author said this only to dispel the misconception that since the assignee is equal to the creditors after the assignor's death, the assignor's right is attached to that debt, so the assignor should have the right to demand from the assignee. The conclusion is that the assignment of debt, although it necessitates the attachment of the assignee's right to that debt, is of a lower rank than mortgage, so that the assignee does not become more entitled to it after the assignor's death. The assignee's right is attached to it, just as if someone consumes the mortgaged property, he must guarantee it to the mortgagee because it belongs to him. This is stated in al-Fath and others. [Footnote 1] His saying: 'Despite the fact that the assignee...' i.e., despite the fact that these properties, when the assignee's right is attached to them, it would be expected that the assignee should not be equal to the creditors after the assignor's death, as in the case of mortgage where he is not equal to them; because the property in the assignee's possession belongs to the assignor. And the debt owed to him did not become owned by the assignee through the contract of assignment, neither in terms of possession (which is obvious) nor in terms of ownership; because assignment was not established for ownership, but for transfer, so it remains among the creditors. As for the mortgagee, he owns the mortgaged property in terms of possession and detention, so a kind of special right is established for him over the mortgaged property by law, and it is not established for others, so others cannot share it with him. Thus in al-Durar. So the conclusion is that if the assignor dies, the debt and the property assigned are shared proportionally among his creditors. And Zufar said: The assignee has exclusive right to it, and this is by analogy; because his right was attached to it during the assignor's life, and the assignor is like a stranger to it, so much so that he cannot take it, so it becomes as if it is outside his ownership, so his debts are not settled from it. Even if his ownership is established, the assignee's right is prior, like the mortgagee is exclusive due to the prior attachment of his right, and like the debt of health is preferred over the debt of illness for what we have said. Our argument: This is the property of the assignor; no one else has the right of collection against it, so it is shared among his creditors. This is because the assignee has not owned it; because transferring ownership of a debt from someone other than the debtor is invalid. Rather, by assignment, a debt becomes obligatory upon the assignee in the assignor's liability while the assignor's debt remains; and because of this, if what is owed by the assignee is lost, it reverts back to the assignor. Also, the right of collection is not established upon him because establishing possession over what is in another's liability is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The issue is the status of the assignee (muhtal) after the assignor (muheel) dies. The text begins by addressing a potential misconception: one might think that because the assignee is treated equally with other creditors after the assignor's death, the assignor's right to the debt still exists, allowing him to demand payment from the assignee (the party upon whom the assignment is made). The passage clarifies that assignment (hawalah) does attach the assignee's right to the debt, but it is of a lower rank than mortgage (rahn). Unlike a mortgagee who has a special right (ikhtisas) over the mortgaged property, the assignee does not become the owner of the debt; rather, the debt is transferred but remains part of the assignor's estate. Therefore, if the assignor dies, the assigned debt is distributed proportionally among all creditors, including the assignee. The text then presents the opinion of Zufar, who argues by analogy (qiyas) that the assignee should have exclusive right, similar to a mortgagee, because his right was attached during the assignor's life. However, the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and the majority) responds that the assignee does not acquire ownership of the debt, and the assignor's estate remains liable. The assignee only has a claim against the party on whom the assignment was made, but he does not have a right of priority over other creditors of the assignor. This ruling is based on the principle that hawalah is a transfer of debt, not a transfer of ownership, and the assignee does not have a right of collection (yad al-istifa) because that is impossible over a debt in another's liability.

Key Terms
حوالة (hawalah): Transfer of debt from one party to another, where the assignor transfers his claim against the debtor to the assignee.
المحتال (al-muhtal): The assignee, i.e., the party who receives the right to collect the debt.
المحيل (al-muheel): The assignor, i.e., the party who transfers his claim to another.
المحال عليه (al-muhtal 'alayh): The party upon whom the assignment is made, i.e., the original debtor who now owes the assignee.
أسوة (uswah): Equality; the assignee is equal to other creditors, with no priority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority position, what is the status of the assignee (muhtal) after the assignor (muheel) dies?
A) He has exclusive right to the assigned debt.
B) He is equal to other creditors and shares proportionally.
C) He loses all rights because the assignment is void upon death.
D) He becomes the owner of the debt and can claim it from the estate.

Key Takeaway
In hawalah, the assignee does not gain priority over the assigned debt after the assignor's death because hawalah transfers the debt, not ownership of the debt.

Absolute Hawalah: Assignor's Right to Demand
Explains that in absolute hawalah, the assignor retains the right to demand from the assignee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for absolute hawalah and distinguish it from restricted hawalah regarding the assignor's right to demand and the effect on validity.

Core Idea
In absolute hawalah, the assignor may demand payment from the assignee without voiding the assignment.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a key distinction between two types of hawalah (debt assignment). In absolute hawalah, the assignor retains the right to demand the debt directly from the assignee, and doing so does not nullify the contract. In contrast, in restricted hawalah, taking the specific asset voids the assignment. Understanding this difference is essential for applying hawalah correctly in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of hawalah
Distinguish between mutlaqah (absolute) and muqayyadah (restricted) based on whether the assignment is tied to a specific asset or debt in the assignee's possession.
2
State the ruling for absolute hawalah
In absolute hawalah, the assignor may demand the debt from the assignee, and the hawalah remains valid even if the assignor takes the debt.
3
Contrast with restricted hawalah
In restricted hawalah, if the assignor takes the specific asset, the hawalah becomes void because the assignee's consent was based on that asset.
4
Explain the reasoning
The difference stems from the assignee's consent (rida'): in absolute hawalah, the assignee's acceptance is not tied to the specific asset; in restricted hawalah, it is. Taking the asset frustrates consent in the latter.
5
Consider practical consequences
In absolute hawalah, the assignor remains free from the debt, and the assignee becomes a creditor of the assignee (the person on whom assignment was made). The assignee does not compete with the assignor's other creditors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وفي المطلقة له الطلبُ من المحتال عليه : أي إذا كانت الحوالة مطلقة غير مقيَّدة بالوديعة، أو المغصوب أو الدين، فللمحيل طلب الوديعة، والمغصوب، والدين من المحتال عليه. ولم تبطل بأخذ ما عليه أو عنده) وإنما لم يكن للمحيل أن يأخذه ؛ لأنَّ المحال عليه لا يقبل بحوالة إلا ليتملك ما في ذتِهِ أو ليوفي من ذلك المال، فلو أخذه يفوتُ الرِّضاءُ، فتبطل الحوالة بخلاف الرهن ؛ لأنه يثبت عليه يد الاستيفاء ؛ ولهذا لو هلك يهلك على المرتهن ، فكان هو أحقُّ به، وكان ينبغي للمحتال أن لا يكون له حق المزاحمة ؛ لأن دينه تحول إلى ذمة المحال عليه ، فلا يزاحم غرماء المحيل كما إذا كانت الحوالة مطلقة. وإنما يثبت حق المزاحمة ؛ لأنَّ الحوالة كانت مقيَّدة بذلك المال، فإذا أخذ منه ذلك المالُ فاتَ الرِّضاءُ بالحوالة، فتبطل الحوالة، فيعودُ الدِّينُ إلى ذمَّةِ المحيل على ما كان قبل الحوالة، واستوضح ذلك بمسألة الوديعة والغصب ونحوهما، بخلاف ما إذا كانت مطلقة ؛ لأنَّ المحيل بالحوالة بريء من دين | وصار المحتال من غرماء المحال عليه، فلم يتعلق له حق بماله ، فلا يزاحم غرماء المحيل، وإذا قسم الدين بين غرماء المحيل لا يرجعُ المحتال على المحيل بحصة الغرماء ؛ لأنّ الدين الذي على المحال عليه صار مستحقاً ، فليس له أن يرجع عليه به، كما لو استحق الرهن ، ولا بما بقي من دينه بعد المحاصة ؛ لأنه صار تأوياً فلا يرجع به على أحد. كذا «التبيين»)، وغيره. [١] قوله : وفي المطلقة ... الخ ؛ المطلقة : هي أن يقول المحيل للمطالب : أحلتك بالألف التي لك على هذا الرجل ، أو لم يَقُل ليؤديها من المال الذي اليا عليه، فلو لم یکن عنده وديعة أو مغصوبة أو دين كان له أن يطالبه به ؛ لأنه لا تعلّق للمحتال بذلك الدين أو العين ؛ لوقوعها مطلقة عنه، بل بذمة المحتال عليه، وفي الذمة سعة، فيأخذ دينه أو عينه من ا المحتال عليه لا تبطل الحوالة. ومن المطلقة : أن يحيل على رجل ليس له عنده ولا عليه شيء.
And in the absolute (assignment), he (the assignor) has the right to demand from the assignee: i.e., if the assignment is absolute, not restricted to a specific deposit, or stolen property, or debt, then the assignor may demand the deposit, the stolen property, and the debt from the assignee. And it does not become void by taking what is upon him or with him. And why the assignor did not have the right to take it? Because the assignee does not accept an assignment except to own what is in his liability or to pay from that wealth; so if he takes it, the consent is frustrated, and the assignment becomes void. Unlike mortgage, because (the mortgagee) has the right of collection over him; and for this reason, if it perishes, it perishes at the risk of the mortgagee, so he is more entitled to it. And it would be appropriate for the assignee not to have the right to compete (with creditors), because his debt has been transferred to the liability of the assignee, so he does not compete with the assignor's creditors as in the case of absolute assignment. And the right to compete is established only because the assignment was restricted to that wealth; so if he takes that wealth from him, the consent for the assignment is frustrated, and the assignment becomes void, and the debt returns to the assignor's liability as it was before the assignment. And this was clarified by the issue of deposit and usurpation and the like. Unlike the absolute case, because by the assignment the assignor becomes free from the debt, and the assignee becomes one of the creditors of the assignee (i.e., the party on whom assignment was made), so no right attaches to his property, so he does not compete with the assignor's creditors. And if the debt is divided among the assignor's creditors, the assignee does not recourse against the assignor for the share of the creditors, because the debt owed by the assignee has become due, so he cannot recourse against him for it, just as if the mortgage is due, nor for the remainder of his debt after the distribution, because it becomes deferred, so he does not recourse against anyone. Thus in al-Tabyin and others. [Footnote 1] His saying: 'And in the absolute...' The absolute assignment is where the assignor says to the claimant: 'I assign you the thousand that you have against this man,' or he does not say 'to be paid from the money that is with him.' So if he does not have with him a deposit or stolen property or debt, he can demand it from him, because the assignee's right is not attached to that debt or property, since the assignment was absolute concerning it, but rather to the liability of the assignee, and liability is broad, so he takes his debt or property from the assignee without the assignment becoming void. And among the absolute is that he assigns to a man who has nothing with him nor owed to him.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling for an absolute hawalah (assignment without specification). In such a case, the assignor (muheel) retains the right to demand the debt or property from the assignee (muhtal 'alayh). The key point: if the assignor takes back what he had assigned, the hawalah does not become void—it remains valid. This is contrasted with a restricted hawalah, where the assignment is tied to a specific asset (e.g., a deposit or stolen property). In that case, if the assignor takes that asset, the hawalah becomes void because the consent (rida) is lost. The passage also clarifies that in absolute hawalah, the assignor becomes free from the debt, and the assignee becomes a creditor of the assignee (the party on whom assignment is made). Therefore, the assignee does not compete with the assignor's other creditors. The text further notes that if debts are distributed among the assignor's creditors, the assignee cannot seek reimbursement from the assignor for the share taken by other creditors. The footnote provides the definition of absolute hawalah: when the assignor says, 'I assign you the thousand that you have against this man' without specifying payment from a particular asset. It also includes the scenario where the assignee has nothing with him and owes nothing—the assignor can still demand payment directly, and the hawalah remains valid.

Key Terms
مطلقة (mutlaqah): Absolute assignment: a hawalah that is not restricted to a specific asset or debt already in the assignee's possession.
مقيَّدة (muqayyadah): Restricted assignment: a hawalah that is tied to a specific asset (like a deposit or stolen property) held by the assignee.
يد الاستيفاء (yad al-istifa'): Right of collection or possession for the purpose of receiving payment; it gives special priority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The hawalah becomes void because consent is frustrated.
B) The hawalah remains valid, and the assignor can take it.
C) The assignee becomes free of all obligations.
D) The assignor must compensate the assignee.

Key Takeaway
In absolute hawalah, the assignor retains the right to demand payment from the assignee, and doing so does not invalidate the assignment.

Effect of Transferor Taking from Transferee on Hawalah
Explaining validity of hawalah when transferor takes from transferee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on whether hawalah becomes void when the transferor takes from the transferee, and distinguish between absolute and restricted hawalah in this context.

Core Idea
Taking from the transferee does not void hawalah; restricted hawalah may void only if the specific debt is discharged.

A common question in hawalah (transfer of debt) is what happens if the transferor (muheel) actually collects the debt from the person he transferred the debt to (muhtal 'alayh). Does this action void the hawalah, or does it affect the rights of the parties? This passage clarifies the Hanafi position: the hawalah remains valid in both absolute and restricted forms, but with important nuances regarding when the underlying debt is discharged.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and action
The scenario is: the original debtor (muheel) takes something from the person on whom the hawalah was made (muhtal 'alayh) – whether it is a debt, a usurped item, or a deposit.
2
Classify the hawalah as absolute or restricted
Determine whether the hawalah is mutlaqah (not tied to a specific debt owed by muhtal 'alayh to muheel) or muqayyadah (tied to a specific debt). The ruling diverges based on this classification.
Absolute: muhtal 'alayh may or may not owe muheel. Restricted: muhtal 'alayh owes a specific debt to muheel, which is the subject of the transfer.
3
Apply the general rule: taking does not void hawalah
In both types, the hawalah does not become void simply because the muheel takes from the muhtal 'alayh. The act of taking is either a payment of his own right (absolute) or a wrongful taking that makes him liable (restricted), but the hawalah contract remains intact.
4
Analyze the exception for restricted hawalah
In restricted hawalah, if the specific debt itself becomes void (e.g., the sold item is found to be owned by another, or the slave is free), then the hawalah becomes void. However, if the debt falls due to a supervening cause without exonerating the original debtor (e.g., sold item perishes before delivery), the hawalah does not void; the muhtal can pay and then seek recourse from the muheel.
Key distinction: voiding of the specific debt vs. discharge by supervening event
5
Confirm absolute hawalah never voids
In absolute hawalah, the hawalah never becomes void under any circumstances. Even if it later emerges that the muhtal 'alayh owed nothing to the muheel, the hawalah remains valid, and the muhtal 'alayh must still pay the muhtal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحوالة ما تصح به الحوالة [1] ٤٠٩ أي لم تبطل الحوالة بأخذ المحيل ما على المحتال عليه، أو عنده، وهو الدين، والمغصوب والوديعة سواء كانت الحوالة مطلقة أو مقيدة، ففي المطلقة ظاهر، وأما في المقيدة ; فلأنَّ المحيل"" ليس له حق الأخذ من المحتال عليه، فإن دفع إليه المحتال عليه ، فقد وقع ما تعلّق به حق المحتال ، فيضمن المحتال عليه. والفرق بين المطلقة والمقيدة : أنه في المقيدة انقطعت مطالبة المحيل من المحال عليه ، فإن بطلَ الدِّينُ في المقيّدة، وتبيَّن براءة المحال عليه من الدين الذي قيَّدت الحوالة به بطلت : كما أن يحيل البائع رجلاً على المشتري بالثمن، ثم استحق المبيع أو ظهر حُرَّاً فتبطل، وللمحال الرجوع على المحيل بدينه. وكذا لو قيد بوديعة فهلكت عند المودع ، وأما إذا سقط الدينُ الذي قيدت به الحوالة بأمرٍ عارض ولم تتبين براءة الأصيل منه فلا تبطل ، مثل : أن يحتال بألف من ثمن مبيع، فهلك المبيع عنده قبل تسليمه للمشتري، سقط الثمن عن المشتري، ولا تبطل الحوالة، ولكنّه إذا أدّى رجعَ على المحيلِ بما أدى ; لأنه قضى دينه بأمره. وأما إذا كانت مطلقة فإنّها لا تبطل بحال من الأحوال، ولا تنقطع فيها مطالبة المحيل عن المحال عليه إلى أن يؤدّي، فإذا أدّى سقط ما عليه قصاصاً، ولو تبين براءة المحال عليه من دين المحيل لا تبطل أيضاً. كذا في «الجوهرة النيرة)، و«فتح القدير» وغيرهما. (A) ، [1] قوله : أي لم تبطل....الخ ; فيه إشارة إلى مرجع الضمير وهو الحوالة، وإلى أنَّ إضافة المصدر وهو الأخذ إلى قوله : ما عليه ; إضافة المصدر إلى المفعول، والفاعلُ محذوف، وهو المحيل، وإلى مرجع الضمير المجرور. (٢) قوله : فلان المحيل...الخ ; حاصله : لأن المحتال عليه قد دفع ما تعلق به حق المحتال إلى من ليس له حقُّ الأخذ فيضمنه للمحتال ، ويرجع إلى المحيل بما دفع إليه فلا تبطل الحوالة. (۱) «الجوهرة النيرة» (١ : ٣١٦). (۲) «فتح القدير» (٦ : ٣٥٥).
Book of Hawalah: What makes hawalah valid. [1] 409 That is, hawalah does not become void by the transferor taking what is on the transferee or with him, which is the debt, the usurped property, and the deposit, whether the hawalah is absolute or restricted. In absolute it is apparent; as for restricted, because the transferor does not have the right to take from the transferee. If the transferee pays him, then what is attached to the right of the transferee has occurred, so the transferee becomes liable. The difference between absolute and restricted: in restricted, the transferor's claim against the transferee is cut off. If the debt in the restricted case becomes void, and it becomes clear that the transferee is free from the debt with which the hawalah was restricted, it becomes void: like when the seller transfers a man onto the buyer for the price, then the sold item is claimed or appears to be free, then it becomes void, and the transferee has recourse to the transferor for his debt. Similarly, if he restricted with a deposit and it perishes with the depositary. As for when the debt with which the hawalah was restricted falls due to a supervening cause and the exoneration of the original debtor is not established, it does not become void: like transferring a thousand from the price of a sold item, then the sold item perishes with him before delivery to the buyer; the price falls from the buyer, and the hawalah does not become void, but if he pays, he has recourse to the transferor for what he paid, because he paid his debt by his order. As for when it is absolute, it does not become void in any case, and the transferor's claim against the transferee is not cut off until he pays. When he pays, what is upon him falls as a setoff, and even if it becomes clear that the transferee is free from the debt of the transferor, it also does not become void. As mentioned in 'Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah' and 'Fath al-Qadir' and others. (A), [1] His statement: 'That is, does not become void...' etc.; in it is an indication of the referent of the pronoun which is hawalah, and that the addition of the infinitive 'taking' to his statement 'what is on him' is the addition of the infinitive to the object, and the subject is omitted, which is the transferor, and to the referent of the genitive pronoun. (2) His statement: 'Because the transferor...' etc.; its summary: because the transferee has paid what is attached to the right of the transferee to one who does not have the right to take, so he becomes liable to the transferee, and he has recourse to the transferor for what he paid, so the hawalah does not become void. (1) 'Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah' (1:316). (2) 'Fath al-Qadir' (6:355).
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Kitab al-Hawalah on the conditions of validity. It states that hawalah does not become void simply because the transferor (muheel) takes from the transferee (muhtal 'alayh) what is due or in his possession, whether it is a debt, a usurped item, or a deposit. This applies to both absolute (mutlaqah) and restricted (muqayyadah) hawalah. In absolute hawalah, the reason is clear: the muhtal 'alayh owes the muheel anyway. In restricted hawalah, the muheel has no right to take from the muhtal 'alayh because the muhtal (the beneficiary) has a right in that specific item. If the muhtal 'alayh pays the muheel, he has violated the muhtal's right and becomes liable to him. The key difference between the two types: in restricted hawalah, the muheel's claim against the muhtal 'alayh is cut off. So if the specific debt (e.g., the price of a sale) becomes void—like if the sold item is found to be owned by another or the slave is free—the hawalah becomes void, and the muhtal can revert to the muheel. Similarly, if the hawalah was restricted to a deposit and the deposit perishes. However, if the debt falls due to a supervening cause without the original debtor being exonerated—like the sold item perishes before delivery, so the price falls, but the seller (original debtor) is not exonerated—the hawalah does not become void; the muhtal can pay and then seek recourse from the muheel. In absolute hawalah, the hawalah never becomes void, and the muheel's claim against the muhtal 'alayh remains until payment. Even if it later emerges that the muhtal 'alayh owed nothing to the muheel, the hawalah remains valid. The commentary clarifies grammatical points and the reasoning: when the muhtal 'alayh pays the muheel, he is effectively paying the muhtal's right, so he must compensate the muhtal. The sources cited are Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah and Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
الحوالة (al-ḥawālah): Transfer of debt; a contract whereby a debtor transfers his obligation to a third party who then becomes liable to the creditor.
المحيل (al-muḥīl): The transferor; the original debtor who transfers the debt to another.
المحتال (al-muḥtāl): The transferee (creditor); the person to whom the debt is transferred, i.e., the one who becomes the new creditor.
المحتال عليه (al-muḥtāl ʿalayh): The person upon whom the hawalah is made; the new debtor who assumes the obligation to pay.
مطلقة (muṭlaqah): Absolute hawalah; a transfer of debt not restricted to a specific debt owed by the transferee to the transferor.
مقيدة (muqayyadah): Restricted hawalah; a transfer of debt that is conditioned upon a specific debt owed by the transferee to the transferor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a restricted hawalah, if the specific debt (e.g., the price of a sale) becomes void because the sold item is found to be owned by another, what happens to the hawalah?
A) The hawalah remains valid and the muhtal must pay the muhtal 'alayh
B) The hawalah becomes void and the muhtal can revert to the muheel
C) The hawalah becomes void and the muhtal has no recourse
D) The hawalah remains valid but the muhtal cannot revert to the muheel

Key Takeaway
Hawalah remains valid even when the transferor takes from the transferee; only in restricted hawalah does it void if the specific debt is discharged.

Dispute over Basis of Hawalah: Who Is Believed?
Explaining two hawalah dispute scenarios and the Hanafi rule on credibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on disputes in hawalah and explain why acceptance of hawalah does not imply a prior debt.

Core Idea
In hawalah disputes, the party denying the underlying debt is believed — hawalah does not prove a debt.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a critical principle in hawalah disputes. When a disagreement arises about the cause of the transfer, the law does not automatically side with the transferor. Instead, the party who denies the existence of a prior debt is given the benefit of the doubt, because the act of hawalah is legally ambiguous: it can be based on a debt or simply an authorization to collect. Understanding this protects the rights of both parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dispute scenarios
First, the muhtal 'alayh pays the muhtal then demands reimbursement from the muheel, who claims the hawalah was for a debt owed by the muhtal 'alayh. Second, the muheel demands the amount received by the muhtal, who claims it was for a debt owed by the muheel. Recognize the parties and the claims.
In both scenarios, the party who originally transferred or received money is disputing the basis.
2
Recognize that the party denying the debt is believed
In the first scenario, the muhtal 'alayh denies owing a debt to the muheel, so his word is accepted unless the muheel brings proof. In the second scenario, the muheel denies owing a debt to the muhtal, so the muheel's word is accepted. The key is who denies the existence of the underlying debt.
This is consistent with the principle that the default state is freedom from debt.
3
Understand the reasoning: hawalah does not imply debt
The text explains that acceptance of hawalah (by the muhtal 'alayh) is not an admission of debt because hawalah is permissible even without a debt on the muhtal 'alayh. Similarly, the muheel's act of hawalah is not an admission of debt because hawalah can be used as an agency (wakalah) to authorize collection. Thus, the act itself is neutral and does not prove the basis.
This reasoning is based on the Hanafi view that hawalah is a transfer of a right, not necessarily a debt.
4
Apply the principle to similar disputes
If a party disputes the basis of any transfer that could serve multiple purposes (e.g., gift, loan, agency), the same logic applies: the act alone does not concede the specific characterization claimed by the other party. The burden of proof is on the party asserting the existence of a debt or obligation.
Always identify which party is denying the obligation and why their denial is prima facie accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقبل قول المحيل للمحتال عليه عند طلبه مثل ما أحال : أحلت بدين كان لي عليك ولا قول المحتال للمحيل عند طلبه ذلك أحلتني بدين لي عليك، [1] قوله : ولا يقبل ...الخ ; يعني إذا طالب المحتال عليه المحيل بمثل مـا أحـال بـه فقال : أحلت بدين لي عليك، لا يسمع قول المحيل للمحتال عليه : أحلت بدين لي عليك ، حين طلب المحتال عليه من المحيل مثل ما أحاله إلا ببينة. [2] قوله : يكون القول له ; إذ المحتال عليه أنكرَ الدَّين; لأنَّ إقراره بالحوالة وقبوله لا يكون إقراراً ولا دليلاً على أنّ عليه له ديناً ، إذ الحوالة تجوزُ بدون الدين على المحتالِ عليه، بل يسمعُ طلب المحتال عليه ; لوجود سبيه وهو أداء الدين بأمر. [3] قوله : ولا قول المحتال للمحيل .... الخ ; يعني لو طالب المحيل المحتال بما أحال ، فقال : أحلـتنـي بـديـن لـي عليك ، لا يسمع قول المحتال للمحيل : أحلـتني بـديـن لـي عليك، حين طلب المحيلُ من المحتال ما قبضه إلا ببينة. [4] قوله : فالقولُ له لا للمحتال عليه ; لأنّ المحيل أنكر الدين، إذ إقراره بالحوالة وإقدامه عليها لا يكون إقراراً بالدين ; لأنَّ الحوالة تستعمل في الوكالة، بمعنى نقـل التصرف بل يسمعُ طلب المحيل كطلب الموكل من الوكيل ما قبضه.
And the word of the transferor to the transferee is not accepted when he demands from him like what he transferred: 'I transferred for a debt that was owed to me by you,' nor the word of the transferee to the transferor when he demands that: 'You transferred me for a debt owed to me by you.' [1] His statement: 'And the word...' etc.; meaning if the transferee demands from the transferor like what he transferred, and he says: 'I transferred for a debt that was owed to me by you,' the word of the transferor to the transferee is not heard: 'I transferred for a debt that was owed to me by you,' when the transferee demands from the transferor like what he transferred except with evidence. [2] His statement: 'The word is his'; because the transferee denies the debt; for his admission of the hawalah and his acceptance do not constitute an admission or evidence that he owes him a debt, since hawalah is permissible without a debt on the transferee. Rather, the demand of the transferee is heard due to its cause, which is the payment of the debt by order. [3] His statement: 'Nor the word of the transferee to the transferor...' etc.; meaning if the transferor demands from the transferee what he transferred, and he says: 'You transferred me for a debt owed to me by you,' the word of the transferee to the transferor is not heard: 'You transferred me for a debt owed to me by you,' when the transferor demands from the transferee what he received except with evidence. [4] His statement: 'The word is his, not the transferee's'; because the transferor denies the debt, for his admission of the hawalah and his undertaking it do not constitute an admission of the debt; because hawalah is used in agency, meaning transfer of disposal. Rather, the demand of the transferor is heard like the demand of the principal from the agent for what he received.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage provides two related rulings on disputes arising from hawalah. First, if the muhtal 'alayh (the new debtor) pays the muhtal (the creditor) and then demands from the muheel (the original debtor) the amount he paid, and the muheel claims that the hawalah was for a debt owed by the muhtal 'alayh to him, the muheel's claim is not accepted without proof. The reason is that acceptance of hawalah does not imply admission of debt, since hawalah can be valid even if the muhtal 'alayh owes nothing to the muheel. The muhtal 'alayh's payment was by the muheel's order, so he can recover. Second, if the muheel demands from the muhtal the amount he received from the muhtal 'alayh, and the muhtal claims that the hawalah was for a debt owed by the muheel to him, the muhtal's claim is not accepted without proof. This is because the muheel's action of hawalah is not an admission of debt; hawalah can also be used as a form of agency (wakalah) to authorize collection. Therefore, the muheel is like a principal who can demand from his agent. In both cases, the party who denies the underlying debt is believed with an oath, following the principle that the original state is freedom from debt. The commentary emphasizes that hawalah is not an admission of debt.

Key Terms
المحال عليه (al-muḥāl ʿalayh): The person upon whom the debt is transferred; synonymous with المحتال عليه in this context.
الوكالة (al-wakālah): Agency; the text mentions that hawalah can be used in the sense of agency, i.e., authorization to collect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the muhtal 'alayh pays the muhtal and then demands from the muheel the amount paid, and the muheel claims it was for a debt owed to him by the muhtal 'alayh, whose word is accepted?
A) The muheel's word is accepted because he is the transferor
B) The muhtal 'alayh's word is accepted because he denies the debt
C) Neither party's word is accepted without proof
D) The muhtal's word is accepted because he witnessed the transaction

Key Takeaway
In hawalah disputes, the party denying the existence of a prior debt is believed because the act of hawalah itself does not prove a debt.

The Ruling on Suftajah (Bill of Exchange)
Stating that suftajah is disliked

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on suftajah and explain the reason for its dislike.

Core Idea
Suftajah is makruh because it resembles a loan that yields benefit, leading to potential usury.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on suftajah—a classical bill of exchange used for transferring debts. The passage places this issue in the chapter on hawalah (transfer of debt) because suftajah is fundamentally a debt transaction. Understanding why it is disliked helps clarify the Hanafi stance on financial instruments that may facilitate riba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruled act
Recognize what al-suftajah refers to: a written instrument for transferring a debt from one place to another, often with a fee.
It is not a modern innovation; it was known in classical Islamic commerce.
2
State the hukm level
The passage says 'yuqrah' (is disliked). Determine the precise level: makruh tanzihi (disliked but not prohibited).
Makruh tanzihi means it is better to avoid it, but it is not sinful.
3
Understand the context
The commentator notes that this issue is placed in the chapter on hawalah (debt transfer) because it is a transaction concerning debt.
This placement highlights the connection to debt and potential usury.
4
Explore the underlying reason
The dislike arises because suftajah often involves a benefit to the lender (e.g., fee or convenience), which resembles riba (usury) forbidden in Islam.
Hanafi principle: any loan that brings a benefit to the lender is akin to usury.
5
Distinguish from haram
Note that it is not haram but makruh. This means it is discouraged but the contract is valid if executed.
The ruling is based on caution, not a definitive prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويكره السفتجة [5] قوله : ويكره ...الخ ; إنّما أورد هذه المسألة في هذا المقام ; لأنها معاملة في الدِّينِ
And suftajah is disliked. [5] His statement: 'And suftajah is disliked...' etc.; he only mentioned this issue in this context because it is a transaction concerning debt.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This brief passage states the ruling that suftajah (a bill of exchange or a written instrument for transferring debt) is disliked (makruh). The commentary explains why this ruling is placed in the chapter on hawalah: because suftajah involves a transaction related to debt. In Hanafi fiqh, suftajah is considered makruh because it often involves a loan that yields a benefit to the lender (e.g., the fee for the instrument), which resembles riba (usury). The ruling is not haram but makruh tanzihi, meaning it is better to avoid it. This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that any loan that brings benefit is usurious.

Key Terms
السفتجة (al-suftajah): A bill of exchange or a written order for payment of a debt from one place to another, often involving a fee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on suftajah?
A) It is haram
B) It is makruh (disliked)
C) It is mubah (permissible)
D) It is wajib

Key Takeaway
Suftajah is makruh tanzihi in the Hanafi school due to its resemblance to a usurious loan; it is not prohibited but should be avoided.

Disputed Claims after Hawalah: Who Has the Burden of Proof?
Ruling on non-acceptance of claims by debtor and creditor after hawalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the admissibility of claims in hawalah disputes and apply the principle that accepting a hawalah does not constitute an admission of an underlying debt.

Core Idea
Accepting hawalah is not admitting debt; the denying party's statement (with oath) prevails.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common procedural question: after a hawalah transaction, if a dispute arises about whether a prior debt existed, who must prove their claim? The matn and commentary lay down a clear rule: the party who denies the existence of a prior debt has the advantage; their statement is accepted with an oath, and the claimant must bring evidence. This is because the act of hawalah (transfer of debt) is valid even if no prior debt existed on the part of the transferee or transferor. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly assigning the burden of proof in hawalah-related litigation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dispute scenarios
The text presents two post-hawalah disputes: (1) After the muhtal 'alayh pays the muhtal, the muhtal 'alayh demands reimbursement from the muheel, who claims he transferred because of a debt due from the muhtal 'alayh. (2) After the muhtal takes money from the muhtal 'alayh, the muheel demands that amount from the muhtal, who claims it was for a debt the muheel owed him.
In both cases, one party asserts a prior debt, the other denies.
2
Identify the claim and denial
In scenario 1: muheel claims debt owed by muhtal 'alayh; muhtal 'alayh denies. In scenario 2: muhtal claims debt owed by muheel; muheel denies. The denying party is the one whose statement is prioritized.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle
The key principle is that acceptance of hawalah is not an admission of a prior debt. Hawalah is valid even if the muheel had no debt on the muhtal 'alayh (it can be a mere agency). Therefore, the act of accepting or initiating a hawalah does not prove the existence of the claimed debt.
The text states: 'because the transfer is valid without the transferor having any debt due on the transferee' and 'because the transfer is used in the sense of agency.'
4
Determine which party's statement is accepted
Since acceptance does not imply debt, the denying party's statement (with oath) is accepted. The claimant must bring evidence (bayyinah). In scenario 1, the muhtal 'alayh's statement is accepted; in scenario 2, the muheel's statement is accepted.
The commentary clarifies: 'so the statement is for him, not the receiver' and 'so the statement is for the transferor, not the receiver.'
5
Note the practical consequence
The burden of proof falls on the party who asserts the existence of a prior debt. The denying party simply takes an oath. This differs from schools that might consider acceptance of hawalah as an implicit admission of debt.
The thamarah al-khilaf: in a Hanafi court, the denier's oath suffices; elsewhere, the claimant might not need additional proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ولا يقبل قول المحيل للمحتال عليه عند طلبه مثل ما أحال : أحلت بدين كان لي عليك) : أي أحال رجل رجلاً على آخر بمئة ، فدفع المحتال عليه إلى المحتال ، ثُمَّ طلب المحتال عليه تلك المئة من المحيل، فقال المحيلُ : إنما أحلتُ بمئة لي عليك، والمحتال عليه يُنْكِرُ أن عليه شيئاً يكون القول له لا للمحيل، ولا يكون قبولُ الحوالة إقراراً من المحتال عليه بمثةٍ ؛ لأنَّ الحوالة تصح من غير أن يكون للمحيل على المحتال عليه شيء.

( ويكره السفتجة :
[١] قوله : ولا يقبل ...الخ ؛ يعني إذا طالب المحتال عليه المحيل بمثل مـا أحـال بـه فقال : أحلت بدين لي عليك، لا يسمع قول المحيل للمحتال عليه : أحلت بدين لي عليك ، حين طلب المحتال عليه من المحيل مثل ما أحاله إلا ببينة.
[٢) قوله : يكون القول له ؛ إذ المحتال عليه أنكرَ الدَّين؛ لأنَّ إقراره بالحوالة وقبوله لا يكون إقراراً ولا دليلاً على أنّ عليه له ديناً ، إذ الحوالة تجوزُ بدون الدين على المحتالِ عليه، بل يسمعُ طلب المحتال عليه ؛ لوجود سبيه وهو أداء الدين بأمر.
[٣] قوله : ولا قول المحتال للمحيل .... الخ ؛ يعني لو طالب المحيل المحتال بما أحال ، فقال : أحلـتنـي بـديـن لـي عليك ، لا يسمع قول المحتال للمحيل : أحلـتني بـديـن لـي عليك، حين طلب المحيلُ من المحتال ما قبضه إلا ببينة. [٤] قوله : فالقولُ له لا للمحتال عليه ؛ لأنّ المحيل أنكر الدين، إذ إقراره بالحوالة وإقدامه عليها لا يكون إقراراً بالدين ؛ لأنَّ الحوالة تستعمل في الوكالة، بمعنى نقـل
التصرف بل يسمعُ طلب المحيل كطلب الموكل من الوكيل ما قبضه.
(And the statement of the transferor (muheel) to the transferee (muhtal 'alayh) when he demands like what was transferred: 'I transferred a debt that was due on you' is not accepted): That is, a man transferred another man's debt to a third for one hundred, so the transferee (muhtal 'alayh) paid the receiver (muhtal), then the transferee demands that one hundred from the transferor. The transferor says: 'I only transferred a debt that was due on you,' and the transferee denies that he owes anything. So the statement (the oath) is for the transferee, not the transferor. And acceptance of the transfer is not an admission by the transferee of having a debt, because the transfer is valid without the transferor having any debt due on the transferee. And the statement of the receiver (muhtal) to the transferor when he demands that: 'You transferred to me a debt that was due on you' is not accepted: That is, he transferred, so the receiver took the money from the transferee, then the transferor demands that money from the receiver. The receiver says to the transferor: 'You transferred to me by a debt that was due on you,' and the transferor denies that he owes him anything. So the statement (the oath) is for the transferor, not for the receiver. And the transfer is not an admission by the transferor of a debt due to the receiver, because the transfer is used in the sense of agency (wakalah). [Commentary:] (And suftaja is disliked...) [Footnote 1: The phrase 'and it is not accepted' etc. means if the transferee demands from the transferor like what was transferred, and the transferor says 'I transferred by a debt due on you,' his statement is not heard without evidence.] [Footnote 2: 'The statement is for him' because the transferee denied the debt; his acceptance of the transfer is not an admission or proof that he owes a debt, since the transfer is valid without a debt due on the transferee. Rather, the demand of the transferee is heard because of its basis: payment of debt by order.] [Footnote 3: 'And not the statement of the receiver to the transferor' etc. means if the transferor demands from the receiver what he received, and the receiver says 'you transferred to me by a debt due on you,' his statement is not heard without evidence.] [Footnote 4: 'So the statement is for him, not the receiver' because the transferor denied the debt; his acceptance of the transfer and undertaking it is not an admission of debt, because the transfer is used in agency, meaning transfer of authority. Rather, the demand of the transferor is heard, like the demand of the principal from the agent for what he received.]
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
Lesson Type: Ruling with proof. This passage addresses disputes after a hawalah transaction. The matn (original text) states that in two scenarios, the claim of one party is not accepted without evidence. The commentary expands the ruling. First scenario: after the transferee (muhtal 'alayh) pays the receiver (muhtal), the transferee demands repayment from the transferor (muheel). The transferor claims he only transferred because he had a debt due on the transferee. The transferee denies owing any debt. The ruling: The statement (with oath) is with the transferee, not the transferor. Reason: Acceptance of hawalah is not an admission of debt, because hawalah is valid even if the transferor has no debt on the transferee. The transferee's demand is based on having paid by the transferor's order. Second scenario: after the receiver takes the money from the transferee, the transferor demands that amount from the receiver. The receiver claims the transfer was for a debt the transferor owed him. The transferor denies owing any debt. The ruling: The statement is with the transferor, not the receiver. Reason: The transferor's undertaking of hawalah is not an admission of debt, because hawalah can be used in the sense of agency (wakalah) — merely authorizing the receiver to collect. Therefore, the transferor's claim is like a principal demanding from his agent. In both cases, the claimant must bring evidence.

Key Terms
المحيل (al-muheel): The transferor, who assigns a debt to be paid by another person.
المحتال (al-muhtal): The receiver (creditor in hawalah), who now claims the debt from the transferee.
المحتال عليه (al-muhtal 'alayh): The person on whom the hawalah is made; the one who is directed to pay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first dispute scenario, after the muhtal 'alayh pays the muhtal, the muhtal 'alayh demands repayment from the muheel. The muheel claims he transferred a debt due on the muhtal 'alayh. Whose statement (with oath) is accepted?
A) The muheel's
B) The muhtal's
C) The muhtal 'alayh's
D) Both must provide evidence

Key Takeaway
In hawalah disputes, the denying party's statement is accepted with an oath because the transfer does not imply a prior debt.

Ruling on Suftaja (Credit Instrument) in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling and juristic differences on the permissibility of suftaja

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on suftaja, distinguish between conditional and unconditional forms, and apply the prohibition of qard jarra naf'an.

Core Idea
Suftaja is only prohibited if the benefit (avoiding travel risk) is stipulated in the loan; otherwise, it is permissible.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on suftaja—a credit instrument used to avoid carrying cash while traveling. The matn of al-Hidayah declares it makruh, but later scholars clarify that the ruling depends on whether the benefit is a condition of the loan. Understanding this nuance prevents invalid contracts and ensures compliance with the prohibition of loans that draw a benefit.

Guided Path
1
Define suftaja and its context
Suftaja is a loan given in one city to be repaid in another, avoiding the risk of transporting money. Identify the act and its common usage among merchants.
The term appears in classical fiqh as a financial instrument.
2
Identify the initial ruling in the matn
The text states 'yukrah al-suftaja' (suftaja is disliked). Recognize that this is the base position.
The term 'karaaha' here is ambiguous—tanzihi or tahrimi?
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Scholars differ: some take the dislike as absolute, others (Zayla'i, Ibn al-Humam) condition it on whether the benefit is stipulated. Note the evidence each side uses.
This is a legitimate difference within the school.
4
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The root proof is the hadith 'kullu qardin jarra naf'an fa-huwa riba'. Explain how suftaja may fall under this if the benefit is a condition.
The proof is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view: if the suftaja is stipulated in the loan contract, it is haram and the loan is fasid; if it is not stipulated, it is permissible.
This is from Ibn al-Humam and later authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ويكره السفتجة : وهي إقراض لسقوط خطر الطريق)(۱)

وقال العلامةُ العَيْني في «الرمز» () : وفي «الواقعات»: رجل أقرض رجلاً مالاً
على أن يكتب له بها إلى بلد كذا، فإنه لا يجوز، وإن أقرضه بغير شرط وكتب كان جائزا ، وكذا لو قال : اكتب لي سفتجة إلى موضع كذا على أن يعطيك هنا فلا خير فيه. وفي «كفاية البيهقي»: وسَفاتج التجارِ مكروهة ؛ لأنه ينتفع بإسقاط خطر الطريق، إلا أن يقرض مطلقاً ، ثمَّ يكتب السُّفتجة فلا بأس، هكذا روي عن ابن عباس ه. انتهى. وإنما كرهت لورودِ النَّهي عن قرض جرّ نفعاً ؛ ولأنه تمليك دراهم بدراهم
(And suftaja is disliked: It is a loan to remove the danger of the road. Like kafalah (surety) and hawalah, or because it shifts the expected danger to the borrower, so it is similar to hawalah, or because it resembles hawalah insofar as one lends to a merchant then transfers what is due to a creditor in a certain city, often a friend. The apparent statement is that it is agreed upon, but it is not so. Rather, it is said: dislike is absolute in this action, which is what is indicated by the absolute wording of this matn, and of al-Kanz, al-Multaqa, and al-Tanwir. Allamah al-Zayla'i said in Sharh al-Kanz: It is said: if the benefit is not stipulated, then there is no harm. End quote. Ibn al-Humam said in al-Fath: In al-Fatawa al-Sughra and others: if the suftaj is stipulated in the loan, then it is haram, and the loan with this condition is fasid; but if it is not stipulated, it is permissible. End quote. Allamah al-Ayni said in al-Ramz: In al-Waqiat: A man lends money to another on condition that he writes a bill for him to such-and-such a place – then it is not permissible. If he lends without condition and then writes (the suftaja), it is permissible. Similarly, if he says: 'Write me a suftaja to such place on condition that I give you here' – there is no good in it. In Kifayat al-Bayhaqi: The sufatij of merchants are disliked because one benefits by removing the danger of the road, unless he lends absolutely then writes the suftaja, then there is no harm. Thus it is related from Ibn Abbas. End quote. It is disliked because of the prohibition of a loan that draws a benefit, and because it is a transfer of dirhams for dirhams.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
Lesson Type: Ruling with proof. This passage discusses the ruling on suftaja, defined as a loan to avoid the risk of travel (e.g., lending money in one city for repayment in another to avoid carrying cash). The matn states it is makruh. The commentary provides various positions: some say it is makruh absolutely; others differentiate based on whether the benefit (avoiding risk) is stipulated in the loan contract. If stipulated, it is haram and the loan is fasid; if not stipulated, it is permissible. The dislike (karahah) is due to the hadith prohibition of a loan that draws a benefit (qard jarra naf'an), and because it resembles a sale of money (money exchange with delay). The final position is that if the suftaja is not a condition of the loan, it is allowed; if it is a condition, it is prohibited and the loan is void. This is the relied-upon view.

Key Terms
السفتجة (al-suftaja): A credit instrument where money is lent in one city for repayment in another, often to avoid the risk of carrying cash while traveling.
قرض جر نفعاً (qard jarra naf'an): A loan that brings a benefit to the lender beyond the repayment of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, under what condition is suftaja considered haram and the loan fasid?
A) If the suftaja is written after the loan without condition
B) If the suftaja is stipulated in the loan contract
C) If the loan is for a merchant
D) If the suftaja is used for travel

Key Takeaway
Suftaja is permissible if the loan is given absolutely without stipulating the benefit; if the suftaja is a condition of the loan, it is haram and the loan is fasid.
