The Ruling on al-Suftaja: Loan to Avoid Travel Risk
Definition and ruling on al-sufṭajah with intra-Hanafi disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on al-sufṭajah and distinguish between a stipulated and unconditional suftaja.

Core Idea
A loan conditioned on a benefit is prohibited — the suftaja is only valid if the benefit is not stipulated.

Al-sufṭajah is a financial instrument used to transfer value across cities without physically carrying cash, thereby avoiding travel risks. While it may seem convenient, its validity hinges on a subtle condition: whether the benefit of risk elimination is stipulated in the loan contract. This lesson dissects the definition, the intra-Hanafi disagreement, and the relied-upon ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition
Find the core definition of suftaja in the passage: a loan given to eliminate travel risk, typically by lending money to a merchant or agent who then repays in another city.
The term comes from a hollow container (sifta) used to hide money.
2
Locate the Key Legal Element
The benefit is the elimination of travel risk. The ruling depends on whether this benefit is made a condition (shart) of the loan.
3
Trace the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Earlier texts (Kanz, Multaqa, Tanwir) declare suftaja absolutely disliked (makruh). Later authorities like Ibn al-Humam and al-Ayni distinguish between stipulated and unconditional suftaja.
The absolute dislike view is mentioned but not relied upon.
4
Apply the Principle 'Qard Jarra Naf'an'
If the benefit is stipulated, the loan becomes a 'qard jarra naf'an' (loan drawing benefit), which is prohibited by hadith. Therefore, the loan is haram and fasid (corrupt). If unconditional, it is permissible.
The hadith 'Every loan that brings a benefit is prohibited' is the dalil.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon Hanafi position is the conditional one: haram if stipulated, permissible if not. This is the view taken by later fatwa authorities.
Authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحوالة ما تصح به الحوالة وهي إقراض لسقوط خطر الطريق وهي إقراض لسقوط خطر الطريق)(۱) ٤١١ كالكفالة والحوالة، أو لأنه أحال الخطر المتوقع على المستقرض، فيكون في معنى الحوالة، أو لأنها تشبه الحوالة من جهة أنه يقرضُ تاجراً ثم يحيل ما عليه لغريم به في بلد كذا، أو صديق له غالباً. وظاهر الكلام أنه متفق عليه ، وليس كذلك، بل قيل : الكراهة في هذا الفعل (٤) مطلقا، وهو الذي يفيده إطلاق هذا المتن ، و«الكنز» (۳) ، و«الملتقى» (۳) ، و«التنوير» وقال العلامة الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز ) : وقيل : إذا لم تكن المنفعة مشروطة فلا بأس به. انتهى. وقال ابن الهمام في «الفتح») : وفي «الفتاوى الصغرى» وغيرها : إن كان السفتج مشروطاً في القرض فهو حرام، والقرضُ بهذا الشرط فاسد، وإن لم يكن مشروطاً جاز. انتهى. (۷) وقال العلامةُ العَيْني في «الرمز» () : وفي «الواقعات»: رجل أقرض رجلاً مالاً على أن يكتب له بها إلى بلد كذا، فإنه لا يجوز، وإن أقرضه بغير شرط وكتب كان جائزا ، وكذا لو قال : اكتب لي سفتجة إلى موضع كذا على أن يعطيك هنا فلا خير فيه. وفي «كفاية البيهقي»: وسَفاتج التجارِ مكروهة ؛ لأنه ينتفع بإسقاط خطر الطريق، إلا أن يقرض مطلقاً ، ثمَّ يكتب السُّفتجة فلا بأس، هكذا روي عن ابن عباس ه. انتهى. وإنما كرهت لورودِ النَّهي عن قرض جرّ نفعاً ؛ ولأنه تمليك دراهم بدراهم ، (۱) ظاهر كلام «الكنز» (ص (١١٤) ، و«الملتقى» (ص ۱۲۷)، و«التنوير»(ص١٤٥) أنها مكروهاً مطلقاً، ولكن صاحب «التبيين» (٤) : (١٧٥)، و«الفتح» (٦ : ٣٥٦)، و«الرمز»(٢: ٨٢)، و«حاشية رد المحتار (۳): (۱۷۱) أنه إذا لم تكن المنفعة مشروطة فلا بأس به. (۲) «کنز الدقائق»(ص١١٤). (۳) «ملتقى الأبحر»(ص۱۲۷). (٤) تنوير الأبصار» (ص ١٤٥). (٥) تبيين الحقائق (٤ : ١٧٥). (٦) «فتح القدير (٦ : ٣٥٦). (۷) رمز الحقائق» (۲ : ۸۲). ٤١٢ زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية في «المغرب»: السُّفْتَجةُ : بضم السَّينِ، وفتح التَّاء : أن يدفع إلى تاجر مالاً بطريق الإقراض ليدفعه إلى صديقه في بلدٍ آخر، وإنما يقرضُهُ لسقوطِ خَطَرِ الطَّريقِ، وهي تعريب سفتة ) وإنما سمي الإقراض المذكور بهذا الاسم تشبيهاً بموضع الدراهم والدنانير في السفاتج : أي في الأشياء المجوفة، كما يجعل العصا مجوفاً ويخبو فيه المال وإِنما شُبِّه به ؛ لأنَّ كلا منهما احتيال لسقوط خطرِ الطَّريقِ ، أو لأنَّ أصلها أن الإنسانَ إِذا أَرادَ السفر وله نقد، أو أراد إرساله إلى صديقه، فوضعَهُ في سفتجةٍ ثم مع ذلك خاف الطَّريقَ فأقرض ما في السُّفْتَجةِ إنساناً آخر، فأطلق السفتجة على إقراض ما في السُّفتجة ، ثُمَّ شاعَ في الإقراض لسقوط خطر الطريق. فإذا شرط أن يدفع في بلد آخر صارَ في حكم التأجيل ، والتأجيل في الأعيان لا يصح، ذكره شيخنا العلامةُ الطَّحْطَاوِيُّ (۱) نقلاً عن الحموي. فقال : [١] قوله : قوله في «المغرب»(۲) ؛ قال في «المصباح»(۳) : السُّفتجة : قيل بضم، السين وقيل : بفتحها، وأما التاء فمفتوحة فيهما ، فارسي معرب ، وفسرها بعضهم هي كتاب صاحب لوكيله أن يدفع مالاً قرضاً يأمنُ به خطر الطريق. انتهى. [٢] قوله : سفتة ؛ بضم أول : بمعنى تحفة ؛ وجيزي باشد كه شخصي بجهـت شخصي از مكي به ملك ويكر . برسم تكلف یا بضاعت بفرستد، وبفتح أول : بروزن ؛ هفته آن است كه كسي جيزي از كسي بطريق عاریت یا قرض ياور عوض جيزي بكير وتادر شهري ديكر بازوهــد و دست لاف راینز گویند و آن سوداي اول اصناف وفـر وشندگان باشد باین معنی بضم اول هم آهده است. كذا في «البرهان القاطع».
Book of Transfer of Debt: What is valid for transfer of debt. It is a loan for the elimination of road danger. It is a loan for the elimination of road danger... like kafala and hawala, or because he transfers the expected danger to the borrower, so it is in the meaning of hawala, or because it resembles hawala in that he lends to a merchant then transfers what is owed to his creditor in such a city, or often to his friend. The apparent speech is that it is agreed upon, but it is not; rather it is said: dislike in this action absolutely, and that is what is indicated by the absolute wording of this text, al-Kanz, al-Multaqa, and al-Tanwir. Al-Zayla'i said in Sharh al-Kanz: It is said: if the benefit is not stipulated, then there is no harm. End quote. Ibn al-Humam said in al-Fath: In al-Fatawa al-Sughra and others: if the suftaj is stipulated in the loan, it is prohibited and the loan with this condition is corrupt (fasid); and if it is not stipulated, it is permissible. End quote. Al-Ayni said in al-Ramz: In al-Waqi'at: A man lent money to a man on condition that he write for him to such a city, it is not permissible; and if he lent without condition and wrote, it was permissible; and likewise if he said: write me a suftaja to such a place on condition that I give you here, there is no good in it. In Kifayat al-Bayhaqi: The sufatij (plural) of merchants are disliked because one benefits by eliminating road danger, except that he lends absolutely, then writes the suftaja, then no harm. Thus narrated from Ibn Abbas. End quote. It is disliked because of the prohibition of a loan that draws benefit; and because it is a transfer of dirhams for dirhams... The apparent wording of al-Kanz, al-Multaqa, and al-Tanwir is that it is disliked absolutely, but the author of al-Tabyin, al-Fath, al-Ramz, and Hashiyat Radd al-Muhtar state that if the benefit is not stipulated, there is no harm. al-Mughrib: al-Suftaja: with damma on the sin and fatha on the ta: that one pays money to a merchant by way of loan to pay it to his friend in another city, and he lends him only for the elimination of road danger, and it is an Arabization of 'sifta'. It is called this as a resemblance to the place of dirhams and dinars in al-sufatij: meaning in hollow things, like making a staff hollow and hiding money in it. It is likened to it because each is a stratagem to eliminate road danger; or because its origin is that when a person wanted to travel and had cash, or wanted to send it to his friend, he put it in a suftaja, then still feared the road, so he lent what was in the suftaja to another person, so suftaja was applied to the loan of what is in the suftaja, then it became common for lending to eliminate road danger. If he stipulates that it be paid in another city, it becomes in the ruling of deferment, and deferment in physical objects is invalid. Our teacher al-Tahtawi mentioned it from al-Hamawi. He said: [1] His saying: His saying in al-Mughrib; he said in al-Misbah: al-Suftaja: it is said with damma on the sin, and it is said with fatha, and as for the ta it is fatha in both, Persian arabicized, and some interpreted it as a document from the owner to his agent to pay money as a loan to be safe from road danger. End quote. [2] His saying: sifta: with damma on the first: meaning a gift; something that a person sends to a person from Makka to another town as a gift or commodity, and with fatha on the first: on the weight of 'hafta'? That is that someone takes something from someone by way of loan or borrowing without exchange for something and gives it back in another city, and they call it 'dast-i-laf rahin'?, and that is the trade of first types of merchants? By this meaning it also comes with damma on the first. Thus in al-Burhan al-Qati'.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on the contractual instrument known as al-sufṭajah (السفتجة). The author begins by defining it as a loan given to eliminate the danger of travel, where the lender gives money to a merchant or friend in one city to be paid in another city, thereby avoiding the risk of carrying cash on the road. The text then notes that while some earlier Hanafi texts (like al-Kanz, al-Multaqa, and al-Tanwir) declare it absolutely disliked (makruh), later authorities distinguish based on whether the benefit (i.e., the elimination of travel risk) is stipulated as a condition in the loan contract. Al-Zayla'i in his commentary on al-Kanz and Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir clarify that if the suftaja is made a condition of the loan, it is prohibited (haram) and the loan becomes corrupt (fasid) because it is a loan that draws a benefit, which is forbidden. However, if the loan is given unconditionally and the lender later writes a suftaja as a separate arrangement, it is permissible. The passage then discusses the linguistic origin of the term, linking it to hollow containers used for hiding money, and concludes with citations from al-Mughrib, al-Misbah, and al-Burhan al-Qati' providing further definitions. The overall ruling is that the key factor is conditionality: a stipulated suftaja is invalid, while an unconditional loan followed by a suftaja is permitted. This reflects the Hanafi principle that any loan providing a stipulated benefit is akin to riba.

Key Terms
السفتجة (al-sufṭajah): A loan given to eliminate travel risk, where the lender gives money to be repaid in another city, often through a merchant or agent.
الحوالة (al-ḥawālah): Transfer of debt from one person to another; the suftaja is compared to it in function.
قرض جر نفعاً (qarḍ jarra naf‘an): A loan that brings a benefit to the lender, which is prohibited according to a hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in this passage, when is suftaja prohibited (haram)?
A) When it is done unconditionally
B) When the benefit of eliminating travel risk is stipulated in the loan contract
C) In all cases, because it resembles riba
D) When the loan is given to a non-merchant

Key Takeaway
The suftaja loan is only prohibited if the benefit (avoiding travel risk) is a stipulated condition; otherwise it is permissible.

Judgment on an Absent Party: Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Enforceability
Explanation of the ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on enforceability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of absence in Hanafi qada, state the ruling for each, and apply the mufta bihi position regarding enforceability of a judgment on an absent party.

Core Idea
A judgment on an absent person without a representative is void; if issued, it requires another judge's approval to be enforced.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a key rule in Hanafi procedural law: a judge cannot pass a binding judgment on an absent party unless a representative is present. However, what if a judge does so anyway? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether such a judgment can ever be enforced. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the exceptions, and the preferred fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its scope
The basic rule is that a judge does not pass judgment on an absent person except in the presence of his representative (na'ib). The ruling applies to judgments against the absent (qada' 'ala gha'ib) and also for the absent (qada' lahu). This is agreed upon (ittiqaqi).
The text says 'لا يقضي على غائب ولا له'.
2
Distinguish the three types of absence
The passage from al-Ma'din lists three aspects: (1) opponent hiding: judge appoints a deputy by proclamation, then can judge (consensus allowed); (2) opponent present in city but absent from court: prohibited by consensus, except one Shafi'i opinion; (3) opponent absent from the city: the disputed scenario covered by the ruling.
The intended meaning is absence from the city, which includes absence from court a fortiori.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on enforceability (nafadh)
If a judge who permits judgment on an absent person (e.g., from a lenient view) does issue such a judgment, does it take effect? There are two narrations: one says it does not enforce (la yanfadh); another says it can be enforced if approved by another judge (imda' qadi akhar). Some also say if the judge himself sees it, it enforces.
The dispute is over nafadh, not over the original permissibility. The text notes that the original ruling is that such a judgment is not valid (la yasihh) per some, but the focus is on enforcement.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The preferred view (from Fath al-Qadir, Munyat al-Mufti, Majma' al-Fatawa, and Imam Zahir al-Din) is that the judgment does not take effect (la yanfadh) and requires approval from another judge. This is to preserve the integrity of the Hanafi madhhab.
Khahrizada is cited as the one who gave fatwa on enforcement, but the majority and later authorities side with non-enforcement.
5
Apply to scenarios with evidence and confession
If the plaintiff established evidence (bayyinah) against a present defendant, and the defendant later becomes absent, judgment can be passed after his return or through a representative without repeating evidence. Similarly, if the defendant confessed (iqrar) before the judge and then disappeared, judgment can be passed even while absent, because confession is stronger than evidence (can be invalidated only by evidence, not by mere denial).
Abu Yusuf is reported to allow judgment on evidence as well for convenience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حق القاضي؛ لأنَّ المدعي عالم بحقه، والقاضي بان له بكلام المدعي، فتعين أن يكون في حق الخصم، هكذا قالوا في هذا المقام. واعلم أنَّ صاحب «الدر المختار» فسر قول الماتن: لا يقضي على غائب ولا له بقوله: أي لا يصح بل لا ينفذ على المفتى به. انتهى. وقال شيخنا الطَّحْطَاوي: هذا الإضراب لا يتم؛ لأنه إذا كان لا يصح لا يتم، قال في «البحر»: والذي ظهر لي من كلامهم أنَّ المذهب عن أصحابنا عدمُ صحة القضاء على الغائب، وأنَّ القاضي الذي يراه إذا قضى عليه فإنه يتوقف على الإمضاء؛ لأنَّ الاختلاف في نفس القضاء، وما عدا هذا من الأقوال من تصرفات المشايخ. انتهى كلام الطحطاوي. وقال في «المنح»: اختلف التصحيح في نفاذه، فقيل: لا ينفذ، وقيل: ينفذ، ورجح الأوّل في «فتح القدير»، وأنه لا بُدَّ من إمضاء قاض آخر؛ لأن الاختلاف في نفس القضاء. وفي «البزازية» من القضاة قال الإمامُ ظهير الدين الله في نفاذ القضاء على الغائب روايتان، ونحن نفـتـي بعـدم النفاذ، كيلا يتطرقوا إلى إبطال مذهب أصحابنا. انتهى. والقائل بأنّ الفتوى على النفاذِ خُواهَر زَادَه الله له، وفي «البحر» نقلا عن «منية المفتي»: القضاء على الغائب بلا خصم فيه روايتان، ويفتى بعدم النفاذ، وقيل: إن رآه قاض فقضى به ينفذ. انتهى. وفي «مجمع الفتاوى» عن «المنتقى»: إنه لا ينفذ وعليه الفتوى. انتهى. فالحاصل أنه لا خلاف عندنا في عدم جواز القضاء على الغائب، وإنما الخلاف في أنه لو قضى به من يرى جوازه هل ينفد بدون تنفيذ، أو لا بد من إمضاء قاض آخر، وحال الترجيح ما علمت آنفاً.
[1] قوله: على غائب؛ قال في «المعدن»: المسألة على ثلاثة أوجه: ١. في وجه يجوز القضاء على الغائب اتفاقاً، وهو ما إذا اختفى الخصم في بيته ولا يحضر مجلس القاضي، وبعث أمناءه إلى داره ونوادي على باب داره، ينصب وكيلاً ثم يقضي على وجه الوكيل، ويكون ذلك قضاءً على الغائب. كذا في «الذخيرة»، وغيره. ٢. وفي وجه: لا يجوز اتفاقاً، وهو ما إذا كان ظاهراً في المصر، غائباً عن المجلس إلا على أحد قولي الشافعي له. ٣. وفي وجه: خلاف، وهو إذا كان غائباً عن البلد. انتهى. فظهر أنَّ المراد على غائب عن البلد، فكان غائباً عن المجلس بالطَّريقِ الأولى، ولو أقام المدعي البينة على خصم حاضر، وزكّيت بينته، ثمَّ غاب المدعى عليه لا يقضي عليه حتى يحضر هو، أو من يقوم مقامه فيقضى عليه بتلك البينة من غير إعادتها، وكذا إذا غاب قبل التزكية. ولو أقر عند الحاكم فغاب قبل أن يقضي عليه قضى عليه وهو غائب؛ لأنَّ له أن يظهر البينة فيبطل به دون الإقرار، وعن أبي يوسف له أنه يقضي بالبينة أيضاً وهذا أرفقُ إلى الناس على ما قال السَّرَخْسِي له، والتقييد بقوله: على غائب اتفاقي؛ لأنه لا يقضي لغائب أيضاً. كما صرحوا به. [2] قوله: إلا بحضرة نائبه... الخ؛ تفصيلُه: أَنَّ مَن يقوم مقام الغائب: إما أن يكون بإنابته كالوكيل والوصي ومتولي الوقف.
The right of the judge; because the claimant knows his right, and the judge sees it through the claimant's speech, so it is determined to be in the right of the opponent. Thus they said in this context. And know that the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar interpreted the author's saying: "He does not pass judgment on an absent person nor for him" by his saying: i.e., it is not valid, rather it is not enforceable according to the fatwa. End. And our teacher al-Tahtawi said: This apodosis is not complete; because if it is not valid, it is not complete. He said in al-Bahr: What appeared to me from their speech is that the madhhab of our scholars is the invalidity of judgment on the absent, and that the judge who sees it if he passes judgment on him, then it depends on approval; because the difference is in the judgment itself, and other than this among the statements are from the actions of the mashayikh. End of al-Tahtawi's words. And he said in al-Munah: The correction differed regarding its enforcement; some said: it is not enforced, and some said: it is enforced, and the first was preferred in Fath al-Qadir, and that there must be approval from another judge because the difference is in the judgment itself. And in al-Bazzaziyyah from the judges, Imam Zahir al-Din said regarding the enforcement of judgment on the absent: two narrations, and we give fatwa of non-enforcement, so that they do not seek to invalidate the madhhab of our scholars. End. And the one who says that the fatwa is on enforcement is Khahrizada, may Allah increase him. And in al-Bahr quoting from Munyat al-Mufti: Judgment on the absent without a litigant has two narrations, and fatwa is given for non-enforcement, and it is said: If a judge sees it and passes judgment accordingly, it is enforced. End. And in Majma' al-Fatawa from al-Muntaqa: It is not enforced and upon it is the fatwa. End. So the conclusion is that there is no difference among us regarding the impermissibility of judgment on the absent, but the difference is that if someone who sees its permissibility passes judgment, is it enforced without approval, or must there be approval from another judge, and the state of preference is as you knew earlier.
[1] His saying: on an absent; he said in al-Ma'din: The issue is on three aspects: 1. In one aspect, judgment on the absent is permissible by consensus, which is when the opponent hides in his house and does not attend the judge's court, and the judge sends his trustees to his house and proclaims at the door of his house: appoint a deputy, then he passes judgment on the basis of the deputy, and that is a judgment on the absent. Thus in al-Dhakhira and others. 2. In one aspect: it is not permissible by consensus, which is when he is apparent in the city, absent from the court, except according to one of Imam al-Shafi'i's two opinions. 3. In one aspect: there is difference, which is when he is absent from the city. End. So it appears that what is meant is absent from the city, so he is absent from the court a fortiori. And if the plaintiff establishes evidence against a present opponent, and his evidence is scrutinized, then the defendant becomes absent, judgment is not passed against him until he is present or someone standing in his place, and judgment is passed against him on the basis of that evidence without repeating it, and similarly if he becomes absent before scrutiny. And if he confesses before the judge then becomes absent before judgment is passed, judgment is passed against him while he is absent; because he can present evidence to invalidate it, unlike confession. And from Abu Yusuf: he passes judgment on the basis of evidence as well, and this is more convenient for people according to al-Sarakhsi. And the restriction by his saying: on an absent is agreed upon; because he does not pass judgment for an absent either, as they explicitly stated. [2] His saying: except in the presence of his representative... etc.; its details: Whoever stands in the place of the absent: either by his appointment, like the deputy, executor, and trustee of a waqf.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the ruling that a judge may not pass a binding judgment on an absent party (قضاء على غائب) except in the presence of his representative (نائب). The commentary first addresses the enforceability (نفاذ) of such a judgment if a judge issues it despite the ruling. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some say the judgment is void from the outset (لا يصح) and thus not enforceable; others say it can be enforced if approved by another judge. The preferred view (مفتى به) is that it does not take effect without separate approval, to preserve the strength of the Hanafi position. The passage then clarifies the types of absence. The ruling applies specifically when the person is absent from the city (غائب عن البلد). If the opponent is present in the city but absent from court, it is forbidden by consensus except according to one of Imam al-Shafi'i's opinions. If the opponent is hiding, the judge can appoint a deputy for him and then pass judgment. The passage also discusses cases where evidence was established before the opponent's absence: judgment can still be passed after he returns or through a representative. A confession (إقرار) given before absence allows judgment even while absent, since confession is stronger than evidence. Abu Yusuf is reported to allow judgment based on evidence as well for convenience. The core principle is that judgment cannot be passed for an absent person either; the representative is required for both plaintiff and defendant in absentia.

Key Terms
قضاء على غائب (qada' 'ala gha'ib): A judge passing a binding ruling against a party who is not present in the court and has no representative.
نائب (na'ib): A representative authorized by the absent party, either by direct appointment (wakil) or by legal standing (wasi al-qadi, mutawalli al-waqf).
نفاذ (nafadh): Enforceability or legal effect of a judgment. The dispute is whether a judgment issued contrary to the ruling can take effect without further approval.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view regarding the enforceability of a judgment passed on an absent person without a representative?
A) It is automatically void and cannot be enforced under any circumstances.
B) It is enforceable immediately if the judge so decides.
C) It is not enforceable unless another judge approves it.
D) It is enforceable only if the absent person confesses.

Key Takeaway
A judgment on an absent party without a representative is not enforceable unless approved by another judge; this preserves the Hanafi position against dilution.

Enforceability of Arbitrator's Judgment on the 'Aqilah in Accidental Death
Commentary on arbitrator's authority and its limits in qatl khata' cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the enforceability of an arbitrator's judgment concerning diyah upon the 'aqilah and explain the rationale based on the hadith of Ḥamal ibn Mālik.

Core Idea
An arbitrator's judgment is binding only on those who appointed him, not on third parties like the 'aqilah.

This lesson examines a key ruling from Hanafi fiqh: when an arbitrator (muhakkam) adjudicates a case of accidental death and orders diyah upon the 'aqilah (tribal surety), that judgment is not enforceable against them. Why? Because the 'aqilah never consented to the arbitration. We will explore the reasoning, the supporting hadith, and the practical limitations of arbitration in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the first line of the passage: 'The judgment of the arbitrator in accidental blood concerning the diyah upon the 'aqilah is not enforceable.' Note that the same applies if he judges diyah upon the killer.
2
Understand the reason for non-enforceability
The passage states: 'because the 'aqilah did not appoint him.' The arbitrator's authority is limited to those who consented to his arbitration. Third parties, like the 'aqilah, are not bound.
3
Distinguish binding effect on litigants vs. third parties
The arbitrator's judgment is binding on the parties who appointed him (e.g., buyer and seller in a sale dispute). He can compel them to perform. This is the benefit of arbitration.
Example given: the arbitrator can imprison a party who refuses to deliver the sold item or pay the price.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence
The hadith of Ḥamal ibn Mālik shows that the Prophet (peace be upon him) placed diyah on the 'aqilah (asaba) in an accidental death. This establishes the liability of the 'aqilah, but the arbitrator's judgment against them is not enforceable without their consent.
The hadith is quoted in the passage.
5
Examine the role of the formal judge
If the arbitrator's judgment is raised to a formal judge, the judge may uphold it if it agrees with his school, or void it if it does not. A formal judge's ruling is binding on all, unlike the arbitrator's.
This contrasts with the judgment of a qadi, which cannot be voided by another qadi even if it contradicts his school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحُكم المحكم في دم خطأ بالدِّيةِ على العاقلة لا يَنْفُدُّ، وكذا إن حَكَمَ بِالدِّيَةِ على القاتل لا يَنْفُدُ أيضاً، فينقضه القاضي، ويقضي على العاقلة وفائدته إلزام الخصم، فإنَّ المتبايعينَ إِن حَكَما حَكَماً، فالمحكمُ يُجيرُ المشتري على تسليم الثَّمن، والبائع على تسليم المبيع، ومن امتنع يحبسه ، فذكر المجتهدات ليدل على غيرها بالطريق الأُولَى.
وإذا صح " التحكيم في جميع القضايا لا يُفتى بذلك ؛ لأن العوام يتجاسرونَ على ذلك، فيُقِلُّ الاحتياج إلى القاضي، فلا يَبْقَى لحكام الشرع رونق، ولا للمحكمة جمال وزينة.
وحُكْمُ المُحَكِّم في دم خطأ بالدِّيَةِ على العاقلة لا يَنْقُدُ)؛ لأنَّ العاقلة لم يُحكموه، وكذا إن حَكَمَ بالدِّيَةِ على القاتل لا يَنْقُدُ أيضاً، فينقضُهُ القاضي، ويقضي على العاقلة ) بالذكر؛ لاحتراز عمّا عداها ، فلا يكون الحكم فيها كذلك ، والحال أنه ليس كذلك، وتقرير الدفع ظاهر غير محتاج إلى التحرير. [١]قوله : وفائدته ؛ دفع دخل مقدر تقريره : أنّ ما ثبت بالكتاب أو السنة أو الإجماع لم يسوع فيه الاجتهاد، فما فائدة التحكيم؟ حاصل الجواب : إن فائدة التحكيم في هذه الصورة أن يكون الحكم لازماً للخصم ، وينفذ الحكم على المحكوم عليه بجبر المحكم. [٢] قوله : وإذا صح ...الخ ؛ قال الصدر الشهيد له في «شرح أدب القضاء») : هو الظاهر عند أصحابنا، وهو الصحيح، لكنَّ مشايخنا امتنعوا عن هذه الفتوى، وقالوا : يحتاج إلى حكم الحاكم، كما في الحدود والقصاص، كيلا يتجاسر العوام فيه. انتهى. [۳] قوله : على العاقلة ؛ خرج به ما إذا كانت على القاتل بأن يثبت القتل بإقراره ، أو ثبتت جراحة ببينته وأرشها أقل مما تحمله العاقلة خطأ كانت الجراحة أو عمداً أو كانت قدر ما تتحمله، ولكن كانت الجراحة عمداً لا توجب القصاص، فينفذ حكمه. كذا في «البحر»)، وغيره.
فإن رفع إلى قاض آخر إن وافق مذهبه أمضاه، وإلا أبطله.
لأنَّ حُكْمَ المحكم مخالف لمذهب القاضي، ومخالف للنَّص، وهو قوله : «قوموا فَدُوه ، ومعنى عدم نفاذه على العاقلةِ أن للمُحَكَمَ لا يكون ولاية طلب الدِّيةِ من العاقلة، وحبسهم إن امتنعوا، فإن رُفِعَ إلى قاض آخر إن وافق مذهبه أمضاه ، وإلا أبطلَهُ ) : أي ليس حُكْمُ المُحَكّم مثلَ حُكْمِ المولى في أن المختلف فيه يصير مجمعاً عليه. [١] قوله : أمضاه ؛ لأنه لا فائدة في نقضه ثم إبرامه على الوجه الذي حكم به المحكم ، وفائدة إمضائه أنه لو رفع إلى حاكم يخالف مذهبه لم يتمكن من نقضه، ولو لم يمض لتمكن ؛ لأنّ إمضاء الأوّل بمنزلة حكم نفسه، كما ذكروه. وإلا أي وإن لم يوافق مذهبه عطله ؛ لأنَّ حكم المحكم لا يلزم القاضي ؛ لعدم التحكيم منه بخلاف حكم الحاكم، فإنّه لا يبطل الثاني، وإن كان مخالفاً لمذهبه ؛ لكون ولايته عامة، فكان قضاؤه حجَّةً في حق الكل فلا يجوز للثاني ردّه.
(1) وهو حديث حمل بن مالك له، ولفظه : عن المغيرة بن شعبة الله قال : ضربت امرأة ضرتها فسطاط وهي حبلى فقتلتها قال : وإحداهما لحيانية ، قال : فجعل رسول الله دية المقتولة على عصبة القاتلة وغرة لما في بطنها، فقال رجل من عصبة القاتلة : أنغرم دية من لا أكل ولا شرب ولا استهل فمثل ذلك يطل ، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : أسجع كسجع الأعراب، قال : وجعل عليهم (الدية في مسلم (۳ : ۱۳۱۰)، واللفظ له، وفي روايات أخرى صرح باسم حمل بن مالك له، وذكرت القصة بطولها وفي مسند أحمد (۱) : ٣٦٤)، و(سنن ابن ماجه (۲ : ۸۸۲) ، وغيرها.
The judgment of the arbitrator in accidental blood (death) concerning the diyah upon the 'aqilah is not enforceable. Similarly, if he judges the diyah upon the killer, it is also not enforceable. So the judge overturns it and judges upon the 'aqilah. Its benefit is binding the opponent. For if two parties in a sale appoint an arbitrator, the arbitrator compels the buyer to deliver the price and the seller to deliver the sold item, and if one refuses, he imprisons him. He mentioned the mujtahadāt (cases requiring ijtihad) to indicate others by a fortiori reasoning.
And if arbitration is valid in all cases, a fatwa is not given to that effect because common people would become audacious to do that, reducing the need for the judge, and then the rulers of Sharia would have no dignity, nor the court beauty and adornment.
The judgment of the arbitrator in accidental blood (death) concerning the diyah upon the 'aqilah is not enforceable because the 'aqilah did not appoint him. Similarly, if he judges the diyah upon the killer, it is also not enforceable. So the judge overturns it and judges upon the 'aqilah. By mentioning only these, he avoids what is other than them, so the ruling is not like that in other cases, and the situation is not so. The exposition of the rebuttal is apparent and does not need writing. [1] His statement: 'Its benefit' is a rebuttal of a presumed objection: What is established by the Book, Sunnah, or Ijma does not allow ijtihad, so what is the benefit of arbitration? The gist of the answer is that the benefit of arbitration in this situation is that the judgment becomes binding upon the opponent, and the judgment is executed on the one against whom it is given by the compulsion of the arbitrator. [2] His statement: 'And if it is valid...etc.'; Al-Sadr al-Shahid said in his 'Sharh Adab al-Qada': It is the apparent position of our companions, and it is correct, but our later scholars refrained from giving fatwa on this, and said: It needs the ruling of a judge, as in hadd and qisas, so that common people do not become audacious. End. [3] His statement: 'Upon the 'aqilah' excludes the case when it is upon the killer, such as when the killing is proven by his confession, or a wound is proven by evidence and its compensation is less than what the 'aqilah bears, whether the wound was accidental or intentional, or it was equal to what the 'aqilah bears but the wound was intentional and does not require qisas; then his judgment is enforced. Thus in 'al-Bahr' and others.
If it is raised to another judge, if it agrees with his school, he upholds it, otherwise he voids it.
Because the judgment of the arbitrator opposes the school of the judge and opposes the text, i.e., his statement: 'Stand and ransom him.' The meaning of its non-enforceability upon the 'aqilah is that the arbitrator has no authority to demand the diyah from the 'aqilah nor to imprison them if they refuse. If it is raised to another judge, if it agrees with his school, he upholds it, otherwise he voids it. That is, the judgment of the arbitrator is not like the judgment of the ruler in that a disputed matter becomes agreed upon. [1] His statement: 'He upholds it' because there is no benefit in overturning it and then confirming it in the manner the arbitrator judged. The benefit of upholding it is that if it is raised to a judge who opposes his school, he cannot overturn it, whereas if he had not upheld it, he could have overturned it, because the first judge's upholding is like his own judgment, as they mentioned. 'Otherwise' i.e., if it does not agree with his school, he voids it because the judgment of the arbitrator is not binding on the judge due to the lack of his appointment, unlike the judgment of a judge, which the second judge does not void even if it opposes his school, because his authority is general, so his judgment is a proof for all, and it is not permissible for the second to reject it.
(1) This is the hadith of Ḥamal ibn Mālik, may Allah be pleased with him, and its wording: From al-Mughīra ibn Shuʿba, may Allah be pleased with him, he said: A woman struck her co-wife with a tent pole while she was pregnant and killed her. He said: One of them was from Lahyān. He said: The Messenger of Allah made the diyah of the killed woman upon the ʿaṣaba (agnates) of the killer and a ghurra for what was in her womb. A man from the ʿaṣaba of the killer said: Should we pay diyah for one who neither ate nor drank nor cried? Such a one should be neglected. The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: Are you speaking rhymed prose like the bedouins? He said: And he made the diyah upon them. In Muslim (3:1310) and the wording is his, and in other narrations the name of Ḥamal ibn Mālik is explicitly mentioned, and the story is related in full in Musnad Ahmad (1:364), Sunan Ibn Mājah (2:882), and others.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn concerning the validity and enforceability of arbitration (tahkim) in Hanafi law, specifically in cases of accidental death (qatl khata') where the diyah is upon the 'aqilah (the tribal surety responsible for paying blood money).

The passage begins by stating a core ruling: The judgment of an arbitrator (muhakkam) in a case of accidental death requiring diyah upon the 'aqilah is not enforceable ('la yanfudh'). Similarly, if he judges the diyah upon the killer himself, it is also not enforceable. The reason given is that the 'aqilah did not appoint the arbitrator; they are third parties not consenting to the arbitration. Therefore, a formal judge (qadi) must overturn the arbitrator's decision and give a proper judgment against the 'aqilah, because the arbitrator lacks authority over them.

What then is the benefit of arbitration? The text explains that its benefit lies in binding the actual litigants who appointed him. For example, in a sale dispute, the arbitrator can compel the buyer to pay the price and the seller to deliver the item, and imprison the one who refuses. The benefit is that the arbitrator's decision is binding between the parties who consented, even though it does not bind third parties like the 'aqilah.

The commentary further clarifies that although arbitration is theoretically valid in all cases (including those requiring ijtihad), later Hanafi scholars (musha'ikh) refrained from issuing fatwas to that effect. They feared that if people commonly resorted to arbitration, they would lose respect for the formal judiciary, and the need for judges would diminish. Therefore, they advised that arbitration in serious matters like homicide should go through the regular court, similar to hadd and qisas penalties, to maintain the dignity of the Sharia courts.

The passage then discusses exceptions: If the arbitrator's judgment is against the killer (e.g., based on confession or clear evidence) and the diyah is on him, it may be enforceable, especially if the injury was intentional but does not require qisas. However, if the judgment goes against the 'aqilah, it remains unenforceable unless the 'aqilah had also agreed to arbitration.

An important point is what happens when the case is raised to another judge. If the second judge's school (madhhab) agrees with the arbitrator's decision, he upholds it (yamdi). If it disagrees, he voids it (yubtilu). This contrasts with the judgment of a formal judge, which is binding even if it contradicts the second judge's school, because the formal judge's authority is general and his judgment is a proof for all.

Finally, the text cites the hadith of Ḥamal ibn Mālik, which is the basis for placing diyah on the 'aqilah in accidental death cases. This hadith is used as evidence for the ruling that the 'aqilah is liable, and thus the arbitrator cannot enforce a decision against them without their consent.

Key Terms
المحكم (al-muhakkam): An arbitrator appointed by disputing parties to resolve their conflict; his authority is limited to those who appointed him.
العاقلة (al-ʿāqilah): The tribal surety or group of male relatives who are liable to pay diyah (blood money) in cases of accidental homicide.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money, a financial compensation paid to the victim's heirs in homicide or injury cases where qisas (retaliation) is not applicable.
التحكيم (al-taḥkīm): The process of appointing an arbitrator to adjudicate a dispute; considered valid but discouraged for public use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason the arbitrator's judgment regarding diyah upon the 'aqilah is not enforceable?
A) The 'aqilah is not legally responsible for accidental killings.
B) The 'aqilah did not consent to the arbitrator's authority.
C) The arbitrator is not a qualified judge.
D) The diyah amount is disputed.

Key Takeaway
An arbitrator's judgment in a case of accidental death is not enforceable against the 'aqilah (tribal surety) because they did not appoint the arbitrator; it only binds the parties who consented to arbitration.

Opening Doors in Curved vs Rectangular Dead-End Alleys
Ruling with proof on door openings in shared dead-end alleys

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on opening doors in curved and rectangular dead-end alleys and distinguish between permission for light/air and prevention for passage.

Core Idea
Shared ownership of a curved alley permits door openings; rectangular alleys are separate property, restricting passage.

The Hanafi school carefully regulates the opening of new doors in shared alleys to protect the rights of all residents. This passage distinguishes between curved (mustadīra) and rectangular (murabba'a) cul-de-sacs, with different rulings based on the nature of shared ownership. We will explore the proof, the difference of opinion, and the crucial distinction between opening a door for passage versus for light and air.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of alleys
Read the passage and locate the two types of dead-end alleys (zā'igha): curved (mustadīra) and rectangular (murabba'a). Note how the author describes their physical layout.
Curved: a single shared street. Rectangular: a street within a street.
2
State the ruling for curved alleys and its proof
The author says it is permissible to open a door anywhere in a curved alley. The proof is that all residents have equal shuf'a (preemption) rights, indicating common ownership. Confirm this from the text.
The proof is from the right of preemption.
3
State the ruling for rectangular alleys and its reasoning
In a rectangular cul-de-sac, residents of the main alley cannot open a door into the rectangular offshoot because it is like a separate street. The reasoning: the residents of the rectangular part have exclusive passage rights; proof is that shuf'a belongs only to them when a house is sold.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference of opinion
The passage mentions a view (qīla) that any owner may open a door because they could demolish the entire wall. The correct view (al-ṣaḥīḥ) is that they are prevented if the door is for passage. Identify the authority (Fakhr al-Islam) and the basis (istihsān).
The relied-upon position uses istihsān to prevent harm.
5
Apply the distinction between passage and light/air
The passage states that if the door is for passage, it is prevented; if for light and air only, it is permitted. Also note that if the alley is a through-passage (nāfidha), there is no prevention. Practice applying this distinction to a scenario.
Through-passage: general public has right of passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدور التي في الزائغة المستديرة فيجوز فتح بابها في تلك السكة، في أي موضع شاء صاحب الدار؛ لأنها سكة واحدة ، وهي بينهم بالشركة، ألا ترى أنّ لهم وجوب الشفعة لهم جميعاً، بخلافِ الدور التي في الزائغة المربعة ؛ فإنَّ الزائغة المربعة كسكة في سكة، فليس لأهل الأولى الفتح في المربعة، فاحفظ. قوله : فتح باب ; لأنَّ الباب يقصد للمرور ، ولا حق لهم في الدخول فيها ; لكونها غير نافذة، وإنّما ذلك لأهلها على الخصوص، ألا ترى أنه لو بيعت دار منها كان حق الشفعة لهم لا لأهل الأولى، بخلاف ما إذا كانت الزائغة المنشعبة نافذة ; لأنَّ الاستطراق حق العامة، وهم من جملتهم. وقيل : لا يمنعون من فتح الباب ; لأنَّ له أن يرفع جداره كله بالهدم ، فأولى أن يكون له نقض البعض، والصحيح هو الأوّل ، ولكن هذا فيما إذا أراد أن يفتح الباب للمرور، فإنه يمنع استحساناً ، وإذا أراد به الاستضاءة والريح دون المرور لم يمنع من ذلك ، كذا نقل فخر الإسلام عن الفقيه أبي جعفر . كذا في «الرمز»، وغيره. قوله : في القصوى ; أي البعدى ، وهي المنشعبة من الأولى الغير النافذة، أما النافذة فلا يمنع من الفتح فيها ; لأنَّ لكلّ واحدٍ حق المرور فيها. قوله : لهم ذلك ; أي فتح باب في حائطه في أي جهة شاء ; لأن الزائغة لما كانت مستديرة، وهي التي فيها اعوجاج حتى يبلغ عوجها رأس السكة صارت كلتاهما سكة واحدة مشتركة، ولكلّ واحدٍ منهم حق المرور في كلّها، ولهذا لو بيعت فيها دار كانت الشفعة للكلِّ على السواء، فبفتح الباب لا يحدث لنفسه حقاً فلا يمنع. في القصوى، وفي مستديرة لزق طرفاها بالمستطيلة لهم ذلك في القصوى : أي في المنشعبة من الأولى. وقوله : لزق طرفاها : أي اتصل طرفاها بالمستطيلة، والمراد بطرفيها نهاية سعتها، وهذا إذا كانت مثل نصف دائرة أو أقلّ حتّى لو كانت أكثر من ذلك لا يَفْتَح فيها الباب، فلنصور صورتين في الأولى يكون له فتح الباب دون الثانية ، والفرق أنَّ الأولى تصير ساحةً مشتركة بخلاف الثانيةِ ، فَإِنَّهُ إذا كان داخلها أوسعَ من مدخلها يصير موضعاً آخرَ غير تابع للأُولَي.
As for houses in the curved cul-de-sac, it is permissible to open their door in that street, in any place the owner of the house wishes, because it is one street, and it is shared among them. Do you not see that they all have the right of preemption? Contrary to houses in the rectangular cul-de-sac; for the rectangular cul-de-sac is like a street within a street, so the residents of the first [street] do not have the right to open a door in the rectangular one, so remember. He said: Opening a door, because the door is intended for passage, and they have no right to enter it because it is not through-passage, rather that right belongs specifically to its residents. Do you not see that if a house there were sold, the right of preemption belongs to them, not to the residents of the first [street]? Contrary to when the branching cul-de-sac is through-passage, because the right of passage belongs to the general public, and they are among them. And it is said: They are not prevented from opening the door, because he may demolish his entire wall, so a fortiori he may demolish part of it. But the correct view is the first. However, this is when he intends to open the door for passage; then he is prevented by istihsan. If he intends it for light and air without passage, he is not prevented from that. Such was transmitted by Fakhr al-Islam from al-Faqih Abu Ja'far. Thus in al-Ramz and others. He said: 'In the far one,' meaning the remote one, which branches off from the first non-through one. As for the through one, there is no prevention from opening a door in it, because each person has the right of passage in it. He said: 'They have that,' meaning opening a door in his wall in any direction he wishes, because since the cul-de-sac is curved, i.e., it has curvature such that its curvature reaches the head of the street, both become one shared street, and each of them has the right of passage through all of it. For this reason, if a house there is sold, the right of preemption belongs to all equally. By opening a door, he does not create a right for himself, so he is not prevented. In the far one, and in a curved one whose both ends adjoin the elongated one, they have that in the far one: meaning in the branch from the first. And his saying: 'its both ends adjoin' means its two ends connect with the elongated one, and what is meant by its two ends is the extent of its width. This is when it is like a half circle or less; if it were more than that, he does not open a door in it. So we can depict two cases: in the first, he has the right to open a door, but not in the second. The difference is that the first becomes a shared courtyard, unlike the second, because when its interior is wider than its entrance, it becomes another place not subordinate to the first.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding opening doors in dead-end alleys (al-zā'igha). It first establishes the general rule for curved (mustadīra) cul-de-sacs: a house owner may open a door anywhere in the wall adjacent to the alley because the entire alley is shared property. The proof is that all residents have equal right of shuf'a (preemption) in the alley, indicating common ownership. The rectangular (murabba'a) cul-de-sac is treated differently: it is like a street within a street, so the primary alley residents cannot open a door into the rectangular offshoot. The commentary explains that if the alley is a through-passage (nāfidha), the general public has right of passage, so residents of the branching alley may open doors. There is a cited difference of opinion: some say any resident may open a door since they could demolish the entire wall; but the correct view (al-ṣaḥīḥ) is that they are prevented if the door is for passage (istihsānan). If only for light and air, it is permitted. Further, if the curved cul-de-sac forms a semicircle or less where both ends meet the main alley, it is a shared space; opening a door does not create new rights. If the interior is wider than the entrance, it becomes a separate place not subordinate, and the door is not allowed.

Key Terms
الزائغة (al-zā'igha): A dead-end alley branching off a main street; a cul-de-sac.
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): The right of preemption; the right to purchase a neighbor's jointly-owned property before a stranger.
استحسان (istihsān): Juristic preference; departing from qiyas for a stronger reason, here used to prevent door-opening for passage to prevent harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can a house owner in a curved cul-de-sac (mustadīra) open a door in any direction along the alley?
A) Because the alley is through-passage and owned by the public.
B) Because the alley is a single shared property among all residents, giving all equal passage rights.
C) Because the owner may demolish his entire wall, so he may open a partial hole.
D) Because the alley is rectangular and thus has its own rules.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, opening a door in a shared dead-end alley is governed by the type of alley and the purpose: curved alleys permit openings for light/air, but prevent for passage by istihsān; rectangular alleys restrict outside residents entirely.

Opening Doors in Curved Alleys: The Semicircle Condition
Explaining when a curved alley's residents may open doors in the far end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on opening doors in a curved alley (mustadirah) attached to a main street (mustatilah) and apply the condition that the curved portion must be a semicircle or less for permission.

Core Idea
A curved alley whose two ends touch the main street is treated as part of it only if the curve is a semicircle or less; otherwise, it becomes a separate area.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains a key Hanafi rule on shared rights of passage in alleys. It distinguishes between a curved alley that functions as an extension of the main street and one that becomes an independent location. Understanding this distinction is essential for property disputes over doors and rights of way.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
The passage concerns a curved alley (mustadirah) whose two ends are attached to a straight main alley (mustatilah). The ruling: residents may open doors in the far end (al-quswa).
2
Understand the condition
This permission applies only if the curved portion is like a semicircle or less. If the curve is more than a semicircle, no door may be opened.
3
Grasp the reasoning (ta'lil)
If the curve is a semicircle or less, the interior functions as a shared courtyard (saha mushtarakah). If more, the interior becomes a separate place not subordinate to the main alley.
4
Apply the rule
Scenario 1 (semicircle or less): door opening allowed. Scenario 2 (more than semicircle): door opening not allowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في القصوى، وفي مستديرة لزق طرفاها بالمستطيلة لهم ذلك في القصوى ) : أي في المنشعبة من الأولى. وقوله : لزق طرفاها : أي اتصل طرفاها بالمستطيلة، والمراد بطرفيها نهاية سعتها، وهذا إذا كانت مثل نصف دائرة أو أقلّ حتّى لو كانت أكثر من ذلك لا يَفْتَح فيها الباب، فلنصور صورتين في الأولى يكون له فتح الباب دون الثانية ، والفرق أنَّ الأولى تصير ساحةً مشتركة بخلاف الثانيةِ ، فَإِنَّهُ إذا كان داخلها أوسعَ من مدخلها يصير موضعاً آخرَ غير تابع للأُولَي.
In the far end: And in a curved alley whose two ends are attached to the straight alley, they have that [right] in the far end. That is, in the branch from the first. His statement: 'its two ends are attached' means its two ends are connected to the straight alley, and by its two ends is meant the extent of its width. This applies if it is like a semicircle or less; if it is more than that, no door may be opened in it. So we depict two scenarios: in the first, he may open a door, but not in the second. The difference is that the first becomes a shared courtyard, unlike the second, because if its interior is wider than its entrance, it becomes another place not subordinate to the first.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn ruling about opening doors in alleys. It explains that a curved alley (mustadirah) whose two ends are attached to a main straight alley (mustatilah) is treated as part of the same alley. Residents may open doors in the far end (quswa) of the curved alley. However, this applies only if the curved part is a semicircle or less. If it is more than a semicircle, the interior becomes a separate private area, not a shared right-of-way, so no door may be opened. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that when the two ends touch the main alley, the entire curved area functions as a shared courtyard (saha mushtarakah), giving all residents right of passage. But if the interior is wider than the entrance, it becomes a distinct location not subservient to the first alley. This is a Hanafi ruling on property and shared rights.

Key Terms
مستديرة (mustadirah): A curved alley that is not straight, like a semicircle.
مستطيلة (mustatilah): A straight main thoroughfare alley.
القصوى (al-quswa): The far end of the curved alley, the point farthest from the entrance.
ساحة مشتركة (saha mushtarakah): A shared courtyard or open space accessible to multiple property owners.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition determines whether a door may be opened in a curved alley (mustadirah) attached to a straight alley?
A) If the curved alley is longer than the straight alley.
B) If the curved portion is a semicircle or less.
C) If the curved portion is more than a semicircle.
D) If the straight alley is a dead end.

Key Takeaway
A curved alley attached to a main street permits door opening in the far end only if the curve is a semicircle or less; otherwise, it becomes a separate area.

The Charity Vow and Basic Needs: Surplus Principle
Hanafi ruling on vow to give charity: needs vs surplus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a charity vow when the vower has only essential property, and distinguish the relied-upon position (istihsan) from Imam Zufar's analogical view.

Core Idea
A vow to give charity only binds surplus wealth, not the basic necessities of life.

When a person vows to give charity from his property, does he have to give everything he owns, even if that means leaving himself and his family without basic sustenance? The Hanafi school answers with a nuanced ruling based on istihsan (juristic preference), differentiating the vow from a will and drawing an analogy to the obligation of zakat. This passage from the Hidayah commentary presents the ruling, the textual proof, and the intra-madhhab dispute with Imam Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario and the ruling
The scenario: a person vows to give charity from his property but only possesses enough for his basic needs (qut). The Hanafi ruling: he may keep his sustenance and later, when he acquires new wealth, give charity equal to that amount.
The amount kept is not fixed; it depends on the person's circumstances.
2
Locate the evidence (dalil) for the ruling
The ruling is based on istihsan, which uses an analogy to the obligation of zakat. In the Qur'an, 'wealth' (amwal) in verses like 'in their wealth is a known right' is understood to mean surplus wealth. Similarly, the servant's vow is interpreted as referring to surplus.
The underlying 'illa (effective cause) is that basic needs take priority over charity.
3
Understand the opposing view (Imam Zufar)
Imam Zufar held that the vow applies to all property, including necessities, based on the general wording 'from my property' and an analogy to a will (wasiyyah), which also covers all property.
Zufar's view is qiyas (analogy) based on the literal meaning.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Zufar
The Hanafi response distinguishes the vow from a will: the will is a brother of inheritance, which covers all property, but the vow is an act of worship analogous to Allah's obligation (zakat). Since zakat applies only to surplus, so does the vow.
This is the key distinction: 'ibadah vs. property transfer after death.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to the Hanafi ruling, the vower keeps his basic necessities and only gives from surplus. According to Zufar, the vower must give everything, even essentials. The Hanafi ruling is relied upon (mufta bihi).
This protects the vower from destitution and begging.

Source Text

فإن لم يجد إلا ذلك أمسك منه قوته ، فإذا ملك تصدق بما أخذ، ولم يقدر بشيء لاختلاف أحوال الناس (وعند زفر وَ الله يَقَعُ على كلِّ شيءٍ كما في الوصية قضية لإطلاق اللفظ ، ونحن اعتبرنا إيجاب العبدِ بإيجاب الله تعالى. (فإن لم يجد إلا ذلك أمسك منه قوته ، فإذا ملك تصدق بما أخذ، ولم يقدر بشيء لاختلاف أحوال الناس ) ألا ترى أنه يقال : ملك النكاح ، وملك القصاص، وملك المتعة، والمالُ لا يطلقُ على ما ليس بمال ، فإذا كان لفظ الملكِ أعمّ يتناولُ جميع ما يتصدق به، كما لو نص عليه. لكن الصحيح أن لفظ الملك والمال سواء في الحكم، وهو الاختصاص بالأموال الزكاتية ؛ لأنَّ الظاهر من حال الناذر التزام الصدقة من فاضل ماله ، فإنَّ الحياة مظنَّةً الحاجة إلى ما تقوم به حوائجه الأصلية، والمالُ الفاضل عن حوائجه الأصلية هو مال الزكاة، فيختصان به (۱). (۳) قوله : فإن لم يجد ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا لم يكن له مال سوى ما دخل تحت الإيجاب يمسك . إذا وَكُل رجلاً بالبيع من ذلك قدر قوته ، فإذا أصاب شيئاً من المال بعد ذلك تصدق بمثل ما أمسك ؛ لأنَّ حاجته مقدّمة، ولو لم يمسك قدر حاجتِه لَتَكَفَّفَ النَّاسَ من ساعته، وليس من الحكمة أن يتصدق بما عنده ثمَّ يتكفف الناس من ساعته. Chapter of Judgment, Section of Arbitration and Judgment Regarding Inheritances. If he does not find except that, he keeps from it his sustenance, then when he becomes wealthy, he gives charity with what he took, and it is not estimated with anything due to the different conditions of people. (And according to Zufar: it applies to everything, as in the will, as a requirement of the general application of the wording. And we considered the servant's obligation by the obligation of Allah Almighty. (If he does not find except that, he keeps from it his sustenance, then when he becomes wealthy, he gives charity with what he took, and it is not estimated with anything due to the difference in people's conditions.) Do you not see that it is said: ownership of marriage, ownership of retaliation, ownership of temporary marriage, and property is not applied to what is not property. So if the word 'ownership' is more general, encompassing everything given in charity, it is as if he explicitly stated it. But the correct view is that the words 'ownership' and 'property' are the same in ruling, and that is specification with zakatable assets; because the apparent state of the vower is that he commits to charity from the surplus of his property. Life is a time of need for what sustains his basic necessities, and the wealth surplus to his basic necessities is zakatable wealth, so they are specific to it. [1] His statement: And according to Zufar, in both cases it applies to everything from his wealth, whether from zakatable assets or not, so he must give all in charity, and that is analogy as a requirement of the general application of the wording; meaning, due to the general import of what he said, for the word 'wealth' encompasses everything. Allah said: Do not consume your wealth among yourselves unjustly. [2] His statement: And we considered... this is the face of istihsan, and its explanation: The obligation of the servant is considered by the obligation of Allah; for the servant does not initiate obligation lest it lead to partnership. And the absolute 'wealth' in the chapter of charity is directed to some, as in His statement: in their wealth is a known right and His statement: take from their wealth a charity to purify them. Likewise what the servant obligates upon himself, unlike the will, for the will is a sister of inheritance in that both establish ownership after death, and Allah has obligated inheritance in every specific property and debt, and the will is not specific to one wealth without another. (3) His statement: If he does not find... etc.; meaning, if he has no wealth other than what falls under the obligation, he keeps. If he appoints a man to sell from that the amount of his sustenance, then when he gets some wealth after that, he gives charity the like of what he kept; because his need is prior, and had he not kept the amount of his need, he would have begged people immediately, and it is not wise to give charity with what he has and then beg people immediately. [1] His statement: It was said... etc.; it was not clarified in al-Mabsut the amount he keeps; because that differs with the difference of dependents, and with the difference of what is newly acquired by him. So some craftsmen get their output every day, some every three days, some more, some less, and likewise merchants, and farmers have their harvest newly acquired. [2] His statement: al-Mustaghall; a passive participle from istighlal, or a locative adverb, and istighlal means: taking yield, seeking to bring yield, having yield, etc. (as defined in al-Sirah). And it is said in Dhakirat al-'Uqba: The owner of the mustaghall is the owner of the yield who owns houses, shops, and homes that he rents out monthly; because his hand reaches what he spends month after month. End.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on a person who makes a vow (nadhr) to give charity from his property but only possesses enough for his basic needs. The ruling: he may keep from that property an amount sufficient for his sustenance (qut), and later when he acquires additional wealth, he must give charity equal to what he kept. The amount to keep is not fixed but depends on his circumstances and the needs of his dependents. The passage also records a dispute: Imam Zufar (from the Hanafi school) held that the vow applies to all his property, including necessities, based on the general wording of the vow; this is the qiyas (analogy) view. The Hanafi position (the relied-upon view) is istihsan (juristic preference): the servant's obligation is analogized to Allah's obligation in the matter of charity, where the word 'property' in the Qur'anic verses (e.g., 'in their wealth is a known right') is understood to refer to surplus wealth, not basic necessities. This is because life has necessities, and the apparent intention of the vower is to give from surplus, not essential needs. Therefore, the vow is interpreted as referring to zakatable assets (amwal zakawiyyah), i.e., wealth exceeding basic needs. The commentary further explains that the distinction between will (wasiyyah) and vow: will is like inheritance, covering all property, so a vow may not be analogized to will. The passage also discusses the temporal amounts to keep for craftsmen, merchants, and landowners, based on the frequency of their income.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow whereby a person obligates himself to perform an act of worship (like charity) if a certain condition is met or unconditionally.
مال (mal): Property; in this context, it refers to what a person owns, but in the verse and vow it is interpreted as surplus wealth (mal fadil).
أموال زكاتية (amwal zakawiyyah): Zakatable wealth; property that exceeds one's basic needs and is subject to zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school reject the analogy to a will (wasiyyah) in the context of a charity vow?
A) Because wills are not obligatory.
B) Because a will is a brother of inheritance and covers all property, whereas the vow is an act of worship analogized to zakat.
C) Because the vow is always conditional.
D) Because the vower is not allowed to make a will.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a vow to give charity from one's property is interpreted as referring only to surplus wealth beyond basic needs, based on istihsan and the analogy to zakat.

Conditions for Valid Notification of Agent Dismissal: The 'Adl Requirement and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains when notification of dismissal requires 'adl and 'adad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for valid notification of agent dismissal in the Hanafi school and explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the report of a sinner.

Core Idea
A notification of agent dismissal requires one just person or two chaste persons because it is part imposition.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya addresses a practical question: when does an agent become bound by a notification of dismissal? The answer depends on the nature of the legal act and the reliability of the informant. The author distinguishes between pure imposition (testimony) and pure permission (appointment), placing dismissal in an intermediate category.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling for notification of dismissal
Identify the condition: the informant must be an 'adl (just person) or two masturan (chaste persons). Without this, the notification is not legally effective.
2
Understand the rationale based on ilzam
The author argues that dismissal is 'imposition from one aspect' because it removes the agent's authority, causing harm. Therefore it requires some element of confirmation, like testimony.
3
Compare with appointment (tawkeel)
Appointment has no element of imposition; it is a pure transaction. Hence only general capacity (tamyiz) is needed, not witness qualities.
4
Examine the analogous cases
The same rule applies to slave jirah, pre-emption, virgin's consent, and obligations of a non-emigrant Muslim. In each, if the informant is 'adl or two masturan, the legal effect follows; otherwise not.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Abu Hanifa insists on the 'adl or two masturan condition; the Sahibayn accept a single fasiq's report as sufficient for knowledge. Note the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf).
The marginal note (1) clarifies the three positions.

Source Text

أي إذا عزل الموكل الوكيل، فأخبره بذلك عدل أو مستورانِ لا يصح تصرفه بعد ذلك، ولو أخبره فاسق أو مستور الحال لا اعتبار لإخباره حتَّى يجوز تصرفه ، وكذا إذا جَنَى عبد خطأ فَعَلِمَ السَّيِّدُ بجنايتِهِ بإخبارِ عدل أو مستورين، فباعَ السَّيِّدُ عبده يكون مختاراً للفداء، وكذا إذا عَلِمَ الشَّفيع بيعَ الدَّارَ فسكت إن أخبرَهُ عدل أو مستوران، يكون سكوته تسليماً، وكذا في علم البكر بإنكاحها إذا سكتت، والمسلم الذي لم يهاجر إذا أخبرَهُ عدل أو مستورانِ يَحِبُ عليه الشَّرائع.
أمَّا صحَّةُ التَّوكيل فلا يشترط لها ذلك حتَّى إذا أخبره فاسق بأن فلاناً وكُلَهُ بالبيع، فباع، يجوز بيعه ؛ وذلك لأنَّه إنما يشترط العدد والعدالة في الشَّهادة ؛ لأنها إلزام محض فلا بُدَّ من التأكيد ، أمَّا التَّوكيل فليس فيه معنى الإلزام أصلا، فلا يشترط فيه شيء من وصفي الشهادة: أي العدد والعدالة.
وأما عزل الوكيل ونحوه فإلزام من وجه دون وجه ، فمن حيث إِنَّهُ لا يَبْقَى له ولاية التصرف يكون إلزام ضرر، ومن حيث أنَّ الموكل يتصرف في حق نفسه بالعزل ليس بالزام، فيشترط له أحد وصفى الشهادة.
(1) قوله : وكذا...الخ ؛ حاصله : أنه لو أخبر فاسق للسيد بأنَّ عبده جنى خطأ فباع أو عتق لا يصير مختاراً للفداء عنده ، وعندهما يصير. والشفيع إذا سكت بعدما أخبره فاسق بالبيع لا يكون تاركاً للشفعة . عنده ، وهما يكون وإذا أخبر فاسق البكر فسكتت لا تصير راضية بالنكاح عنده ، خلافاً لهما. ومن أسلم في دار الحرب ولم يهاجر إلينا فأخبره بالشرائع فاسق لا يؤخذ عنده ، خلافاً لهما. ويشترط سائر الشروط في الشاهد مع العددِ أو العدالة على قول الإمام، فلا يثبت بخبر [المرأة والعبد والصبي وإن وجد العدد أو العدالة، وهذا مقيد بأن يكون المخبر غير الخصم ورسوله، فلا يشترط فيه العدالة، حتى لو أخبر الشفيع المشتري بنفسه وجب الطلب إجماعاً. والرسول يعمل بخبره وإن كان فاسقاً اتفاقاً صدقه أو كذبه. كما ذكره الإسبيجايي. وكذا لو كان الرسول صغيراً، وظاهرُ ما في «العماديّة»: إنّه لا بُدَّ من أن يقول له إِنِّي رسول بعزلك. كذا في «منح الغفار) ، وغيره.
(۲) قوله : لأنه...الخ ؛ حاصله أن التوكيل من المعاملات فلا يشترط فيه إلا التمييز.
That is, if the principal dismisses the agent and a just person or two chaste persons inform him of that, his subsequent action is invalid. But if a sinner or a person of unknown status informs him, his information is not considered, so his action remains valid. Similarly, if a slave commits a wrongful homicide and the master learns of his crime through the information of a just person or two chaste persons, then if the master sells the slave, he becomes bound by the option of ransom. Likewise, if the pre-emptor learns of the sale of the house and remains silent after being informed by a just person or two chaste persons, his silence is considered consent. Similarly, regarding the virgin's knowledge of her marriage if she remains silent, and the Muslim who has not emigrated—if a just person or two chaste persons inform him, the religious obligations become binding upon him. As for the validity of appointment (tawkeel), such conditions are not required. Thus, if a sinner informs him that so-and-so appointed him to sell, and he sells, the sale is valid. This is because number and integrity are required only in testimony, as it is pure imposition and must be confirmed. But appointment (tawkeel) has no element of imposition at all, so none of the two qualities of testimony (i.e., number and integrity) are required. As for dismissal of the agent and the like, it is imposition from one aspect and not from another. Since he retains no authority to act, it is a harmful imposition; but since the principal acts in his own right by dismissing, it is not an imposition. So one of the two qualities of testimony is required. (1) His saying: 'Similarly etc.'; its summary: If a sinner informs the master that his slave committed wrongful homicide and he sells or manumits, then according to him (Imam Abu Hanifa) he does not become bound by the option of ransom, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he does. And if the pre-emptor remains silent after a sinner informs him of the sale, he is not considered relinquishing the right of pre-emption according to him, but according to the two he is. And if a sinner informs a virgin and she remains silent, she is not considered consenting to marriage according to him, contrary to the two. And if someone becomes Muslim in dar al-harb and does not emigrate to us, and a sinner informs him of the religious obligations, he is not held to them according to him, contrary to the two. And all other conditions of the witness are required along with number or integrity according to Imam, so it is not established by the report of a woman, slave, or minor even if number or integrity exist. This is restricted to when the informer is not the opponent or his messenger, so integrity is not required there. Thus, if the pre-emptor is himself informed by the buyer, demand is obligatory by consensus. And the messenger's report is acted upon even if he is a sinner, according to agreement, whether he is believed or not, as mentioned by al-Isbijabi. Similarly, if the messenger is a minor, and the apparent meaning of what is in al-Imadiyya is that he must say, 'I am a messenger with your dismissal.' So in Manh al-Ghaffar etc. (2) His saying: 'Because etc.'; its summary: Appointment (tawkeel) is a transaction, so only discernment is required therein.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya, explaining the conditions for a valid notification of dismissal of an agent. The author first states the ruling: if the principal dismisses the agent, and a just person (adl) or two chaste persons (masturan) inform the agent of the dismissal, then the agent's subsequent actions are invalid. However, if a sinner (fasiq) or a person of unknown status informs him, the notification is not considered, so the agent's actions remain valid. The author then provides analogous cases: a slave's jirah, pre-emption (shuf'a), consent of a virgin in marriage, and obligations of a non-emigrant Muslim — all follow the same rule. The underlying principle is that dismissal ('uzl) is partly an imposition (ilzam) because it removes the agent's authority (harmful), so it requires some confirmation (adl or two witnesses). In contrast, appointment (tawkeel) is purely a transaction (mu'amala) with no element of imposition; hence only general capacity (tamyiz) is needed, not witness qualities. The marginal note (1) clarifies the intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa requires two just witnesses or one just person? Actually, the text says 'adl or masturan', meaning one just or two chaste. But the note indicates a difference: for Imam, a single fasiq's report is insufficient; for Sahibayn, it suffices. The note also mentions that other conditions of witness (like not being a woman, slave, or minor) apply according to Imam. However, if the informer is the opponent himself or his messenger, integrity is not required by consensus. The second marginal note (2) reinforces that tawkeel requires only tamyiz. The passage thus clarifies the hierarchy of evidentiary requirements based on the nature of the legal act.

Key Terms
عدل ('adl): A person who is just (possesses 'adala), i.e., upright in religious observance and honorable in social dealings, avoiding major sins and not persisting in minor sins.
مستور (mastur): A person of chaste and hidden status, i.e., whose outward condition is upright and not known to be sinful, though not necessarily proven just. Two such persons can substitute for one 'adl in certain notifications.
إلزام (ilzam): Imposition or obligation; a legal act that binds someone against their will, requiring strict proof.
توكيل (tawkeel): Appointment of an agent; a contract by which the principal authorizes the agent to act on his behalf.
فاسق (fasiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, lacking 'adala.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the condition of 'adl or masturan required for notification of dismissal of an agent but not for appointment?
A) Because dismissal is a form of testimony and appointment is a transaction
B) Because dismissal is an imposition from one aspect, while appointment has no imposition
C) Because dismissal is a harmful act, while appointment is beneficial
D) Because dismissal involves a change in ownership, while appointment does not

Key Takeaway
Notification of agent dismissal requires one 'adl or two masturan because it is partially an imposition; a fasiq's report is insufficient according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Women's Testimony on Hidden Matters: One Woman Suffices
Explaining witness numbers for women's hidden matters and other cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts the testimony of one woman for matters hidden from men, and evaluate the proofs of opposing schools.

Core Idea
One woman's testimony suffices for hidden matters because necessity drops both maleness and number requirements.

In cases where only women can observe certain bodily conditions—like virginity, childbirth, or private defects—the Hanafi school uniquely accepts the testimony of a single upright woman. This ruling is rooted in Prophetic practice and a distinctive usul principle regarding generic plurals. In this lesson, we examine the proofs, the inter-madhhab debate, and the practical consequences for Islamic court procedure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Pinpoint the exact cases where Hanafi law accepts a single woman's testimony (virginity, childbirth, women's defects) and those where it does not (e.g., defects men can see). This is an exception to the general rule of two men or one man+two women.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof from hadith
Study the text of the hadith from Ibn Shihab al-Zuhri: 'The sunnah is that the testimony of women is permitted in what no one else can see...' Understand how the Hanafi school infers that the plural 'women' includes the singular minimum. The usul principle: al-jam' al-muhalla bi alif lam li al-jins yafid al-umum wa yatanaulu al-aqall.
Wajh al-dalalah: the definite plural indicates the genus, so it covers even one woman.
3
Examine the opposing positions
Al-Shafi'i requires four women (analogy to one man = two women). Ibn Abi Layla requires two women (maleness dropped, number kept). Understand their reasoning and supporting evidence. The text also attributes the two-woman view to Malik and al-Thawri.
These positions are based on applying the general rule of two women equivalent to one man.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response to the opponents
The Hanafis argue that the underlying rationale for dropping maleness is necessity (privacy). This same necessity justifies dropping the number requirement because one woman's observation is sufficient. Two women are merely precautionary, not required.
The principle of 'akhaf' (lighter) applies: one woman's glance is less intrusive than two's.
5
Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In a Hanafi court, one female witness is enough; in a Shafi'i court, four are needed; in a Maliki court (according to this report), two. This affects the feasibility of proving such claims.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

[1] قوله: امرأة؛ واحدة بشرط الحريَّةِ والعقل والإسلام والبلوغ، والأحوط امرأتان، والأحب ثلاث، والمخرج عن الخلاف الطبع، ولفظ الشهادة يشترط عند مشایخ خراسان، لا عند مشايخ العراق. Book of Testimony and Retraction / Its definition, its required number, and what the witness bears. And for virginity, childbirth, and women's defects in matters that men do not have access to. We said: Consideration is for the generality of the wording, not for the specific cause, as has been proven in [other] places. No right is established legally by witnesses more than two except the hadd of zina, so the establishment of all other rights is determined by two witnesses, and from among other rights are the rest of the hudud, so they are established by the testimony of two men. And the author of al-Manh added to the rest of the hudud: the Islam of a male disbeliever and the apostasy of a Muslim, for in these two, only the testimony of two men is accepted, so the testimony of one man and two women is accepted for her Islam. [1] His saying: 'and for virginity...etc.' — because 'Abd al-Razzaq narrated in his Musannaf from Ibn Shihab al-Zuhri who said: The sunnah is that the testimony of women is permitted in what no one other than them can access, concerning women's childbirths and their defects.
