And Hudhayfah said: The Messenger of Allah allowed the testimony of the midwife over childbirth. And al-Shafi'i said: Four are required, and it is the saying of 'Ata, because every two women stand in place of one man in testimony. And Ibn Abi Layla said: Two are required, because when maleness was dropped, the number remained considered. And al-Shumunni attributed it to Malik and al-Thawri. The proof against al-Shafi'i and Ibn Abi Layla is what we narrated from the hadith of Ibn Shihab al-Zuhri, and because maleness dropped only due to glancing; because the glance of one gender is lighter, so also the number is dropped because the glance of one woman is lighter than the glance of two, except that two is more precautionary, because it contains an element of compulsion. One woman, and for other than that: property or non-property, like marriage, fosterage, divorce, agency, will: two men or one man and two women. He only said this because women's defects, if they are such as men can access, like an extra finger for example, the testimony of one woman does not suffice. (And for other than that: property or non-property, like marriage, fosterage, divorce, agency, will: two men or one man and two women): He only said 'property or non-property' because there is disagreement with al-Shafi'i, for non-property cases do not accept the testimony of one man and two women according to him; rather, this is specific to property. [2] And al-Shumunni mentioned: If a man testifies about childbirth by saying 'I responded and my glance fell upon her,' it is accepted if he is upright. If he said 'I deliberately looked,' it is not accepted. And some of al-Shafi'i's companions said this, and some of our scholars said it is also accepted, and some of al-Shafi'i's companions said this. And the testimony of women regarding the cry of an infant is not accepted according to Abu Hanifa with respect to inheritance, but it is accepted with respect to prayer, because the cry is an audible sound, and men and women are equal in it, so it is among matters that men can access, even if they are not present; their testimony becomes like their testimony regarding women's injuries in baths, unlike childbirth, because it is the separation of the child from the mother, so men do not access it. And prayer is among religious matters, and the report of one woman is an evidence in that, like her testimony on the crescent of Ramadan. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: It is accepted regarding inheritance as well, and al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad said so, because men customarily do not attend that place to hear his voice, so it becomes like their testimony on childbirth. [1] His saying: 'one woman' — one woman with the condition of freedom, sanity, Islam, and puberty. And the precautionary is two women, and the preferred is three, and the way out of disagreement is based on nature. The wording of testimony is required according to the scholars of Khurasan, not according to the scholars of Iraq. [2] His saying: 'It is not accepted...etc.' — the principle is non-acceptance of their testimony due to deficiency of intellect.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage from the Hanafi fiqh work 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah' is a commentary expanding on the matn of al-Wiqayah. It addresses the required number of witnesses in various situations within the law of testimony (shahada). The central ruling is that for matters that men are not normally exposed to — such as virginity, childbirth, and private defects of women — the testimony of a single upright woman is sufficient. This is proven by the hadith of Ibn Shihab al-Zuhri and the specific practice of the Prophet allowing the testimony of a midwife. The Hanafi school argues that since the requirement of maleness was dropped due to the necessity of access, the requirement of multiple women is also dropped because one woman's observation is lighter than two's. However, two women are considered more precautionary. The text then contrasts this with the opinion of al-Shafi'i, who requires four women (analogizing two women to one man), and Ibn Abi Layla, who requires two women (maintaining the number requirement). The Hanafi proof refutes both. The passage then moves to general testimony for property and non-property cases (marriage, divorce, etc.), where the standard is two men or one man and two women. Here, al-Shafi'i restricts the one man-two women format to property only, while the Hanafis extend it to non-property as well. A notable sub-issue is a man accidentally witnessing childbirth: if he claims he did not intend to look, his testimony is accepted; if he deliberately looked, it is not, as it would be sinful. Regarding the cry of a newborn (istihlal) for inheritance, Abu Hanifa does not accept women's testimony because the cry is audible and theoretically accessible to men, analogizing it to injuries in baths. But for prayer (e.g., determining death for funeral prayer), he accepts it, as religious matters are open to a woman's single report. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad accept it for inheritance too, since men are typically absent at childbirth. This intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is noted, with the Sahibayn's view aligning with the majority of other schools. The commentary concludes with conditions for the single woman witness: she must be free, sane, Muslim, and adult. While one woman suffices in the Hanafi school, two is precautionary and three better to avoid dispute. The formal wording of testimony is required according to Khurasan scholars but not Iraq scholars. The final note reiterates the general principle that women's testimony is not accepted due to deficiency of intellect, except where specified.

Key Terms
الشهادة (al-shahādah): In this passage, shahada refers to the legal testimony given in court to establish rights or claims.
نصاب الشهادة (niṣāb al-shahādah): The required number of witnesses for the validity of testimony in various cases.
الاستهلال (al-istihlāl): The cry of a newborn baby after birth, used as evidence of live birth for inheritance and prayer purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Four women
B) Two women
C) One woman
D) Three women

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on the hadith's generic plural to accept one woman's testimony in private female matters, overriding the general rule of two witnesses.

Number of Witnesses for Tazkiyah and Translation of Witnesses
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tazkiyah and ruling on translation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the number of witnesses required for secret and open tazkiyah, explain the distinction between tazkiyah and testimony, and identify the ruling on translating witnesses.

Core Idea
Tazkiyah is not testimony; one witness suffices in secret tazkiyah, and translation of witnesses requires two for caution.

The validity of witness testimony often depends on the character of the witness, which is verified through tazkiyah. However, is tazkiyah itself considered testimony? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on how many validators are needed. Additionally, when a witness speaks a language the judge does not understand, translation becomes necessary. This lesson unpacks the ruling on the number of witnesses for tazkiyah and the caution required in translating testimony.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish secret vs open tazkiyah
Identify the two contexts: tazkiyah done privately (secret) and tazkiyah done in court (open). The ruling differs between them.
Open tazkiyah is agreed to require two witnesses by consensus; the dispute is only about secret tazkiyah.
2
State the positions of Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad
For secret tazkiyah, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say one witness suffices; Muhammad al-Shaybani requires two. Understand the evidence each side uses.
Muhammad analogizes tazkiyah to testimony; the other two reject the analogy.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to Muhammad's analogy
The text argues tazkiyah is not like testimony because it lacks formal wording and court session; the requirement of two in testimony is textual (nass), not analogical, so it cannot be extended.
The analogy to testimony would suggest one just person suffices, but the nass overrides that. The nass is exclusive to testimony.
4
Understand the rule on translation of witnesses
Translation of a witness's statement is permissible, but two translators are 'more cautious' (ahwat). This applies to translating the plaintiff and defendant as well.
The term 'tarjamah' means explaining or rendering into another language. The translator must be trustworthy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وترجمة الشاهد والرسالة إلى المزكي، والاثنان أحوط. هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، وأما عند محمد فيجب الاثنان، وهذا في تزكية السر، أما في تزكية العلانية، فقد قال الخصاف: يجب الاثنان إجماعا؛ لأنها في معنى الشهادة، حتى لا يصح تزكية العلانية من العبد، ولا بد أن يكون المزكي عدلا، فلا تقبل تزكية الفاسق ومستور الحال. والجواب: إنها ليست في معنى الشهادة؛ ولهذا لا يشترط فيها لفظ الشهادة ومجلس القضاء، واشتراط العدد في الشهادة أمر ثابت بالنص، بخلاف القياس؛ لأن رجحان الصدق في العمل بالعدالة لا بالعدد، كما في رواية حديث النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في حق العلم بالتواتر. وإذا كان كذلك لا معنى لاشتراط العدد في الشهادات، لكنا تركنا ذلك القياس بالنصوص التي فيها بيان العدد، فلا يتعدى اشتراط العدد من الشهادات إلى التزكية. قال في «مجمع الأنهر»: ومحل الاختلاف، فإذا لم يرض الخصم بتزكية الواحد، فإن رضي فجاز إجماعا. انتهى. قوله: وترجمة الشاهد؛ قال في «المصباح المنير»: ترجم كلامه: إذا بينه وأوضحه، وترجم كلام غيره: إذا عبر عنه بلغة غير لغة المتكلم، واسم الفاعل: ترجمان، بفتح التاء وضم الجيم في الفصيح، وقد تضم التاء تبعا للجيم، وقد تفتح الجيم تبعا للتاء، والجمع تراجم. انتهى. قال القهستاني: ترك الإضافة أولى إذ الاثنان أحوط في ترجمة المدعي والمدعى عليه، كما في التمرتاشي وغيره. انتهى.
And the translation of the witness and the sending to the one who validates (al-muzakki), and two is more cautious. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but according to Muhammad, two are obligatory. This pertains to secret validation. As for open validation, al-Khassaf said: Two are obligatory by consensus because it is like testimony, so that validation in open by a slave is not valid. The validator must be just, so the validation of a sinner or one whose status is unknown is not accepted. The answer: It is not like testimony; therefore the wording of testimony and the court session are not required. The requirement of number in testimony is established by text, contrary to analogy because the preponderance of truthfulness in action is by justice, not by number, as in the narration of the Prophet's hadith regarding knowledge through tawatur. If so, there is no meaning to requiring number in testimonies, but we left that analogy due to texts that specify number, so the requirement of number is not extended from testimonies to validation. It is said in Majma' al-Anhur: The point of disagreement is when the opponent does not accept the validation of one; if he accepts, it is permissible by consensus. End. His saying: 'and translation of the witness'; it is said in al-Misbah al-Munir: tarjama is to explain and clarify; tarjama of another's speech is to express it in a different language. The active participle is turjuman, with fat-ha on ta and damm on jim in correct usage; sometimes the ta is made damm following the jim, and the jim is opened following the ta. The plural is tarajim. End. al-Quhustani said: Dropping the idafah is better since two is more cautious in translating the plaintiff and defendant, as in al-Timirtashi and others. End.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage discusses two related matters: the number of witnesses required for tazkiyah (validation of a witness's character) and the translation of a witness's testimony. On the first issue, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute. For secret tazkiyah (where the witness's character is privately verified), Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that one validator is sufficient, while Muhammad al-Shaybani requires two. The basis of Muhammad's view is that tazkiyah is akin to testimony, which requires two witnesses. However, the majority argue that tazkiyah is not testimony, as it does not require the formal wording of testimony or a court session. The requirement of two in testimony is established by explicit texts (nass), and analogy would suggest that one just person is sufficient for credibility, but the texts have set the number for testimony; this requirement should not be extended to tazkiyah by analogy. For open tazkiyah, al-Khassaf reports consensus on requiring two, because open tazkiyah is more like testimony. The text then notes that if the opposing party accepts the validation by one, it is permissible by consensus. The second part discusses translating a witness's statement. The author explains that translation is clarifying or rendering speech into another language. The translator (turjuman) should be careful; it is more cautious to have two translators for the plaintiff and defendant. This is derived from al-Timirtashi and others.

Key Terms
تزكية (tazkiyah): The process of validating the character and reliability of a witness (shahid) by a reputable person (muzakki).
ترجمة (tarjamah): Translation of a witness's testimony from one language to another so that the court may understand it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on translation of witnesses?
A) Translation is not allowed; witnesses must testify in Arabic.
B) One translator is sufficient because it is a form of testimony.
C) Two translators are more cautious, especially for the plaintiff and defendant.
D) Translation must be done by a judge.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, secret tazkiyah can be done with one witness unless the opponent objects, and translation of testimony should ideally involve two translators for caution.

Types of Testimony a Witness May Bear Without Being Requested
Explains self-evident testimony and voice identification rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two categories of testimony a witness may bear and apply the Hanafi rule on hearing from behind a curtain.

Core Idea
A witness may testify to self-evident matters without prior request, but voice identification requires certainty.

In this passage, the author explains that a witness may testify to what he has heard or seen without being formally requested if the matter is self-evident. However, caution is required when identifying a person by voice alone. This lesson clarifies the general rule and its exceptions, including the stricter requirement for women's voices.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two categories of testimony
Identify what is self-evident (e.g., sale, acknowledgment, judge's ruling, visible acts) and what needs a formal request (istishhad).
Self-evident matters are established by themselves; no prior summoning is needed.
2
Apply the rule to audible and visible matters
Understand that both heard events (sale, acknowledgment, judge's ruling) and seen events (usurpation, killing) fall under the first category.
The witness may testify even if not requested.
3
Analyze the exception for hearing from behind a curtain
Recognize the general prohibition due to voice similarity, and the narrow exception when certainty of identity is achieved.
Certainty requires knowledge that only one person is present and no other path.
4
Note the stricter rule for women's voices
For a woman's voice, the witness must see her person; voice alone is insufficient.
This reflects extra caution to avoid error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان أنواع ما يتحمله الشاهد
ولمن سمع بيعا، أو إقرارا، أو حكم قاض، أو رأى غصبا، أو قتلا أن يشهد به، وإن لم يشهد عليه. قوله: ولمن سمع... الخ؛ لما فرغ المصنف من مراتب الشهادة شرع في بيان ما يتحمله الشاهد وهو نوعان: الأول: ما ثبت بنفسه بلا استشهاد. والثاني: ما لا يثبت بنفسه بل يحتاج إلى إشهاده. فشرع في الأول وقال: ولمن... الخ، وحاصله: أن الشاهد يشهد بكل ما سمعه من المسموعات كالبيع والإقرار وحكم الحاكم، وغير ذلك، أو رآه من المبصرات كالغصب والقتل وغير ذلك، وإن لم يشهد على ما ذكر من جانب المدعي؛ لأن كل واحد منها ثابت الحكم بنفسه، فلا حاجة فيه إلى الإشهاد. ولو سمع من وراء الحجاب لا يسعه أن يشهد لاحتمال أن يكون غيره، إذ النغمة تشبه النغمة، إلا إذا كان في الدار رجل وحده، وعلم الشاهد أنه ليس فيه غيره، ثم جلس على المسلك، وليس له مسلك غيره، فسمع إقرار الرجل ولا يراه؛ لأنه يحصل به العلم. وينبغي للقاضي إذا فسر له أن يقبله؛ لأن النغمة تشبه النغمة، وليس من ضرورة جواز الشهادة القبول عند التفسير. وقالوا: إذا سمع صوت امرأة من وراء الحجاب لا يجوز أن يشهد عليها، إلا إذا كان يرى شخصها، ذكره الزيلعي وغيره من الفقهاء الأخيار.
Chapter explaining the types of things a witness bears. Whoever hears a sale, or an acknowledgment, or a judge's ruling, or sees usurpation, or killing, may testify to it, even if he was not requested to witness. His saying: 'Whoever hears... etc.'; when the author finished the ranks of testimony, he began explaining what a witness bears. It is of two types: First: what is established by itself without being summoned. Second: what is not established by itself but needs to be witnessed. He began with the first and said: 'Whoever hears... etc.' Its summary: the witness testifies to everything he heard of audible things like sale, acknowledgment, judge's ruling, etc., or saw of visible things like usurpation, killing, etc., even if he was not requested to witness from the side of the plaintiff, because each of them is established in ruling by itself, so there is no need for being summoned. If he hears from behind a curtain, he may not testify, due to possibility that it could be someone else, since one voice resembles another. Unless the house has only one man, and the witness knows there is no one else, then he sits on the path and there is no other path, so he hears the man's acknowledgment without seeing him, because knowledge is attained. It is appropriate for the judge, if it is explained to him, to accept it, because one voice resembles another, and acceptance upon explanation is not a necessary condition for the permissibility of testimony. They said: If he hears a woman's voice from behind a curtain, he may not testify against her unless he sees her person. Mentioned by al-Zayla'i and other righteous jurists.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage marks a new chapter on the types of testimony a witness may bear. The author divides what a witness bears into two categories: (1) matters that are self-evident and do not require a prior request (istishhad), and (2) matters that require being formally requested to witness. Here he discusses the first category. The ruling is straightforward: whoever hears a transaction like a sale, an acknowledgment of debt, or a judge's verdict, or sees an act of usurpation (ghasb) or a killing, may testify to it even if he was not asked to do so. The reason is that these events have legal effects (ahkam) that exist independently and do not depend on formal witnessing. The text then addresses a practical problem: hearing from behind a curtain or screen. Generally, if one hears a voice from behind a curtain, he cannot testify because voices can be similar, and it might be someone else. However, an exception is made if the house contains only one man, the witness knows that, and he hears the man's acknowledgment while not seeing him—then knowledge is certain, and testimony is permissible. For a woman's voice, the ruling is stricter: the witness must see her person to be certain of identity. The judge is advised to accept testimony when the circumstances are explained, but acceptance is not a condition for the validity of the testimony itself. This passage shows the Hanafi emphasis on certainty (yaqin) and caution (ihtiyat) in testimony.

Key Terms
استشهاد (istishhad): The act of formally requesting someone to witness an event or transaction.
نغمة (naghmah): Voice, tone, or sound, particularly in the context of identifying a speaker.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which situation may a person testify after hearing from behind a curtain?
A) Always, if the voice is clear
B) Never, because of similarity of voices
C) Only if the house has one man, no other path, and the witness knows no one else is present
D) Only if the witness also sees the person

Key Takeaway
A witness may testify to self-evident matters without prior request, but caution is required when relying on voice alone; certainty is essential.

The Hanafi Definition of a Just Witness ('Adl)
Expounds the Hanafi definition of a just witness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a just witness ('adl) and identify the conditions required for 'adala, including the role of muru'ah.

Core Idea
'Adl requires avoiding all major sins and base actions, with good deeds prevailing; minor sins alone do not disqualify.

In Islamic law, witness testimony is a cornerstone of justice, but not every person qualifies. The Hanafi school has a precise definition of 'adala (uprightness) that balances moral integrity with human fallibility. This lesson unpacks that definition from a classical commentary, clarifying what makes a witness acceptable and why occasional minor sins do not break it.

Guided Path
1
Locate the key term
Identify the term being defined: العدل (al-'adl). The passage gives the definition from al-Hidayah and then expands it.
The definition includes both religious (avoiding major sins) and social (avoiding base actions) dimensions.
2
Extract the core conditions
List the conditions: (1) avoidance of all major sins (الكبائر), (2) avoidance of base actions indicating lack of muru'ah, (3) good deeds must outweigh bad deeds.
The commentator adds condition (2) as necessary, citing examples like eating or urinating in public.
3
Understand the logical nuance
Note the logical refinement: persistence on a minor sin is itself a major sin, so avoiding all major sins automatically includes avoiding persistence. Thus the definition is coherent.
This answers a potential confusion in the text.
4
Differentiate from other barriers
The commentator clarifies that an 'adl witness may still have testimony rejected due to external factors (e.g., blindness), but these do not diminish 'adala.
This explains that base actions are deliberate choices, unlike blindness.
5
Apply the definition
Use the definition to evaluate a hypothetical witness: does the person avoid major sins and base actions? Do good deeds predominate? Answer determines 'adala.
Minor sins are overlooked unless persisted upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : وغَلَبَ صوابُهُ : أي حسناته أغلب من سيئاته ، فإن الإلمام بالصغيرة لا يُسقط العدالة.
فقوله : ومن اجتنب الكبائر إلى قولِهِ : وغَلَبَ صوابه تفسير العدل. أقول : ولا بُدَّ فيه من قيد آخر، وهو أن يجتنب الأفعال الخسيسة الدالة على الدناءة : أي عدم المروءة : كالأكل في الطريق، والبول على الطريق.
الكبائر كلّها تستلزم أن لا يبقى كبيرة يرتكب منها ، والإصرار على الصغيرة كبيرة أيضاً، فكيف يتصوَّرُ الاجتناب عن الإصرار على الصغيرة بعد الاجتناب عن الكبائر كلها ؛ لأنَّ الإصرار داخل في مجموعة الكبائر، فالصواب أن يقال : ثم الاجتناب عن الكبائر كلّها لا بد... الخ.
[١] قوله : أي حسناته...الخ ؛ وإلى هذا أشار صاحب الهداية»، وقال: هذا هو الصحيح في حد العدالة المعتبرة، إذ لا بُدَّ له من توقي الكبائر كلّها، وبعد ذلك يعتبر الغالب كما ذكر، فأما الإلمام بمعصية لا تنقدح بها العدالة المشروطة فلا يرد به الشهادة المشروعة ؛ لأنَّ في اعتبار اجتنابه الكلّ سد بابه ، وهو مفتوح ؛ إحياء للحقوق. انتهى. [٢] قوله : أقول : لا بد من...الخ ؛ وأجيب بأنَّ العدل ما ذكر في المتن، وقد لا تقبل شهادة العدل لمانع مثل أن يكون أعمى ، ثم الأكل والبول في الطريق من الموانع أيضاً، فلا يَرِدُ ما ذكره فيه أنَّ مثل البول في الطريق ممنوع الخساسته، والعمى لا اختيار فيه، فلا يقدح العدل، فافهم.
And His statement: 'and his righteousness prevails' means: his good deeds are more dominant than his evil deeds, for committing a minor sin does not nullify uprightness. So his statement: 'and whoever avoids major sins' up to his statement: 'and his righteousness prevails' is the interpretation of uprightness. I say: There must be an additional condition therein, and that is to avoid base actions indicating vileness: i.e., lack of chivalry (muru'ah), like eating on the road and urinating on the road. All major sins entail that no major sin remains that he commits, and persistence on a minor sin is also a major sin, so how can avoidance of persistence on minor sin be imagined after avoidance of all major sins? Because persistence is included within the set of major sins. So the correct thing is to say: Then avoidance of all major sins is necessary... etc.
[1] His statement: 'i.e., his good deeds...' etc.; and the author of al-Hidayah alluded to this, saying: This is the correct position regarding the definition of considered uprightness, for he must avoid all major sins, and after that the dominant [good] is considered as mentioned. As for committing a sin that does not undermine the stipulated uprightness, it does not reject the prescribed testimony; because considering avoidance of all would close its door, while it is open for the revival of rights. End.
[2] His statement: 'I say: There is need for...' etc.; and it is answered that the upright person is what is mentioned in the text, and the testimony of an upright person may not be accepted due to a hindrance such as being blind. Then eating and urinating on the road are also among hindrances, so what he mentioned does not apply—that such as urinating on the road is forbidden due to its vileness, and blindness is not a matter of choice, so it does not impugn uprightness. So understand.
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage defines a just witness ('adl) according to the Hanafi school. The author explains that the condition 'his righteousness prevails' means his good deeds outweigh his bad deeds, and occasional minor sins do not destroy 'adala. The commentator adds a necessary condition: avoiding base actions that indicate lack of muru'ah (chivalry), such as eating or urinating in public. He then addresses a logical issue: if persistence on minor sins is itself a major sin, then how can one avoid persistence on minor sins after avoiding all major sins? The answer is that the condition is to avoid all major sins, and persistence is a major sin, so it is included. He quotes al-Hidayah, which confirms that the correct definition is to avoid all major sins, after which the preponderance of good is considered; occasional minor sins do not prevent testimony. The commentator also addresses an objection: even an 'adl person might not have his testimony accepted due to external barriers like blindness, but base actions like urinating in public are avoidable and thus impugn 'adala, while blindness is involuntary and does not affect 'adala.

Key Terms
العدل (al-'adl): A person whose good deeds outweigh his bad, who avoids all major sins and base actions (lack of muru'ah).
الكبائر (al-kaba'ir): Major sins that a just person must avoid.
المروءة (al-muru'ah): Chivalry or decency; avoiding base actions like eating in public.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, what additional condition does the commentator add to the definition of a just witness?
A) Avoiding all minor sins without exception
B) Avoiding base actions that indicate lack of muru'ah
C) Having knowledge of Islamic law
D) Being male

Key Takeaway
A just witness ('adl) in Hanafi law must avoid all major sins and base actions, with good deeds prevailing; minor sins and occasional lapses do not disqualify.

Testimony of an Uncircumcised Adult (al-Aqlaf)
Explains the ruling on accepting testimony from an uncircumcised adult.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of an uncircumcised adult and explain the distinction between a valid excuse and contempt for religion.

Core Idea
Testimony of an uncircumcised adult is accepted if excused, rejected if contemptuous.

In this passage, we examine a nuanced ruling on witness testimony. The Hanafi school does not consider circumcision a condition for uprightness ('adala), but leaving it without excuse indicates contempt for religion, which disqualifies a witness. The key is the person's intent and respect for religious obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act being ruled
The act is giving testimony by an adult male who is uncircumcised (al-aqlaf). Determine the default ruling from the matn.
The matn states testimony is not accepted.
2
Recognize the rationale
The commentator explains that circumcision is a sunnah in the Hanafi school, so leaving it does not automatically impair 'adala. The issue is whether the omission was due to a valid excuse or contempt for religion.
'Adala is uprightness; contempt invalidates it.
3
Analyze the qualification by Qadi Khan
Qadi Khan adds that if the person left circumcision out of fear for himself (e.g., health risk), the testimony is accepted. If left without excuse, it is rejected.
The excuse must be genuine and reasonable.
4
Incorporate al-Zayla'i's elaboration
Al-Zayla'i specifies that excuses include old age or fear of death. If left without excuse but with contempt, testimony is rejected because the person is no longer upright.
Contempt for religion directly harms 'adala.
5
Apply the ruling to new cases
Now you can distinguish between scenarios: fear of harm vs. disregard. The ruling hinges on intent, not on the act itself.
This principle applies to any religious practice left out of contempt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۳] قوله : والأقلف ; وفي بعض النسخ والأغلف بالغين المعجمة، أي ولا تقبلُ شهادة كبير لا يختن ؛ لأنَّ العدالة لا تختل بتركه الختان ؛ لكونه سنة عندنا، وقيده قاضي خان بأن يتركه لخوف على نفسه، أما إذا تركه بغير عذر لم تقبل.
:
وقال الزيلعي (۳) : هذا إذا تركه لعذر به من كبر أو خوف هلاك، وإن تركه من غير عذر استخفافاً بالدين لا تقبل شهادته ؛ لأنه لم يبق عدلاً مع الاستخفاف بالدين
(۱) «الهداية»(۳): ١٢٤).
(۲) في تبيين الحقائق» (٦ : ٢٢٦).
انتهى.
[3] His statement: 'and the uncircumcised' (al-aqlaf); in some copies it is al-aghlaaf with the dotted ghayn, meaning the testimony of an adult who does not circumcise is not accepted, because uprightness is not impaired by leaving circumcision, as it is a sunnah in our school. Qadi Khan qualified it: if he leaves it out of fear for himself, [it is accepted]; but if he leaves it without excuse, it is not accepted. And al-Zayla'i said: This is if he leaves it due to an excuse such as old age or fear of death; if he leaves it without excuse out of contempt for religion, his testimony is not accepted, because he is no longer upright due to contempt for religion. (al-Hidayah 3:124; Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 6:226). End.
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage addresses the testimony of an uncircumcised adult male (al-aqlaf). The matn states that the testimony of such a person is not accepted. However, the commentator clarifies that this is not because circumcision is a condition for 'adala, as it is a sunnah in the Hanafi school, but rather because leaving circumcision without a valid excuse indicates contempt for religion, which impairs 'adala. Qadi Khan distinguishes between leaving it out of fear (e.g., for health reasons) and leaving it without excuse. Al-Zayla'i adds that if a person has an excuse like old age or fear of death, his testimony is accepted; but if done out of contempt, it is rejected. Thus, the ruling hinges on the presence or absence of respect for religious practices.

Key Terms
الأقلف (al-aqlaf): An uncircumcised adult male; testimony is accepted only with a valid excuse.
الاستخفاف بالدين (al-istikhlaf bi al-din): Contempt for religion, which invalidates 'adala and thus testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Accepted only if he left circumcision out of a valid fear
D) Accepted only if he is a minor

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts testimony from an uncircumcised adult only if he has a valid excuse; otherwise, it is rejected due to contempt for religion.

Testimony of a Eunuch, Illegitimate Child, and Workers; Timing of Circumcision
Rulings on testimony of eunuch, illegitimate child, workers, and timing of circumcision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the testimony of a eunuch, an illegitimate child, and workers, and explain the Hanafi response to the Maliki objection regarding the testimony of an illegitimate child.

Core Idea
A witness's reliability is based on his own 'adala, not on his physical condition or birth.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah presents the Hanafi positions on who may testify, addressing three categories often questioned: eunuchs, children born of adultery, and manual workers. It also discusses the timing of circumcision. The key legal thread is that 'adala (moral uprightness) is the sole criterion, with the eunuch's condition and the child's birth being irrelevant to their capacity to testify truthfully. The text specifically rebuts the Maliki objection that an illegitimate child cannot testify in adultery cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three witness categories and their ruling
Read the matn: 'And the eunuch, and the offspring of adultery, and the workers.' The commentator states that their testimony is accepted. Note the key condition: they must be 'adl (upright).
The ruling is general: testimony is accepted unless there is a specific disqualification.
2
Analyze the dalil for the eunuch
The commentator explains that the eunuch is like an amputee—his castration was unjust, and this does not impair his trustworthiness. The proof is from Umar's acceptance of Alqama al-Khasii's testimony, narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba.
This is a qawl al-sahabi (companion's practice), which is a valid proof in Hanafi usul.
3
Examine the dispute over the illegitimate child
The Maliki school argues that a child of adultery loves that others be like him, so his testimony in adultery cases is rejected. The Hanafi response is that if the child is 'adl, he does not desire sin for others; the parents' sin does not affect his character.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (valid scholarly difference) based on a presumption versus individual character.
4
Understand the ruling on workers
The commentary clarifies that 'workers' here includes those who work for the sultan in collecting legitimate dues or those who work with their hands. Work itself is not sinful unless it involves injustice. The hadith praising earning by hand is cited to refute those who reject their testimony.
Some earlier scholars rejected worker testimony; the Hanafi school affirms it based on an-Nabi's ﷺ statement.
5
Note the timing of circumcision
Imam Abu Hanifa did not fix a specific time due to lack of explicit text. Later Hanafis determined the earliest time as 7 years and latest as 12. It is a sunnah for men and an honor for women.
The timing is based on the practice of the Companions, as reported by al-Halwani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخصي، وولد الزنا، والعُمَّال) ، وعند مالك (۱) ه لا تقبل شهادة ولدِ الزنا على الزنا ؛ لأنَّهُ يُحِبُّ أن يكون غيرُه كنفسه. وأما العمال : فإن نفس العمل ليس بفسق " إلا إذا كانوا أعواناً على الظلم وعن ابن عباس : لا تقبل شهادته وهو محمول على ما إذا تركه استخفافاً بالسنة. انتهى.
ولم يقدر له الإمام وقتاً معلوماً بعدم ورود النص به، وقدره المتأخرون ، واختلفوا في ذلك المختار إنَّ أوَّل وقته سبع ، وآخره اثنتا عشر. كذا في «الخلاصة»، وهو سنة للرجال، مكرمةٌ للنساء، إذ جماعُ المختونة ألذ ، قال الحلواني : كانت النساء يختتنَّ في زمن أصحاب رسول الله له كذا في «المنح »
[۱] قوله : والخصي ...الخ ؛ أي تقبل شهادتهم، أما الخصي ؛ فلانه قطع منه عضو ظلماً من غير وجه شرعي ، فصار كالأقطع ، وقد ثبت أنّ عمر ه قبل شهادة علقمة الخصي على ابن مظعون (۳)، رواه ابن أبي شيبة . وأمّا ولد الزنا ؛ فلأنَّ فسق الأبوين لا يوجب قدحاً في عدالة الولد، ككفرهما، والإطلاق يشمل ما إذا شهد بالزنا أو بغيره، والمراد بالعمال عمال السلطان، وهم الذين يعينون السلطان في أخذ الحقوقِ الواجبة كالخراج وزكاة السوائم ونحوهما. وقيل : الذين يعملون بأيديهم ويؤاجرون أنفسهم ؛ لأنَّ من الناس من قال : لا تقبل شهادة هؤلاء، وإنما ذكر العمّال ردّاً على هذا القائل ؛ لأنّ كسبهم أطيب، كما ورد في الحديث، وفضل الناس عند الله من يأكل من كسب يده فلا يوجب حرجاً.
[٢]قوله : لأنه...الخ ؛ قلنا : الكلام في قبول شهادة ولد الزنا إذا كان عدلاً، والعدل لا يستحب أن يكون غيره مثله.
[٣]قوله : ليس بفسق ؛ فإنّ أجلاء الصحابة كانوا عمالاً ؛ لأنَّ العمل عبادة، وله أجر.
(And the eunuch, and the offspring of adultery, and the workers) – and according to Malik, the testimony of an illegitimate child is not accepted in cases of adultery, because he loves that others be like him. As for workers, the work itself is not sin unless they are helpers in injustice. And from Ibn Abbas: his testimony is not accepted, and it is interpreted as when he leaves it out of contempt for the Sunnah. End.
And the Imam (Abu Hanifa) did not fix a specific time [for circumcision] due to lack of explicit text, but later scholars fixed it, and they differed; the chosen view is that its earliest time is seven years and its latest is twelve. Thus in al-Khulasa, and it is a sunnah for men, an honor for women, for intercourse with a circumcised woman is more pleasurable. Al-Halwani said: Women used to be circumcised in the time of the Companions of the Messenger of Allah, as in al-Minh.
[1] His statement: 'and the eunuch...' etc. – meaning their testimony is accepted. As for the eunuch, because a limb was cut from him unjustly without a legal basis, so he becomes like an amputee, and it is established that Umar accepted the testimony of Alqama al-Khasii against Ibn Maz'un, narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba. As for the illegitimate child, because the sin of the parents does not necessitate a defect in the child's uprightness, just as their disbelief [does not], and the absolute includes when he testifies about adultery or other. And by workers are meant the workers of the sultan, those who assist the sultan in taking obligatory rights like land tax and zakah on grazing animals and the like. It is also said: those who work with their hands and hire themselves out, because some people said that their testimony is not accepted, and the mention of workers is a refutation of that speaker, because their earning is purer, as reported in the hadith, and the most virtuous among people in the sight of Allah is he who eats from the earning of his hand, so it does not cause harm.
[2] His statement: 'because...' etc.; we say: the discussion is about accepting the testimony of the illegitimate child if he is upright, and the upright person does not love that others be like him.
[3] His statement: 'it is not sin' – for the noble Companions were workers, because work is worship and has reward.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage covers three categories of witnesses and the timing of circumcision. The matn states that testimony of a eunuch, an illegitimate child, and workers is accepted. The commentator first addresses the eunuch: testicles were removed unjustly, so he is like a person with a severed limb; proof comes from Umar accepting Alqama's testimony. For the illegitimate child, the parents' sin does not affect the child's 'adala. The commentator refutes Maliki view that a child of adultery cannot testify in adultery cases because he would want others like him; the Hanafi response is that an upright person does not desire sin for others. For workers, work is not sinful unless they help in injustice; the hadith praising earning by hand is cited. The text also includes a ruling on the timing of circumcision: Imam Abu Hanifa did not set a time, but later scholars placed it between seven and twelve years; it is a sunnah for men and honorable for women.

Key Terms
الخصي (al-khasii): A eunuch; testimony is accepted because he is like an amputee.
ولد الزنا (walad al-zina): Child born of adultery; testimony accepted if just, regardless of parents' sin.
العمال (al-ummal): Workers, especially those assisting the sultan or working with their hands; testimony accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school accept the testimony of a eunuch?
A) Because castration is not a sin
B) Because a eunuch is more trustworthy
C) Because he is like an amputee, and Umar accepted such testimony
D) Because eunuchs cannot have evil intentions

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a witness's physical condition, birth, or occupation does not affect the admissibility of their testimony; what matters is their personal uprightness (ʿadāla).

Rejection of Testimony for Relatives and Enemy Testimony
States the prohibition of testifying for one's ascendants, descendants, and spouse, and discusses enemy testimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the rejection of testimony for ascendants, descendants, and spouse, and explain the ikhtilaf regarding enemy testimony, including the evidence and the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Testimony for one’s ascendants, descendants, and spouse is not accepted due to potential bias; the enemy’s testimony is rejected according to later Hanafi scholarship.

This passage from al-Wiqayah explains which testimony is accepted and which is rejected. The core rule is that testimony for close relatives is not accepted because of the strong connection of benefits, similar to the prohibition of giving them zakat. The commentary also addresses the controversial issue of enemy testimony, where there are two narrations within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited relationships
List the relatives for whom testimony is rejected: ascendants (parents, grandparents), descendants (children, grandchildren), and spouse.
The matn uses 'aṣl', 'far'', 'zawj', and 'urs'.
2
Understand the evidence
The primary proof is a hadith narrated by al-Khassaf that explicitly lists these categories. Additionally, the analogy with zakat (which cannot be given to these relatives) supports the reasoning that benefits are interconnected, making testimony akin to testifying for oneself.
The dalil is ahad but strong in meaning (dalala qati).
3
Note the scope: for vs. against
The rejection is only when testifying 'for' the relative. Testimony 'against' them is generally accepted, except when a special conflict arises, such as a grandfather testifying against his son for the benefit of a grandson.
The text specifically says 'testimony for them' (lahum).
4
Analyze the enemy testimony dispute
There are two narrations within the Hanafi school: a mansusa narration accepts the enemy's testimony absolutely if the person is just, while later scholars reject it due to the risk of bias. The mufta bihi is rejection.
This is also a point of ikhtilaf with Imam al-Shafi'i, who rejects enemy testimony absolutely.

The book of testimony and retraction from it, chapter of acceptance and non-acceptance. And not for his ascendant, descendant, spouse, and wife. Regarding the enemy: his testimony is not accepted against the one he is hostile to, but it is accepted for him. And in the original [matn] up to the end, it is the opposite. His saying: and not for his ascendant and descendant, meaning testimony is not accepted for them, because of his saying: 'The testimony of a child for his parent, nor the parent for his child, nor the woman for her husband, nor the husband for his wife, nor the slave for his master, nor the master for his slave, nor the hired worker for his employer' – narrated by al-Khassaf with his chain to the Prophet, and it is in the Musannaf of Ibn Abi Shayba and Abd al-Razzaq from the saying of Shurayh, mentioned by al-Zayla'i in Nasb al-Raya. And because the benefits between children and parents are connected; for this reason it is not permissible to pay zakat to them. Thus it is testimony for oneself in a way. The term 'descendant' is general, so it includes the descendant from one side. Therefore the testimony of the child of li'an (the imprecated child) is not accepted for his ascendants, nor for him by them, nor for his descendants; because his lineage is established from one side by the proof of the validity of his claim from him and its invalidity from others, and marriage to him is prohibited, and zakat is placed on him. So the rulings of sonship are established for him except inheritance and maintenance from both sides. This is explicitly stated in 'al-Manh' and others. And it is restricted to testimony for them; because testimony against his ascendant and descendant is accepted, except when the grandfather testifies against his son for his son's son against his father, then it is not accepted due to the existence of the impediment of the beneficiary. Similarly in 'al-Bahr'. His saying: not accepted... etc. al-Yarjandi said in Sharh al-Nuqaya: What is mentioned that the worldly enemy's testimony is not accepted is mentioned in al-Muhit and others, and it is the choice of the later scholars. The narrated transmission (al-riwaya al-mansusa) is what is mentioned in Khizanat al-Fiqh: that the enemy's testimony is accepted absolutely, because if enmity were to drop testimony, it would have to be so in all cases like fisq. And it is mentioned in Sharh al-Sunna: that the enemy's testimony is not accepted according to al-Shafi'i, and it is accepted according to Abu Hanifa if he is just.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding the acceptance of testimony. The matn states that testimony is not accepted for one's ascendant (parent, grandparent), descendant (child, grandchild), and spouse. The commentary provides the evidence: a hadith narrated by al-Khassaf from the Prophet (peace be upon him) listing these relatives, though some versions attribute it to Shurayh as a saying of his. The reasoning is that benefits are closely connected among these relatives, similar to the prohibition of giving them zakat, so testifying for them is like testifying for oneself indirectly. The term 'descendant' is general and includes a child born from li'an (imprecation), who has lineage only from the mother's side, so the rulings of sonship apply except inheritance and maintenance. The restriction is 'for them' – testimony against them is accepted, unless there is a special conflict like a grandfather testifying against his son for the benefit of his grandson, which is not accepted because the beneficiary is the grandson (descendant). The commentary then discusses the enemy testimony: there are two narrations. According to the later scholars (mutakhkhirin) and the choice in al-Muhit, the worldly enemy's testimony is not accepted. But the 'riwaya mansusa' (textually transmitted narration) in Khizanat al-Fiqh is that it is accepted absolutely, reasoning that just as fisq drops testimony universally, if enmity dropped it, it would be universal; but since it is not universal, enmity does not drop testimony. The passage also notes that Imam al-Shafi'i does not accept the enemy's testimony, while Abu Hanifa accepts it if the person is just ('adil).

Key Terms
أصل (aṣl): Ascendant lineage: parents, grandparents, and upwards.
فرع (far'): Descendant lineage: children, grandchildren, and downwards.
زوج / عرس (zawj / 'urs): Spouse: husband (zawj) and wife ('urs).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is accepted because he is a descendant.
B) It is not accepted because the child is related only through the mother for some rulings.
C) It is accepted only if he is just.
D) It is not accepted because the father is an enemy.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, testimony for close relatives is rejected due to potential bias, supported by hadith and analogy; the enemy's testimony is rejected according to the later preferred position, though a valid narration accepts it.

Ruling on Singing and Its Effect on Witness Testimony
States the prohibition of singing and its effect on witness adala with exception for oneself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on singing and its conditions, and apply the exception for private singing to assess its effect on witness adala.

Core Idea
Singing is generally prohibited in Hanafi fiqh, but private singing for oneself does not affect uprightness.

This passage presents the Hanafi position on singing and its impact on the legal uprightness (‘adala) required for witness testimony. It synthesizes multiple authoritative sources to establish that singing is a major sin, but carves out a narrow exception: if a person sings privately to dispel loneliness, his ‘adala remains intact. We will walk through the prohibition, the conditions under which some scholars permitted it, and the evidence for the exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general Hanafi ruling on singing
Look at the opening of the passage: it says singing is haram, agreed upon by scholars, and a major sin. Note the sources cited (Nisab al-Ihtisab, al-Dhakhira, etc.).
The default ruling is prohibition.
2
Examine the conditions for permissibility
Some mashayikh permitted singing under six strict conditions. List them: no beardless youth, only pious males, sincere intention, no food/gathering for food, not standing except overwhelmed, genuine ecstasy.
These conditions show that any permission is extremely limited.
3
Analyze the effect on witness adala
The passage distinguishes between public singing (gathering people for sin) and private singing for oneself. The exception: 'if he sings for himself to dispel loneliness, it does not invalidate uprightness.'
This is the key practical ruling.
4
Evaluate the evidence for the exception
The evidence is the hadith of Bara' ibn Malik, a pious Companion, who sang in the presence of his brother Anas. The Prophet did not forbid singing itself, only warned about attracting women.
The hadith shows private singing is not prohibited.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a person who sings religious nasheeds alone in his car, decide whether his testimony would be accepted. Use the principle that private singing does not affect adala, but public singing might.
Consider whether the singing is truly private or could be considered public.

و ثانياً : إنَّه حرام كما صرح به الفقهاء العظام، قال في «نصاب الاحتساب»: إنَّ التغني والسماع [و] الغناء حرام، اجتمع عليه العلماء وبالغـوا فـيـه، ومن أباحـه مـن المشايخ ممن تخلى عن الهوى وتحلى بالتقوى واحتاج إلى ذلك احتياج المريض إلى الدواء، وعلامته أنه منسل عن الشهوات، مستهو بذكر الله له في الخلوات ، مفرغ يديه عن الأخذ والإعطاء مجرَّد عن الذم ،والثناء مختلف بالواردات، يريد أن يتنفس بتنفس الصعداء، ويعالج ما غلب عليه تشوقه إلى مولاه من الداء، ثمّ إنَّه رخصه، وله شرائط أخذها : الأولى: أنه لا يكون فيهم أمرد. والثانية : أن لا يكون جمعُهم إلا من جنسهم ليس فيهم فاسق ، ولا أهل الدنيا ولا امرأة. والثالثة : أن تكون نية القوال: الإخلاص لا أخذ الأجرة والإنعام. والرابعة : أن لا يجتمعوا لأجل الطعام، أو نظر إلى فتوح. والخامسة : لا يقومون إلا مغلوبين. والسادسة : لا يظهرون وجداً إلا صادقين. انتهى. وذكر في «الذخيرة»: إنّه كبيرة، ومن أباحه من المشايخ، فذلك الذي حركاته حركات المرتعش. انتهى. وذكر في «العوارف»: إنّه لا يليق بمنصب المشايخ والذين يقتدى بهم ؛ لأنه شأنه اللهو، وأنه يباين حال التمكن. انتهى. (۲) وقال القُهستاني في «شرح النُّقاية ) : فهو من أنواع اللعب، وكبيرة في جميع الأديان ، حتى يمنع المشركون من ذلك. كما في «الاختيار، وغيره، وفي «المضمرات»: من أباح الغناء يكون فاسقاً. وفي «شرح السير الكبير للإمام السَّرَخْسِي له : إنه كان يكره رفع الصوت ند قراءة القرآن والوعظ ، فما يفعله الذين يدعون الوجد والمحبة مكروه لا أصل له في الدين، وفي «الجواهرات»: السماعُ والرقص الذي يفعله المتصوفة في زماننا حرام، لا يجوز القصد والجلوس إليه، وهو والغناء والمزامير سواء، ومشايخ قبلهم فعلوا غير ما فعلوا هؤلاء. وفي «العوارف) : سماع الغناء من الذنوب وما أباحه إلا نفر قليل من الفقهاء ، ومن أباحه لم يرَ إعلانه في المساجد والبقاع الشريفة ، وقال : «كان إبليس أول من تغنّى (٢)، وما نقل عنه : إنه سمع الشعر لا يدل على إباحة الغناء. انتهى مختصراً وملخصاً. وإن شئت زيادة التفصيل في هذا المقام ، فارجع إلى «إحياء العلوم للغزالي و«العوارف» (4) ، و«الفتاوى الحمادية من مبسوطات الصوفية الصافية والفقهاء الأعلام، واقتصرنا على ذلك خشية عن طوالة الكلام. وثالثاً : إن قوله : للناس، إشارة إلى أنه لو تغنّى لنفسه لدفع الوحشة لا يسقط العدالة، لما روي أنَّ البراء بن مالك لله دخل عليه أخوه أنس بن مالك ، وهو يُغَنِّي(ه) ، والبراء بن مالك كان من زهاد الصحابة له ، وهذا عند عامة المشايخ. واختاره شمس الأئمة السرخسي ، واختاره المصنف له أيضاً لكن تعليل صاحب «الهداية» بقوله : لأنه يجمع الناس على ارتكاب كبيرة. انتهى.
And secondly: Indeed it is forbidden as the great jurists have stated. He said in 'Nisab al-Ihtisab': Singing and listening [to] singing are forbidden, the scholars have unanimously agreed upon it and were emphatic about it. And whoever among the mashayikh permitted it, [it is] for one who has abandoned desires, adorned himself with piety, and needs it like the patient needs medicine. Its sign is that he is free from lusts, absorbed in the remembrance of Allah in seclusion, his hands empty from taking and giving, free from blame and praise, varying according to spiritual states, wanting to take a deep breath and treat the disease of yearning for his Lord that overcomes him. Then he permitted it, and it has conditions: First: That there be no beardless youth among them. Second: That their gathering be only of their own kind, no corrupt person, no worldly people, and no woman. Third: That the intention of the singer be sincerity, not taking wages and favors. Fourth: That they do not gather for food or looking at openings. Fifth: That they do not stand up except overwhelmed. Sixth: That they do not show ecstasy except truthfully. End. And it is mentioned in 'al-Dhakhira': That it is a major sin, and whoever among the mashayikh permitted it, his movements are like the movements of a trembling one. End. And it is mentioned in 'al-Awarif': That it is not befitting the status of the mashayikh and those who are followed, because it is a matter of frivolity and it contradicts the state of firmness. End. And al-Quhustani said in 'Sharh al-Nuqqaya': It is a type of play and a major sin in all religions, even the polytheists forbid it, as in 'al-Ikhtiyar' and others. And in 'al-Mudmarat': Whoever permits singing becomes a sinner. And in 'Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir' by Imam al-Sarakhsi: He used to dislike raising the voice when reciting the Quran and giving sermons, so what those who claim ecstasy and love do is disliked, having no basis in the religion. And in 'al-Jawahirat': Listening and dancing done by the Sufis in our time are forbidden; it is not permissible to intend or sit for it, and it is the same as singing and flutes. And mashayikh before them did other than what these do. And in 'al-Awarif': Listening to singing is among sins, and none permitted it except a few jurists, and those who permitted it did not allow its public announcement in mosques and noble places. He said: 'Iblis was the first to sing,' and what is narrated about him that he heard poetry does not indicate permissibility of singing. End. Abridged and summarized. And if you wish more detail on this topic, refer to 'Ihya al-Ulum' by al-Ghazali and 'al-Awarif' and 'al-Fatawa al-Hamadiyya' from the works of the pure Sufis and eminent jurists. We have limited ourselves to that for fear of prolonging the discourse. And thirdly: His saying 'for people' indicates that if he sings for himself to dispel loneliness, it does not invalidate uprightness, based on what is narrated that Bara' ibn Malik — his brother Anas ibn Malik entered upon him and he was singing, and Bara' ibn Malik was among the ascetic Companions. This is according to the generality of the mashayikh. It was chosen by Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi, and also chosen by the author, but the explanation of the author of 'al-Hidaya' is because he gathers people to commit a major sin. End.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the prohibition of singing (ghina) and its effect on the legal uprightness ('adala) required for witness testimony. The author presents the Hanafi position that singing is haram (forbidden) by citing multiple authoritative sources: Nisab al-Ihtisab, al-Dhakhira, al-Awarif, Sharh al-Nuqqaya, al-Ikhtiyar, al-Mudmarat, Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir, and al-Jawahirat. These sources collectively affirm that singing is a major sin (kabira) and that any permission granted by some shaykhs is strictly conditional: the singer must be a pious person free from lusts, the gathering must be exclusively male, no beardless youths or women present, intention must be sincere and not for payment, no eating or looking at openings, and the ecstasy expressed must be genuine. The passage then addresses a specific exception: if a person sings to himself privately to dispel loneliness (li daf' al-wahsha), his 'adala is not affected. This is supported by the example of Bara' ibn Malik, a Companion known for his asceticism, who sang in the presence of his brother Anas. The author notes that this exception is accepted by most scholars, including Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi and the author of al-Hidaya, although the author of al-Hidaya cautions that public singing gathering people to commit a major sin remains prohibited. Thus, the ruling distinguishes between private self-entertainment and public performance. The commentary follows the author's order: first the general prohibition, then the conditions for permissibility in exceptional cases, then the specific exception for one's own singing, and finally the effect on 'adala.

Key Terms
الغناء (al-ghina): Singing with musical elements that arouse passions and distract from remembrance of Allah; the text treats it as generally prohibited.
العدالة (al-adala): Legal uprightness required for a witness to be accepted; it is compromised by committing major sins like singing publicly without excuse.
السماع (al-sama'): Listening to music or singing; the text treats it as forbidden like the act of singing itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When it is done in a gathering with beardless youths and women.
B) When it is done for oneself to dispel loneliness.
C) When it is done for a paid performance.
D) When it is done in a mosque.

Key Takeaway
Singing is generally prohibited in Hanafi fiqh, but private singing for oneself does not affect a person's legal uprightness (adala) as a witness.

Ruling on Singing and Its Effect on Testimony
Intra-Hanafi dispute on singing and testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between public and private singing in Hanafi law and state the effect on the legal integrity ('adalah) of a witness.

Core Idea
Singing that gathers people to sin is a major sin and disqualifies testimony; private singing does not.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on singing (ghina) and its impact on a person's legal integrity for testimony. The passage distinguishes between public singing that attracts crowds to sin and private singing for personal comfort, citing the example of the Companion al-Bara ibn Malik. It also presents the intra-Hanafi debate and concludes with the relied-upon view of absolute prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its categories
The passage discusses singing (ghina) and distinguishes two categories: public singing that gathers people to sin, and private singing for oneself to dispel loneliness.
Key condition: the phrase 'li al-nas' (for the people) indicates the public nature.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof cited is the practice of al-Bara ibn Malik, who sang privately in his brother Anas's presence, and he was an ascetic Companion. This shows that private singing does not harm 'adalah.
The Hanafi response to opponents who cite poetry listening is that it is not equivalent to singing.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars permit singing at weddings or for poetic benefit. However, the majority and later authorities (like al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) hold absolute prohibition.
The tarjih authority is al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, which prefers absolute prohibition.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative position for fatwa is that singing is absolutely prohibited, regardless of context. This is based on the reasoning that it leads to sin and resembles the actions of Iblis.
The passage references al-Bahr al-Ra'iq as the authoritative source for this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ثم يردف بما في صفحة 574) ومنهم من جوزه للنَّاس في عرس أو وليمة. ومنهم من جوزه ليستفيد به نظم القوافي وفصاحة اللسان. كذا في «المنح» وذكر في «البحر» : إن المذهب حرمة الغناء مطلقاً، وإن شئت التوضيح فارجع إليه.
And in 'Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir' of Imam al-Sarakhsi: He disliked raising the voice when reciting Quran and preaching. So what those who claim ecstasy and love do is disliked, having no basis in the religion. And in 'al-Jawahirat': Listening and dancing done by the Sufis in our time is forbidden; it is not permissible to intend it or sit for it, and it is the same as singing and flutes. And scholars before them did other than what these do. And in 'al-Awarif': Listening to singing is among sins, and none permitted it except a few jurists. And he who permitted it did not see its announcement in mosques and noble places. And he said: 'Iblis was the first to sing' and what is transmitted about him that he heard poetry does not indicate permission of singing. End, summarized and condensed. And if you wish greater detail on this subject, refer to 'Ihya al-Ulum' by al-Ghazali, 'al-Awarif', and 'al-Fatawa al-Hamadiyya' from the books of the pure Sufis and eminent jurists. And we sufficed with that for fear of prolongation of speech. And thirdly: His saying 'for the people' indicates that if he sings for himself to dispel loneliness, his integrity is not lost, because it is narrated that al-Bara ibn Malik entered upon him (his brother Anas ibn Malik) and he was singing, and al-Bara ibn Malik was among the ascetic Companions. And this is according to the general mashayikh. And Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi chose it, and the author also chose it, but the author of 'al-Hidaya' reasoned by saying: because it gathers people upon committing a major sin. End. And among them are those who permitted it for people at a wedding or feast. And among them are those who permitted it to benefit thereby in arranging rhymes and eloquence of tongue. Thus in 'al-Manah'. And it is mentioned in 'al-Bahr': The madhhab is absolute prohibition of singing, and if you wish clarification, refer to it.
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on singing (ghina) and its effect on the acceptance of testimony. The passage begins by citing al-Sarakhsi's dislike of raising the voice in Quran recitation and preaching, then extends this to singing and dancing claimed by some Sufis, which he labels as disliked or forbidden. The discussion then turns to the condition 'for the people' (li al-nas) in the context of rejecting testimony: if one sings to gather people, it is a major sin; but if one sings privately for oneself to dispel loneliness, it does not disqualify testimony, based on the practice of al-Bara ibn Malik. The passage notes different opinions among scholars: some permit singing at weddings, some permit it to improve poetic skill, but the relied-upon Hanafi position according to 'al-Bahr' is absolute prohibition. The commentary clarifies that the Hanafi school generally considers public singing a major sin that invalidates testimony, while private recitation may be excused. The passage also references authorities like al-Ghazali and al-Awarif for further detail.

Key Terms
الغناء (al-ghina): Singing, specifically vocal music with musical elements that may lead to sinful behavior.
العدالة (al-'adalah): Legal integrity required for testimony; a witness must be of upright character and not commit major sins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, which type of singing does NOT disqualify a person from giving testimony?
A) Singing publicly at a wedding to entertain guests.
B) Singing privately for oneself to dispel loneliness.
C) Singing in a gathering that includes musical instruments.
D) Singing in a mosque to bring people together.

Key Takeaway
Public singing that gathers people to sin is a major sin disqualifying testimony; private singing for oneself does not disqualify.

Testimony with Differing Amounts: Agreement vs Contradiction
Parsing the distinction between agreement and contradiction in testimony amounts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between agreement and contradiction in testimonies where the amounts differ, applying the Hanafi rule that minor additions (e.g., 1000 vs 1100) are acceptable but fundamental numerical contradictions (e.g., 1000 vs 2000) invalidate the testimony.

Core Idea
Two testimonies are agreed if the core amount matches; a difference in number category (singular vs dual) indicates contradiction.

In Hanafi law, when two witnesses give differing amounts in their testimony, the key is whether the difference represents a mere addition or a fundamental contradiction. A difference of 100 dirhams (1000 vs 1100) is considered agreement on the base amount, so the testimony is accepted for 1000. But a difference of 1000 dirhams (1000 vs 2000) changes the singular to dual, indicating a distinct subject, making the testimonies mutually exclusive and both rejected. This parsing exercise sharpens your ability to identify when a difference is reconcilable and when it is fatal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two amounts
Read the testimony of each witness and note the numbers they claim. In the first case, one says 1000 and the other 1100; in the second, one says 1000 and the other 2000.
2
Determine if they share a core amount
Check if the smaller amount is contained within the larger. 1000 is the core of 1100 (the extra 100 is an addition). But 1000 is not the core of 2000 because two thousand is a different numerical category (dual vs singular).
The key is the linguistic category: singular (1000) and dual (2000) are fundamentally different.
3
Classify as muttafiqan (agreed) or muba’inan (contradictory)
If the core matches, the testimonies are muttafiqan on that core, and the addition is disregarded. If the core does not match (due to a change in number type), they are muba’inan — contradictory.
Muba’inan means the speech of each witness is mutually exclusive.
4
State the ruling
For muttafiqan (1000 vs 1100): testimony is accepted for the agreed core amount (1000). For muba’inan (1000 vs 2000): both testimonies are rejected unless one is preferred by other evidence.
The ruling is based on the principle that testimony must be consistent on the essential subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو أنَّهما متَّفقان على الألف في شهادةِ أحدهما بألف والآخر بألف ومئة ، غيرُ متفقين في شهادة أحدهما بألفر والآخر بألفين. [١]قوله : غير متفقين ؛ لأنَّ الألف ،مفرد، والألفين تثنية، واختلاف الألفاظ تثنية وإفراداً يدل على اختلافِ المعاني بالضرورة، فإنَّ الألف لا يعبر به عن الألفين، لا حقيقة ولا مجازاً وبالعكس ، وكان كلام كلِّ واحدٍ من الشاهدين مبائناً لكلام الآخر.
And that is: they are agreed upon the thousand in the testimony of one with a thousand and the other with a thousand and one hundred, and they are not agreed upon in the testimony of one with a thousand and the other with two thousand. [1] His statement: 'they are not agreed' because a thousand is singular, and two thousand is dual, and the difference of wording between dual and singular necessarily indicates difference in meanings, for a thousand is not expressed by two thousand, neither literally nor figuratively, and vice versa, and the speech of each of the two witnesses is contradictory to the speech of the other.
Source pages p. 591

Commentary
This is a parsing exercise (parsing_exercise) that clarifies the ruling on testimony where the amounts differ. The text explains a technical distinction: when one witness says 1000 and the other says 1100, they are considered in agreement on the base amount of 1000. The extra 100 is seen as an addition that does not contradict the core. Therefore, the testimony is accepted for 1000. However, when one says 1000 and the other says 2000, they are not in agreement because the difference is not merely an addition but a contradiction in the principal amount. The commentary emphasizes that the word 'alf' (thousand) is singular, while 'alfayn' (two thousand) is dual; the change in number indicates a difference in the object of testimony. Thus, their testimonies are mutually exclusive, and both are rejected unless one is preferred.

Key Terms
متفقان (muttafiqān): In agreement; here it means the two witnesses are considered in agreement on the base amount despite a slight numerical difference.
مبائناً (mubā’inan): Contradictory; the statements are mutually exclusive because the amounts differ in kind (singular vs dual).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling when one witness testifies that the amount is 1000 dirhams and the other testifies that it is 1100 dirhams?
A) Both testimonies are rejected due to contradiction.
B) They are considered agreed on 1000 and testimony is accepted for 1000.
C) The testimony is accepted for 1100 because that is the higher amount.
D) The judge must average the amounts to 1050.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, testimony with differing amounts is accepted for the agreed core if the difference is a minor addition (e.g., 1000 vs 1100), but rejected if the difference changes the numerical category (e.g., 1000 vs 2000) due to linguistic contradiction.

Testimony with One Witness Adding Partial Payment
Ruling on accepting testimony when one witness adds that part was paid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting testimony when one witness adds that part of the debt was paid, and distinguish the intra-Hanafi positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.

Core Idea
When one witness adds a payment, the main testimony is accepted but the payment claim is rejected as solitary testimony.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in testimony law: two witnesses testify for a debt, but one adds that the debtor paid part of it. How does the court treat this additional statement? The answer hinges on the principle that solitary testimony (shahādat al-fard) is not accepted in financial matters. We'll explore the ruling, the reasoning, and the different views within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Picture two witnesses testifying that the defendant owes a thousand dirhams. Both agree on the principal. However, one witness adds: 'The defendant paid five hundred of it.'
The core element is that both witnesses agree on the obligation of the thousand.
2
Separate the testimonies
The testimony consists of two parts: (A) the agreement on the debt of a thousand, and (B) the individual's statement about payment. Part A is supported by two witnesses; part B is supported by only one.
Hanafi law requires two witnesses for financial claims; one witness is insufficient.
3
Apply the rule of shahādat al-fard
Since part B is the testimony of a single individual, it is rejected unless another witness corroborates. Therefore, the payment claim is not accepted.
The principle: shahādat al-fard ghayr maqbūla (testimony of an individual is not accepted).
4
Address the objection
One might argue: the claimant denies the payment, so the witness is contradicting him. Shouldn't the entire testimony be rejected? The answer: denial of a separate fact does not harm the main testimony, as shown by the analogy where witnesses testify for and then against the same person in different cases.
The denial is about what the witness testified against the claimant (payment), not about what he testified for him (debt).
5
Compare intra-Hanafi views
Abu Yusuf held that the witness's implication that only five hundred remains should be considered, leading to a judgment for five hundred. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rejected this, sticking to the literal agreement on the thousand.
The majority view (Imāmayn) is the muftā bihī: payment statement ignored.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو شهدا بألف ، أو بقرض ألف وزادَ أحدهما قَضَى كذا، قُبِلَتْ بألف ، وبقرض ألف، ورُدَّ قوله قضى كذا ؛ لأنَّ شهادة الفردِ غير مقبولة، إلا إذا شَهِدَ معه آخر، ولا يَشْهَدُ مَن علمهُ حتَّى يُقِرَّ المدعى عند الناس بما قَبَض) : أي يَحِبُّ قالت الثلاثة، وفرقُ أبي حنيفة له أدق وأقوى دراية ، كما لا يخفى على من نظره دقيق. [٢] قوله : ولـو شـهـدا...الخ ؛ يعني لو شهدا بألف ، أو شهدا بقرض ألف، وقال أحدهما : قضى من الألف بخمسمئة ،مثلاً، قبلت الشهادة على الألف لاتفاقهما على وجوب الألف، ولا تقبل على القضاء ؛ لأنه شهادة فرد، إلا أن يشهد معه آخر ، فحينئذ تقبلُ على القضاء أيضاً.
فإن قلت : المدعي كذَّبَ شاهده بالقضاء، فينبغي أن لا تقبل، كما إذا شهد بألف وخمسمئة، والمدعي يدعي ألفاً.
قلت : لم يكذبه فيما شهد له، وإنما كذَّبه فيما شهد عليه، وذلك لا يقدح، كما إذا شهدا لمدع بحق ثم شهدا عليه بحق لإنسان آخر، فإنَّ شهادتهما له لا تبطل، بخلاف ما استشهد به ؛ لأنَّ التكذيب فيه فيما شهد له فيكون قادحاً.
وعن أبي يوسف ، في تلك الصورة يقضي بخمسمئة ؛ لأنّ شاهد القضاء مضمون شهادته أن لا دين إلا خمسمئة ، فيكون هو المتفق عليه معنى، فكان ينبغي أن يكون قول محمد الله كذلك ؛ لأنَّ مذهبه في اعتبارِ المعنى كمذهب أبي يوسف له لكنه خالفه، والله أعلم ؛ لأنه لم يشهد بخمسمئة ابتداءً ، بل اتَّفق الشاهدان على وجوب الألف عليه للمدعي ، ثم انفرد الآخر بالقضاء، فلا يسمع.
And if two testify concerning a thousand, or concerning a loan of a thousand, and one of them added 'he paid such-and-such', it is accepted regarding the thousand and the loan of a thousand, and his statement about the payment is rejected because the testimony of an individual is not accepted, unless another testifies with him. And one who knows of it should not testify until the claimant admits before the people what he received. That is, it is obligatory according to the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). Abu Hanifa's distinction is more precise and stronger in insight, as is not hidden from one who looks with precision. [2] His statement: 'And if they testified... etc.' means: if they testify concerning a thousand, or they testify concerning a loan of a thousand, and one of them says: he paid from the thousand five hundred for example, the testimony on the thousand is accepted due to their agreement on the obligation of the thousand, and it is not accepted on the payment because it is the testimony of an individual, unless another testifies with him, then it is accepted on the payment as well. If you say: the claimant denied his witness concerning the payment, so it should not be accepted, just as if he testified for a thousand and five hundred and the claimant claims a thousand. I say: he did not deny him concerning what he testified for him, but denied him concerning what he testified against him, and that does not harm, just as if two testify for a claimant concerning a right, then they testify against him concerning a right for another person, then their testimony for him is not invalidated, unlike the analogy cited; because the denial there is concerning what he testified for him, so it is harmful. And from Abu Yusuf, in that scenario, he judges with five hundred because the witness to the payment implies in his testimony that there is no debt except five hundred, so that becomes the agreed amount in meaning. So Muhammad's statement should be similar because his doctrine in considering meaning is like Abu Yusuf's, but he differed, and Allah knows best; because he did not testify initially for five hundred, but the two witnesses agreed on the obligation of the thousand to the claimant, then the other departed concerning the payment, so it is not heard.
Source pages p. 591

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof concerning testimony where one witness adds that part of the debt was paid. The passage type is a ruling with proof and includes an intra-Hanafi dispute. The author (the commentator) explains that when two witnesses testify about a debt of a thousand dirhams or a loan of a thousand, and one of them adds that the defendant paid part (e.g., five hundred), the testimony on the thousand is accepted because both agree on the obligation of that amount. However, the additional statement about payment is rejected because it is the testimony of a single witness, and the testimony of one individual is not accepted in financial matters. It would only be accepted if another witness joins him. The commentator then addresses an objection: the claimant denies the payment claimed by the witness, so why isn't the whole testimony rejected? The answer: the denial is about what the witness testifies against the claimant (i.e., that he received payment), not about what he testifies for him (the debt). This is analogous to a case where two witnesses testify for a claimant regarding a right, then later testify against him regarding a different right; their first testimony remains valid. The commentary then mentions a view from Abu Yusuf: in this scenario, the judge should rule for payment of five hundred, because the witness who mentions payment implies that only five hundred remains due. Therefore, the meaning agreed upon is five hundred. Imam Muhammad disagreed because the witnesses initially agreed on the thousand and then one added the payment, so the payment is not part of the original testimony. This shows that Abu Yusuf gives weight to the implied meaning, while Muhammad adheres to the literal agreement.

Key Terms
شهادة الفرد (shahādat al-fard): Testimony of a single witness, which is not accepted in financial matters unless corroborated by another witness.
قضاء (qaḍā’): Payment made by the debtor, which is claimed in the additional testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is accepted because the witness is credible.
B) It is rejected because it is testimony of a single individual.
C) It is accepted only if the claimant confirms it.
D) It is rejected entirely, and the testimony on the 1000 is also rejected.

Key Takeaway
When one witness adds an uncorroborated detail about payment, the main testimony stands, but the detail is ignored.
