Intra-Hanafi Dispute: The Requirement of Specific Lineage in Testimony
Explaining the condition of specific lineage in testimony and judicial letters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the different positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Abu Yusuf on the requirement of mentioning the grandfather (or equivalent) in testimony, and explain the practical consequence of each position.

Core Idea
Identification in testimony requires specific lineage (grandfather or subtribe) per Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf does not require it.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, the validity of testimony depends on proper identification of the person. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies that while witnesses need not know the individual personally, the plaintiff must bring witnesses who can confirm the identity by lineage or other specific attributes. The key dispute among the early Hanafi masters is whether mentioning the grandfather (or its equivalent) is a condition for acceptance—Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes, Abu Yusuf says no. The same rule applies to judicial letters (kitab hukmi).

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational principle
The plaintiff must prove that the person before the court is the one named. The witnesses' testimony establishes a lineage, but the present person might not be the one, so additional witnesses are needed to confirm identity.
This is not about the witnesses knowing the person, but about proving that the lineage fits the present individual.
2
State the preferred Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if the grandfather is not mentioned, the lineage must be specified by mentioning the small street (sikka) or the subtribe (fakhdh) to ensure unique identification.
For Arabs, the subtribe (fakhdh) is the appropriate level; for non-Arabs, trade or occupation may substitute.
3
Contrast with Abu Yusuf's view
Abu Yusuf holds that mentioning the grandfather is not a condition; testimony without it is still accepted. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
Abu Yusuf's view is noted as a valid difference but the majority position is relied upon.
4
Apply the same principle to judicial letters
When a judge receives a letter naming a party, the same identification requirement applies: the plaintiff must bring two witnesses to confirm that the present individual is the one described in the letter.
This ensures continuity of identity across jurisdictions.
5
Understand the lineage hierarchy
The commentary explains the Arab lineage levels (sha'b, qabilah, 'imarah, batn, fakhdh, fasilah). The subtribe (fakhdh) is the most specific level for identification.
This lexical background supports why specific attribution is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Testimony and Retraction. The disagreement in testimony. It is said to him: bring two witnesses that she is so-and-so, and likewise the judicial letter: it is said to him: bring two witnesses that she is so-and-so. Know that the purpose of this issue is that it is not required that the subsidiary (the person testified about) be known, but rather the plaintiff is told: bring two witnesses to testify that the one you have brought is the person testified about. The purpose is not that if they testify about so-and-so daughter of so-and-so al-Mudari, the lineage is complete and the testimony is accepted; because if the grandfather is not mentioned, then it is necessary to attribute her to the small street or to the subtribe (i.e., the specific clan) so that the lineage is complete, and the testimony is accepted according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf, for mentioning the grandfather is not a condition with him, so what takes its place of mentioning the street or subtribe is not required. (And likewise the judicial letter): i.e., if a judge's letter comes to a judge, and the witnesses do not know the person testified about, it is said to the plaintiff: bring two witnesses that this is the person testified about. [3] And the meaning of denial is denial before judgment, for if he denied after judgment, it does not invalidate; for the meaning of invalidation of testimony is non-acceptance, and the judgment that occurred before the denial is not invalidated, as they have clearly stated. [1] His statement: 'It is said to him... etc.' because identification by lineage is established by their testimony, and the plaintiff claims that that lineage belongs to the present woman, but it is possible that it belongs to another, so it is necessary to prove that lineage for the present woman. An analogous case: if they testify about a sale of a bounded property, mentioning its boundaries without knowing its exact location, and they testify against the defendant, then it is necessary for two others to testify that the bounded property with those boundaries is in the possession of the defendant, so that judgment can be passed accordingly. Similarly, if the defendant says that the boundaries mentioned in the testimony are the boundaries of what is in his possession, then it is necessary for two other witnesses to testify that the boundaries mentioned in the testimony are the boundaries of what is in his possession. [2] His statement: 'so-and-so daughter of so-and-so'; he mentioned them as indefinite based on what is in 'al-Misbah': 'fulan and fulanah' without alif and lam are a metaphor for humans, and with them a metaphor for animals; it is said: I rode the fulan, I brought the fulanah. End. [3] His statement: 'i.e., if it comes... etc.' means that the judge wrote in his letter: 'Two just witnesses testified that so-and-so son of so-and-so al-fulani has a claim against so-and-so daughter of so-and-so al-fulaniyah of such-and-such, so you judge against them accordingly.' So the plaintiff brought a woman to the council of the addressee judge, and handed the letter to him, and she denied that she was so-and-so. The judge says: bring two witnesses to testify that this woman you have brought is the one mentioned in this letter, so that reference can be made to her in the judgment. [1] His statement: 'to her subtribe'; he said in 'al-Misbah al-Munir': 'al-fakhdh' (with kasra and with sukun for lightening) is below the tribe and above the clan. Some say: below the clan and above the sub-clan, and it is masculine because it means group, and 'al-fakhidh' (with kasra and sukun for lightening) from the body parts is feminine, and the plural is afkhadh. End. With minimal abridgment. And it is explained in 'al-Hidayah' as the specific tribe, and in 'al-Tabyin' and 'al-Durar' as the highest grandfather. And al-Zamakhshari mentioned in his commentary that the Arabs have six layers: sha'b, qabilah, 'imarah, batn, fakhdh, and fasilah. Thus Mudar is a sha'b, likewise Rabi'ah, Himyar, and Madhij; it is named because tribes branch from it. Kinanah is a qabilah, Quraysh is an 'imarah, Qusayy is a batn, Hashim is a fakhdh, and al-Abbas is a fasilah. [2] His statement: 'because this lineage is general'; its explanation: identification is a condition therein, and that is not achieved by general lineage; rather, lineage to the subtribe is specific because the beginning of lineage is the sha'b, then the qabilah, then the fasilah, then the 'imarah, then the batn, then the fakhdh, so it is the most specific of all. Mentioned in 'al-Nihayah'. (1) Because identification is achieved by mentioning the grandfather and the subtribe, or by a specific lineage; lineage to the city or large quarter is general, and to the small street is specific, and the purpose is notification. See: al-Durr al-Muntaqa (2:214).
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage addresses the requirement for proper identification of the subject of testimony. The author begins by clarifying that the witness need not personally know the individual; rather, the plaintiff must produce two witnesses who can identify that the person brought before the court is indeed the one named. The key issue is the completeness of lineage attribution. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, if the grandfather is not mentioned, one must specify the small street (sikka) or the subtribe (fakhd) to achieve unambiguous identification. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that mentioning the grandfather is not a condition, so even without it the testimony is accepted. This intra-Hanafi difference is important for practical cases. The same principle applies to the judicial letter (kitab hukmi): when a judge's letter arrives naming a party, but the witnesses do not know the person, the plaintiff must bring two witnesses to confirm that the present individual is the one described. The commentary then delves into the definition of 'fakhd', citing lexicographical sources and the hierarchical Arab lineage system (sha'b, qabilah, 'imarah, batn, fakhd, fasilah). The rationale is that identification requires a specific attribution; a broad attribution (e.g., 'al-Mudari') is insufficient because it covers many people. Thus, for Arabs, the subtribe level is necessary, while for non-Arabs, mentioning the trade or occupation suffices since they lost their genealogies.

Key Terms
الشهادة (al-shahādah): Testimony given in court regarding a right or fact.
الكتاب الحكمي (al-kitāb al-ḥukmī): A judicial letter sent by one judge to another, containing a ruling or request to adjudicate based on evidence provided.
الفخذ (al-fakhdh): A subtribe; a subdivision of the Arab lineage hierarchy, more specific than qabilah and batn.
النسبة (al-nisbah): Attribution or lineage; how one is identified by ancestry or location.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The testimony is invalid.
B) The lineage must specify the small street or subtribe.
C) The plaintiff must bring three witnesses.
D) The witness must know the person personally.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi testimony, proper identification requires either mentioning the grandfather or an equivalent specific attribution (street/subtribe); Abu Yusuf's contrary view is a valid but minority position.

Liability of Original and Branch Witnesses Upon Retraction
Dispute among Hanafi imams on liability after retraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on witness liability after retraction, and apply the principle of direct vs. indirect causation to similar cases.

Core Idea
Direct causation trumps indirect: the branch witness who testifies in court is primarily liable.

This passage addresses a key dispute among the Hanafi imams: when branch witnesses retract after judgment, who compensates the plaintiff? The answer depends on whether liability follows the direct cause (the branch's testimony in court) or the indirect cause (the original's delegation). The analysis reveals a broader legal principle: the direct agent (mubashir) bears liability over the indirect cause (musabbib).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two scenarios: (1) branch witnesses testify, judgment is passed, then they retract; (2) original witnesses deny having delegated the branch after judgment. Note that before judgment, retraction or denial stops the process entirely.
Focus on the post-judgment scenarios where liability is disputed.
2
State the positions of the imams
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that only the branch witnesses are liable. Muhammad holds that the plaintiff may choose to hold either the original or the branch liable. Understand the reasoning behind each.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not between schools.
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the judgment was based on the branch testimony in court (direct causation). Muhammad argues that the branches act as agents for the origins, so the origins' testimony is the real basis.
The key distinction is whether the judge's ruling is attributed to the branch's direct testimony or the original's indirect delegation.
4
Apply the mubashir vs. musabbib principle
The commentary clarifies that when direct and indirect causes meet, liability falls on the direct cause. This is a general Hanafi principle applicable beyond testimony law.
This principle helps resolve the dispute: the branch is the direct cause in court, so the two shaykhs' view aligns with the principle.
5
Evaluate the later tarjih
Some later authorities preferred Muhammad's view (al-Multaqa), while others upheld the two shaykhs' view (al-Tanwir, al-Durr al-Muntaqa). Know the practical consequence: the plaintiff's recourse depends on which view is followed.
There is no single mufta bihi; both opinions are transmitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[1] قوله: لأن القضاء...الخ؛ حاصله: إن القضاء وقع بشهادة الفروع، فاختص بهم الضمان. وتفصيله: إن الإتلاف قد حصل بالشهادة التي وجدت في مجلس القاضي، وهي من الفروع مباشرة من كل وجه، والأصول مسببون للتلف من وجه، وقد تقرر في موضعه أن المباشر والمسبب إذا اجتمعا في محل وهما متعديان، فإن الضمان على المباشر دون المسبب. [2] قوله: إن شاء...الخ؛ لأن القضاء وقع بشهادة الفروع من حيث أن الفروع نائبون عنهم، ونقلوا شهادتهم بأمرهم، فيتخير في تضمين أي الفريقين شاء. (1) رجح في «الملتقى» (ص 137) قول محمد، وجزم في «التنوير» (ص155) بقولهما، ونصره الحصكفي في «الدر المنتقى» (2: 220).
Chapter of Testimony and Retraction from It: Disagreement in Testimony. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf because the judgment occurred by the testimony of the branch in a close cause, so the ruling is attributed to him. And according to Muhammad, if he wishes, the original is made liable, and if he wishes, the branch is made liable. Upon him, and it is the witnessing of their testimony, and the cause is a remaining testimony, so the judgment is not nullified because the mentioned denial is a report that may be true or false, so the two reports contradict. So just as if the original witness testified himself, and the judge ruled by his testimony, then he retracted, the judgment is not nullified by the retraction, so here likewise. Contrary to the case if the original witnesses deny the witnessing before judgment by the branch testimony, the judge does not rule by the branch testimony after that, just as if the witnesses retract before judgment where the judge does not rule by that. As for the second case, it is the statement of the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and Muhammad said: They are liable because the branches stood in place of the origins in transmitting their testimony to the court session, and judgment is obtained by the testimony of the origins, therefore their integrity is considered, so it is as if they attended themselves and testified then retracted, and in that liability is incumbent upon them, so here likewise. And for them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): The judgment occurred by the testimony of the branches because the judge rules by what he sees of the proof, and that is the testimony of the branches, and the testimony of the origins is not in the court session, so it is not a proof. [1] His statement: 'because the judgment...' etc.; its summary: The judgment occurred by the testimony of the branches, so liability is specific to them. And its detail: The destruction occurred by the testimony that took place in the judge's court, and it is from the branches directly from every aspect, and the origins are causes of the destruction from one aspect, and it has been established in its place that when the direct agent and the cause meet in a matter and both are transgressors, then liability is on the direct agent, not the cause. [2] His statement: 'if he wishes...' etc.; because the judgment occurred by the testimony of the branches in that the branches are deputies for them, and they transmitted their testimony by their command, so he has choice in making liable whichever of the two groups he wishes. (1) The author of al-Multaqa preferred the statement of Muhammad, and the author of al-Tanwir was decisive with their statement, and al-Haskafi supported it in al-Durr al-Muntaqa (2:220).
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage is an intra-madhhab dispute among the Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani) concerning the liability of original and branch witnesses when the branch retracts after judgment or the original denies delegation. The discussion has two scenarios. First, if the branch testifies, judgment is given, and then the branch retracts, the judgment remains valid according to all. However, if after retraction the branch testifies again, or if the original denies having delegated the branch after judgment, the liability for damages differs: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that only the branch is liable because the direct cause of the judgment was the branch testimony in court, while the originals are mere indirect causes. Muhammad gives the plaintiff the option to seek compensation from either the original or the branch, because the branches acted as agents of the origins. Second, if the original denies delegation before judgment, the branch testimony is not accepted at all. The commentary clarifies that the ruling is based on the principle that direct causation takes priority over indirect causation (mubashir vs. musabbib). The preferred view later evolved: some later authorities preferred Muhammad's opinion, others upheld the two shaykhs' view.

Key Terms
الفرع (al-far‘): The branch witness who testifies based on the delegation of the original witness.
الأصل (al-asl): The original witness who delegates the branch to testify on his behalf.
الإشهاد (al-ishhad): The act of making witnesses (the origins) attest to their testimony before delegation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The original witness only
B) The branch witness only
C) Both at the plaintiff's option
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the direct cause of a judgment bears primary liability, not the indirect cause, so the branch witness is liable unless the original's involvement is equally direct.

Branch's Claim of Original's Lie Is Disregarded
Ruling that a branch witness's accusation against the original is disregarded

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a branch witness accuses the original witness of lying or error, and explain the rationale based on the branch's lack of retraction.

Core Idea
A branch's accusation against the original does not affect the judgment because the branch has not retracted his own testimony.

In the Hanafi law of testimony, a branch witness (al-far‘) transmits the testimony of an original witness (al-asl). But what happens if the branch later claims that the original lied or made a mistake? Does that undo the judgment? This lesson explains that such a statement is inherently unreliable and legally irrelevant—the branch has not retracted his own testimony, so the judgment stands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize the two roles: the original witness (al-asl) who first testifies, and the branch witness (al-far‘) who transmits that testimony in court. Understand that the branch is not an independent witness but a conveyor.
The branch's testimony is derivative; its validity depends on the original's testimony being admissible.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is that the branch's statement 'my original lied or erred' is 'nothing' (laysa bi shay')—it has no legal effect. The judge must disregard it entirely.
This applies after a judgment has been passed based on the branch's testimony.
3
Analyze the first reason
The original's dishonesty cannot be proven by the branch's mere assertion. The branch would need independent evidence (bayyinah) to establish the original's lie. Without it, the claim is unsubstantiated.
In Hanafi law, accusations of lying against a witness require proof; mere allegation is insufficient.
4
Analyze the second reason
The branch has not retracted his own testimony (lam yarji‘ ‘an shahadatihi). Since he still affirms what he said in court, his statement about the original does not conflict with his own testimony. The judgment rests on the branch's testimony, which remains intact.
If the branch had retracted his testimony, different rules would apply (e.g., liability for damages if the judgment was executed).
5
Apply the rule
In practice, if a branch witness after judgment says, 'I now realize the original was mistaken,' the judge should ignore it. The judgment is final unless the branch formally retracts his own testimony and the case is reopened on other grounds.
Retraction of testimony after judgment does not automatically nullify the judgment but may create liability for the witness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول الفرع : كذب أصلي أو غلط فيها ليس بشيء)؛ لأنَّ كذبَ الأصل لا يثبت بقول الفرع، والفرعُ لم يرجع عن شهادته فلا يلتفت إلى قوله. [1] قوله : وقول الفرع ... الخ ؛ أي إذا قال الفرع بعد الحكم بشهادته : إن أصلي قد كذب أو غلط في هذه الشهادة لم يلتفت إلى ذلك القول.
And the statement of the branch: 'My original lied or erred in it' is nothing, because the lie of the original is not established by the statement of the branch, and the branch did not retract his testimony, so his statement is not heeded. [1] His statement: 'And the statement of the branch...' etc., meaning: If the branch, after judgment based on his testimony, says: 'My original lied or erred in this testimony,' that statement is not heeded.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage presents a clear ruling: a branch witness's accusation that the original witness lied or made a mistake in the testimony is legally irrelevant. The reasoning is twofold: first, the claim of the original's dishonesty cannot be proven by the branch's mere word (it would require independent evidence). Second, the branch himself is not retracting his own testimony—he still affirms what he said in court. Since his testimony was the basis of the judgment, and he does not withdraw it, his subsequent comment about the original does not affect the ruling. The commentary explains that this applies after judgment has been rendered based on the branch's testimony. The ruling is straightforward: the judge ignores such a statement.

Key Terms
الفرع (al-far‘): The branch witness who transmits the original's testimony.
الرجوع (al-ruju‘): Retraction from testimony, which if done by the branch would have legal consequences.
الأصل (al-asl): The original witness whose testimony is transmitted by the branch.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A branch witness's accusation that the original lied or erred is legally disregarded because it does not constitute a retraction of the branch's own testimony and the original's dishonesty cannot be established by mere allegation.

Liability of the Muzaqqi (Voucher) Upon Retraction
Dispute on whether a voucher who retracts his certification is liable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on muzaqqi liability and apply the distinction between deliberate falsehood and mistake to determine liability.

Core Idea
A muzaqqi is liable upon retraction only if he knew the witnesses were unfit; honest mistake absolves him.

The muzaqqi plays a pivotal role in court: his certification (tazkiyah) of witnesses is essential for the acceptance of testimony. But what if he later withdraws that certification? This lesson explores the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples on whether retraction triggers liability, and the critical distinctions based on the voucher's knowledge and intent.

Guided Path
1
State the positions
Identify Abu Hanifa's view (liable) and the Sahibayn's view (not liable) regarding the muzaqqi's retraction of tazkiyah.
2
Analyze the proofs
Examine Abu Hanifa's 'illat al-'illah reasoning (tazkiyah is the cause of the cause of loss) and the Sahibayn's 'mere praise' argument.
The commentator uses a metaphor: transmission and witnessing as branches and roots.
3
Identify the qualification
The text narrows the dispute: both sides agree on liability if the muzaqqi admits intentional falsehood or knowledge of unfitness; the disagreement is only when he claims a mistake.
4
Distinguish subtypes
Note the nuance: if the muzaqqi certified the witnesses as free, he is liable if they turn out to be slaves; but if he certified them as upright ('udul), no liability even if they were slaves, because a slave can be upright.
This shows the importance of the specific attribute certified.
5
Determine the thamarah
The practical consequence: in a real case, the court will examine the muzaqqi's statement to decide liability; an admission of error removes liability, while a claim of knowledge or intent imposes it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضَمِنَ المزكي بالرجوع) : عن التزكية هذا عند أبي حنيفة له خلافاً لهما؛ لأنَّ التزكية جعلت الشهادة شهادة. والسر فيه أنَّ التلف هاهنا ثبت بالنقل والإشهاد، فالنقل من الفروع، والإشهاد من الأصول، فلولا إشهاد الأصول، لما تمكن الفروع، ولولا نقل الفروع لما تمكن الأصول، فكان على كل الأصول والفروع في حق المشهود عليه سبب ضمان على سبيل المباشرة. [2] قوله : وضَمِنَ المزكي...الخ ؛ يعني إذا رجع المزكِّي عن تزكية الشاهد ضَمِنَ بالرجوع عند الإمام؛ لأن القاضي لا يعملُ إلا بها، فصارت في معنى علة العلة وقالا : لا يضمن؛ لأنه أثنى على الشهود، ثم رجع عن ثنائه، ولا ضمان به، والخلاف فيما إذا قال : تعمدت أو علمتُ أنَّ الشهود عبيد، ومع ذلك زكيتُهم. أما إذا قال المزكي : اخطأت فيها فلا ضمان فيها. صرح به في «البحر»، وغيره، وقيل : الخلاف فيما إذا أخبر المزكي بالحرية، بأنه قال : إنهم أحرار، أما إذا قال : هم عدول فبانوا عبيداً لا يضمن؛ لأنَّ العبد قد يكون عدلاً. And the muzaqqi (voucher) is liable upon retraction: concerning tazkiyah (certification), this is according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because the tazkiyah made the testimony a testimony. The secret therein is that the destruction here is established by transmission (naql) and witnessing (ishhad): the transmission from the branches, and the witnessing from the origins. If not for the witnessing of the origins, the branches would not be able, and if not for the transmission of the branches, the origins would not be able. So each of the origins and branches, with respect to the one testified against, is a cause of liability in the manner of direct causation. [2] His statement: 'And the muzaqqi is liable upon retraction...' etc., meaning: If the muzaqqi retracts his tazkiyah of the witness, he is liable upon retraction according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), because the judge only acts by it, so it becomes like a cause of the cause. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is not liable, because he praised the witnesses, then retracted his praise, and there is no liability for that. The disagreement is when he says: 'I intentionally did it' or 'I knew that the witnesses were slaves, yet I certified them.' If the muzaqqi says: 'I erred in it,' then there is no liability. This is stated in al-Bahr and others. And it is said: The disagreement is when the muzaqqi informed of freedom, saying they were free. If he says: 'They are upright,' but they turn out to be slaves, he is not liable because a slave may be upright. [3] His statement: 'Because tazkiyah...' etc.; its summary: Testimony does not necessitate anything without tazkiyah, and the cause of destruction is the testimony. So tazkiyah becomes the cause of the cause, and it is like the cause in attributing the ruling to it.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage addresses the liability of a muzaqqi (someone who vouches for the integrity of witnesses) if he retracts his certification. There is an intra-madhhab dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that the muzaqqi must compensate for damages if he retracts, because his certification is the reason the judge accepted the testimony—the tazkiyah is the 'cause of the cause' ('illat al-'illah) of the loss. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree, arguing that tazkiyah is merely praise, and retracting praise does not incur liability. The commentary qualifies the dispute: if the muzaqqi admits deliberate falsehood or knowledge that the witnesses were unfit (e.g., slaves), he is liable; if he claims an honest mistake, he is not liable. There is also a nuance: if he certified the witnesses as free and they turn out to be slaves, he is liable; but if he certified them as upright ('udul) and they were slaves, he is not liable because a slave can be upright. The underlying principle, as explained by the commentator, is that tazkiyah is essential for the testimony to be effective; without it, the testimony would not cause the loss, thus the voucher is a direct cause in the legal sense.

Key Terms
المزكي (al-muzakkī): The person who certifies the integrity ('adalah) of witnesses before the court.
التزكية (al-tazkiyah): The act of vouching for the witnesses' upright character.
علة العلة ('illat al-'illah): The cause of the cause; a secondary cause that is legally treated as a direct cause for liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, in which case is the muzaqqi NOT liable despite retracting his certification?
A) When he says 'I intentionally falsified the certification.'
B) When he says 'I knew the witnesses were slaves.'
C) When he says 'I erred in my certification.'
D) When he says 'They are upright' but they were slaves.

Key Takeaway
A muzaqqi is only liable for retraction if he acted with knowledge or intent; honest mistake protects him from liability.
